(The market cap weight of small caps is much less than  $50\%$ .) [2]

Partly influenced by FF analyses, style investing has become big business in the past 20 years. Institutional investment mandates are often given to value or growth managers (perhaps further segmented into large-cap or small-cap buckets). In FF terminology, value/growth is a one-dimensional concept based on a valuation ratio or blend of valuation ratios. An alternative approach evaluates stocks using a value metric based on valuation ratios, while a distinct growth dimension is based on past multi-year growth or profitability measures (so that, in this configuration, "growth" is not the opposite of "value"). Interestingly, so-called growth managers in the U.S. equity space appear to actually be better characterized as momentum players than growth investors. Their positions across stocks tend to have positive correlations with a momentum style portfolio and negative correlations with a firm's long-term expected growth rate (Ghayur et al., 2010, p. 159).

The magnitude of the long-run value premium is comparable with the long-run equity premium (over cash). Looking at U.S. data from 1926 to 2009, the compound annual value premium is  $4.1\%$  (GM), compared with the  $5.7\%$  equity premium. Thanks to its lower volatility, the value premium has the same Sharpe ratio (0.39) as the equity premium. Figure 12.1 shows cumulative profits for the U.S. VMG series since 1926 and a non-U.S. value growth composite series since 1975.

The trend line in the value premium looks quite stable over decades, with little or no sign of decay. The non-U.S. value premium has been similarly impressive since 1975 and its long-run Sharpe ratio is even higher due to better diversification across many countries and the use of four different valuation ratios to construct it. Over 1975-2009, the non-US value composite has had an SR of 0.78. In other chapters, I combine the U.S. multi-value-indicator composite (SR 0.44 since 1975) with the non-U.S. variant, using equal weights for U.S. and non-U.S. markets; the resulting global composite (SR 0.68) is my proxy for value-based global stock selection.

Figure 12.2 shows that in the U.S. the rolling 60-month Sharpe ratio has been positive through most of available history, with the main exceptions being in 1932 and 1999. The non-U.S. version has been even more robust.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/fd62d865fdd360fcd7465fc823fe8141812c52fbb657160988cd80d1a6fb9109.jpg)

Figure 12.1. Value-vs.-growth cumulative outperformance since 1926 (U.S.)/1975 (non-U.S.). Sources: Ken French's website, Bloomberg.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/31a81c0e14722e9c457b6163ea28487769480d436fd54f68b5bc3382c4f1550f.jpg)

Figure 12.2. Sixty-month rolling Sharpe ratios of value growth long-short portfolios. Sources: Ken French's website, Bloomberg.

The value premium has been especially consistent after the Great Depression. Indeed, the reputation of value stocks as being riskier than growth stocks may date back to 1930s' experience:

- In the 1930s, value stocks had higher equity market betas than growth stocks, making the beta of the VMG factor clearly positive in that decade. After the 1950s the beta sign changed. Between 1980 and 2005 the VMG factor's beta was clearly negative.
- The VMG long-short portfolio has experienced drawdowns exceeding  $30\%$  four times in history. Three of these occurred during the volatile 1930s and the fourth one in 1999-2000.
- The value strategy did not fare particularly poorly during the crashes of October 1929, 1987, and 2008  $(+9\%, +4\%, -3\%)$ . If we consider the biggest equity market drawdowns since 1926 and VMG's coincident performance, it is again clear that VMG's losing periods coincided with broad market losses only in the 1930s and in its echo, the liquidity crisis of 2008 (see Figure 12.3).

# More Fama-French analysis

In a sequence of influential papers since their 1992 hit, Fama and French (FF) drilled deeper on different aspects of the value premium and other regularities. Here are some relevant nuggets from their more recent papers.

Value stocks have outperformed growth stocks reasonably consistently since 1926 both before and after 1963 (the starting year for many empirical studies because Compustat data files become available then). Between 1926 and 1963 the CAPM can explain value outperformance: value stocks have higher equity market betas than growth stocks, and VMG has a significantly positive beta of 0.35. Between 1963 and 2004 value stocks have lower betas than growth stocks, and VMG has a significantly negative market beta of  $-0.28$ . This sign change in value stocks' beta may be behind the empirical finding that high-beta stocks outperformed low-beta stocks before 1963 but not thereafter.

A stock's price-to-book (P/B) ratio reflects expected returns but also expected cash flows; thus it can be a noisy measure of both. The standard valuation equation that relates market prices to expected cash flows and discount rates suggests that, given  $\mathrm{P} / \mathrm{B}$ 's two roles, other control variables can help separate the expected cash flow component from the expected return component. Many documented new anomaly variables (indicators that are able to predict cross-sectional differences in average stock returns beyond the FF three-factor model of beta, value, and size), such as net share issuance, asset growth, accruals, profitability, and momentum, may proxy for expected cash flows. Controlling for  $\mathrm{P} / \mathrm{B}$ , the standard valuation equation suggests that higher expected net cash flows imply higher expected stock returns (whether for rational or irrational reasons).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/8ec1e4f2554d118a56de0a7c0515139629fcc62f94435386c5d6efca9ccc063c.jpg)

Figure 12.3. Value performance during major equity bear markets. Sources: Ken French's website.

FF dissect the value premium since the 1920s by breaking down value growth outperformance into dividend yield advantage and three sources of capital gains: (1) relative growth in book equity (mainly earnings retention); (2) convergence in valuation ratios due to mean-reverting profitability and expected returns; and (3) upward drift in valuation ratios between 1927 and 2006. The main empirical findings are

- Dividends contribute half of large-cap value growth outperformance over the full sample (1.3 of  $2.6\%$ ) but only a tiny fraction of small-cap value growth outperformance (0.2 of  $5.8\%$ ).
- The main source of value stocks' return advantage is convergence in valuation ratios, although its impact is partly offset by growth stocks' faster book growth. Value stocks earn large capital gains over time as some of them become more profitable and their valuations rise. Growth stocks earn high returns from fast book equity growth but lose this advantage due to dwindling profitability in many growth firms and shrinking valuations over time. Companies migrate across style groups because of the invest ments they make and the expected profitability of these investments, which together make their P/B ratios converge.
- The drift term boosts the average returns of all portfolios by about  $1\%$  so it has little impact on relative returns.

Migration dynamics across value-sorted and size-sorted portfolios can be analyzed from another perspective. The value premium has three sources: (1) high returns on value stocks that migrate toward growth; (2) low returns on growth stocks that migrate toward value; and (3) slightly higher returns for non-migrating value than non-migrating growth stocks. Again, converging profitability and converging P/B ratios across stocks are empirically the main driver of the value premium.

A proximate explanation for convergence in valuation ratios is that when firms are sorted into value and growth portfolios based on their P/B ratios they tend to be at opposite ends of the profitability spectrum. Growth stocks tend to be profitable and investors expect them to keep growing rapidly while value stocks are less profitable and expected to remain so. Market expectations for slower growth, perhaps together with higher required returns, explain value stocks' lower P/B ratios. However, extremely high or low profitability is not very persistent. Competition tends to erode growth firms' profitability advantage (reinforced by exercised growth options). Consequently, the P/B ratios of growth stocks tend to fall in the year(s) after portfolio formation while those of value stocks tend to rise.

As always, there are two interpretations for these results. The behavioral story is that market expectations contain systematic forecast errors (overestimating the persistence of growth, with repeated surprises). The rational story is that predictable changes in growth and profitability are properly incorporated into market assessments of expected returns and risks, but that value portfolios are riskier and that market pricing reflects this. Return decompositions such as those presented above are agnostic on the rationality question. Interpretation depends on prior views about market rationality and cannot be settled by data alone.

# Designing value strategies

Investors who believe that value stocks outperform in the long run face several open questions when designing their strategy. The questions, applicable to all active strategies, include the following:

- Is the stock selection process fully systematic or fully discretionary (where a discretionary investor only uses value indicators as inputs for judgmental decisions)—or something in between? I focus here on the systematic approach.
- Will a long-short portfolio, a long-only portfolio, or something in between (say, 130/30) be used? I focus here on a long-short portfolio (which in many cases can be overlaid as an active overweight and underweight portfolio on top of a benchmark equity index).
- Which value indicators are used? Candidate valuation ratios include earnings/price, book/price, dividend/price, cash flow/price, sales/price, and others. There are further choices regarding the numerator such as whether to use forward-looking or trailing earnings, which historical window, and which specific earnings measure? What all these methods have in common is that some measure of the firm's intrinsic value is compared with its market price. However, even the denominator may be challenged: some observers advocate replacing the equity market value with the enterprise value (which includes firm's debt and preferred shares besides its equity). It is common to combine several value indicators into a composite measure, and there are many ways to do this.
- How frequently is the portfolio rebalanced? Value signals are inherently slow moving but turnover can be reduced further by smoothing value indicators. Managers need to balance information decay in value signals against the extra trading costs imposed by frequent portfolio revisions.
- How long a publication lag should one use for accounting measures? (The question arises because it is only "fair" to use data that are fully available information to all investors.)
- Questions on the equity universe:

One country or multiple countries? (In an international context, it may be important to take into account differing accounting conventions across countries. It may be better to run parallel value strategies in several countries and/or regions than to run one global value strategy.)

- Should one analyze all stocks together or large-cap and small-cap stocks separately? (The value premium tends to be stronger for small-cap stocks, at least on paper. Liquidity considerations favor trading large-cap stocks, however.)
- Which stocks are excluded? (REITs, ADRs, ETFs, and closed-end funds are typically dropped—as are, on some occasions, financial stocks for which certain value indicators are not available or meaningful.)
- How are value signals translated into portfolio weights? The most common approach involves sorting stocks into groups and then buying a value-weighted or equally weighted combination of all a group's constituents (e.g., top and bottom  $10\%$ ,  $20\%$ , or  $30\%$  of stocks). Another approach involves making active position sizes directly proportional to value signals, perhaps with a cap.
- Should the strategy be as plain as possible, presumably making it less overfitted and more robust, or should dynamic features (such as tailoring value indicators to each industry sector) and other "bells and whistles" be added if they have been historically successful?
- Few quantitative equity managers only use value indicators. For them a key question is how value indicators are combined with other indicators and how value strategies are applied in other asset classes (see Sections 12.5 and 12.6). In particular, value and momentum strategies tend to be negatively correlated and thus are great complements. Other investors prefer valuation models over value indicators because the former explicitly take into account firms' growth prospects and the quality of management.
- How are risk management, liquidity considerations, and trading cost controls incorporated into the process? Should long and short portfolios have simply equal nominal amounts, equal return volatilities, or equal betas? One crucial question is whether persistent industry sector biases should be allowed or whether sector neutrality should be pursued.

Sector neutrality. Practitioner studies highlight the empirical benefits of sector-neutral approaches. Yet, academic studies and many popular investment products (FF and

LSV, MSCI-Barra and S&P value/growth indices, and the RAFI fundamental index) do nothing to impose sector neutrality. Without any such adjustments, persistent industry concentrations are possible in the long-short portfolio. For example, in early 2008, the long (value) portfolio heavily overweighted finance stocks while the short (growth) portfolio overweighted energy stocks. Such sector biases may or may not boost average returns but they pretty clearly impair value portfolio diversification and thus raise its volatility. Indeed, empirical evidence is mixed on whether imposing sector neutrality boosts or reduces long-run returns, but it does seem to boost risk-adjusted returns. Tactically, it may be worthwhile to remove sector neutrality when valuations across industry sectors are highly dispersed.

There are no correct answers to any of these design questions. Both simple-but-smart and complex approaches have their supporters. A broad menu of available options makes it conceivable that value managers will improve on the VMG's historical performance. Given the variety of choices made in practice, it is not surprising that different value managers can hold very dissimilar portfolios and earn very different returns. However, there is a common core that makes value portfolios co-move together—and, as we learned in 2007, especially so at times of turmoil.

# Fundamental index debate

Arnott-Hsu-Moore (2005) proposed fundamental indices as superior alternatives to the market-cap-weighted indices that dominate the passive indexing business. In a fundamental index and its relatives, stocks are weighted by the economic scale of the company rather than by market cap. Its proponents argue that the market cap portfolio has an inherent tendency to overweight overpriced stocks and/or sectors and to underweight underpriced ones, resulting in a "return drag" when pricing errors normalize. Instead of a single measure of a firm's intrinsic value or economic scale, Arnott-Hsu-Moore use multiple metrics. They combine sales, cash flow, book value, and dividends and smooth these metrics to make signals more robust and to reduce turnover. Empirically, a fundamental index has outperformed a market-cap-weighted index of large-cap U.S. equities by about  $2\%$  over 40 years—and more in small caps and in several non-dollar markets.

The public debate that ensued was less about the desirability of the product and more about its marketing. Supporters of market cap indices object to the name "fundamental index" because it is not as passive a strategy as market cap weighting and because it is not an equilibrium strategy that all investors could employ (the growing popularity of this strategy would make fundamental index weights and market cap weights gradually converge and thereby eliminate any return advantage). Other critics challenge the claims that the approach is inherently novel. Fundamental indexing is a value tilt, or systematic value strategy, although early articles downplayed this aspect. Indeed, the approach is very typical value investing—traditional in that no sector neutrality is imposed—except that position sizing is more dynamic and aggressive than in common sorting approaches. Active weights—deviations from market cap weights—are proportional to value indicators such as book-to-market ratios (thus raising position sizes amidst losses). A final strand of criticism debates the theoretical motivation that is claimed for fundamental indexation: whether it is appropriate to assume symmetric pricing errors centered around an unobservable fair value, as fundamental indexers do, rather than around the observable market price.

Fundamental indexing is just one example of market-value-indifferent weighting methods. Equal weighting, equal volatility weighting, minimum variance portfolios, and diversity weighting are other examples that have a contrarian bias (their rebalancing often involves selling assets whose prices have risen and buying assets whose prices have dropped). All appear to empirically outperform market-cap-weighted portfolios in the long run. To the extent that the claim about return drag in market cap weighting is correct, any pricingindifferent equity ranking system should avoid this drag. A part of the advantage-but not all of it-may reflect avoiding drag from buying new index constituents and selling excluded stocks together with the index-tracking crowd.

# 12.3 TWEAKS INCLUDING STYLE TIMING

For each dynamic strategy, I will discuss some successful tweaks in indicator specification as well as contexts and conditions where and when the strategy seems to work especially well or poorly. Such evidence may enhance our understanding of the sources behind the strategy's success. Yet, we should remember that such analysis is even more vulnerable to data mining than is identification of basic regularities.

Indicator specifications can be fine-tuned. Some empirical studies show that trailing earnings work better than forward-looking earnings in earnings/price ratios, or that smoothed multi-year earnings work better than one-year earnings. There are many ideas about how to adjust reported earnings to capture the trend component or to remove nominal bias in current earnings. Qian-Hua-Sorensen (2007) argue that the ratio of cash flows from operations to enterprise value is the best value indicator for predicting next quarter returns. However, such findings are often based on one particular data sample and several auxiliary decisions, so it is hard to judge how robust they are.

A value strategy may work especially well (or poorly) in certain sectors or countries. So-called contextual models identify subsets of the equity universe according to the effectiveness of certain predictors and customize models accordingly. One finding is that stocks with the lowest institutional ownership have the most significant value premium. This subset includes about three-quarters of stocks but less than one-tenth of the market's total capitalization. It is not surprising that value premium (besides many other regularities) is weakest in the large-cap segment, which is best covered by analysts, most actively traded, easiest to arbitrage, and thus least prone to relative mispricings.

Style timing/rotation. As I stress through this book, historical average premia may be misleading when expected returns vary over time. Investors took money away from value strategies during their underperformance in the dot.com boom. They then piled money into value strategies during the long good spell in the early and mid-2000s, perhaps intending to ride through any temporary downturns with equanimity. However, the 2007-2008 value losses resulted in de-allocations and prompted more investors to seek ways to "time" these value strategy downturns. This quest is fraught with overfitting risks and, at best, may only lead to modest improvement over a static value strategy. There are no strong empirical relations but here are some mildly promising paths: momentum;

ex ante value spread;

- seasonals;
- macroeconomic and liquidity indicators.

The first indicator in many firms' style rotation models is momentum (also called persistence or continuation). When value works, it is reasonable to predict further near-term success. The first two rows in Table 12.1 show evidence of value performance persistence, mainly over a one-month horizon.

Second, value spread measures the size of ex ante opportunity in the value strategy. If there is a wide dispersion of valuation ratios across stocks, the value strategy has larger profit opportunities than if this dispersion is narrow. For example, at the early-2000 peak the earnings yields of top-quartile (value) stocks were  $8\%$  higher than those of the bottom-quartile (growth) stocks ( $12\%$  vs.  $4\%$ ). By early 2007 this spread had narrowed to below  $2\%$  ( $6.8\%$  vs.  $5.2\%$ ). With hindsight, both extremes turned out to be good style-timing signals (to buy value in 2000 and growth in 2007).

If a given implementation of a value strategy imposes sector neutrality, then value spread should also be industry adjusted. The numbers above are not industry adjusted. The pioneering Asness et al. (2000) style-timing study measures value spread by the decile-10 vs. decile-1 difference for several industry-adjusted valuation indicators. The study argues that this spread is especially useful for style timing in conjunction with variation in the expected growth gap between growth and value stocks. If value spread is historically wide but cannot be justified by a historically wide growth expectations gap, the ex ante opportunity for value strategies is especially good.

In addition, the last row in Table 12.1 shows mild evidence of multi-year reversal in value style returns.

Figure 12.4 compares realized cumulative outperformance of value stocks with ex ante value spread, here measured by the B/P-based valuation gap between value and growth stocks (data from Giuliano De Rossi at UBS). Russell 3000 stocks are sorted based on their B/P ratios into three groups; and value-weighted returns and median B/P ratios for each tertile are then computed each month.) Figure 12.4(a) shows returns (outperformance of value over growth) and value spreads for the "raw" stock universe, while Figure 12.4(b) shows the same numbers but for the adjusted, sector-neutral stock universe. (In this sample, the sector-neutral strategy has slightly higher average return than the raw strategy, and one-third less volatility, giving it a clear edge in SR—0.68 vs. 0.37—but the results can vary depending on some design choices.)

The graphs suggest that value spreads have some predictive ability. In Figure 12.4(a), value stocks as a group were relatively expensive in 1998 and 2007 (dispersion between

Table 12.1. Correlation of past VMG returns with next month VMG return

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/fab1f6525d0d12eafcb9ebd416d02e865e4ca6cca80e5cfcdeddd0b53bc155f3.jpg) value and growth stock B/P ratios was narrow) before they underperformed sharply in the following year. The correlation between value spread and next month (next year) realized outperformance is 0.29 (0.48) in the raw case and 0.20 (0.43) in the sector-neutral case. Some other specifications of value spread look different, say, making value stocks appear even cheaper in 2000 and more expensive in 2007 than in Figure 12.4. Note that cumulative gains trend higher, unlike value spread, thanks to value stocks' long-run advantages (yield advantage which is nearly always positive and convergence between valuation ratios of value and growth stocks).

Figure 12.4. Value stocks' ex post outperformance and ex ante value spread: (a) raw, (b) sector-neutral.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/8ceb4acb23743cd7395839f5234a631215caa017e5041c1648ac219f454fa681.jpg)

Source: Giuliano De Rossi. (UBS).

Still, style timing is not easy, especially when investors face the lingering question as to whether a poorly performing strategy is merely temporarily out of favor or permanently damaged. The history of quant-based equity selection strategies is instructive. After several excellent years following 2000, many quantitative equity strategies suffered unprecedented synchronous losses in August 2007, despite having been uncorrelated in the past. The diagnosis was that quantitative strategies—especially those following a value tilt—had become overcrowded and subject to systematic de-leveraging risk among quant traders. There was less agreement on the appropriate response—whether to stick with proven approaches, whether to refine and "time" them, or whether to replace them with more proprietary quant approaches (less crowded but less proven). Value strategies continued to suffer in 2008, and when the value style recovered in 2009 few quant managers benefited fully. By that time, many had re-allocated capital toward momentum style strategies, which had their period of record losses in spring 2009.

Third, seasonals. The value premium (VMG) exhibits a January seasonal that is almost as strong as that of the size premium. Between 1926 and 2008, monthly VMG returns averaged  $2.2\%$  in Januaries vs.  $0.24\%$  in other months. Asness-Moskowitz-Pedersen (2009) document a distinct January seasonal for value premia in Japan and Continental Europe, but in the U.K. they find a reverse pattern. (They also find a January seasonal for a value-driven equity country allocation strategy but not for value strategies in other asset classes.) There is no "Hallowe'en effect" seasonal for value—average returns are similar for summer and winter.

Fourth, macroeconomic and liquidity conditions may help explain the value premium (concurrently) and/or predict it in the near term. Contemporaneous relations include positive correlation with equities in the 1930s and 1940s and negative correlation in recent decades. Some studies identify links between short or long rate changes and the value premium. I do not find even consistent signs for a contemporaneous (or predictive) relation but yield curve steepening has a mild positive contemporaneous correlation with quarterly VMG returns. Some authors argue that the value premium is countercyclical. However, when I compute average returns of the VMG across different stages of the business cycle (for 1926-2008), I find almost no cyclical variation. A variety of studies claim that value tends to underperform when (and before) the economy turns south, when corporate profit growth slows, when liquidity conditions dry up, or when credit spreads sharply widen. All are interesting but weak relations.

# 12.4 THE REASONS VALUE WORKS

I have already referred to two influential publications—Fama-French (1992, FF) and Lakonishok-Shleifer-Vishny (1994, LSV). The two studies presented broadly similar empirical results of value stock outperformance but their interpretations were very different. The debate on rational and irrational explanations has been repeated over and over again, and no amount of empirical evidence can resolve it. I discuss both explanations. This should help the reader form his or her own opinions—and perhaps gain some respect for the opposite view. I believe that both rational and irrational factors contribute to the value premium; the real question is which one is more important. My take is that behavioral stories are, so far, more persuasive.

# Irrational and rational explanations for the value premium

LSV list popular errors that may give rise to mispricings that contrarian investors can exploit. In Section 6.3.2, I list these and other explanations for the value premium, so I won't repeat them here. Here I only focus on the most prominent explanation: extrapolating past earnings growth too far into the future. Figure 12.5 shows that when U.S. stocks are sorted by their P/E ratios, the quintiles are monotonically related to consensus forecasts of long-term earnings growth. Moreover, a similar pattern arises when the aggregate market's P/E ratios and growth forecasts are compared over time during the past quarter-century. In both cases, growth optimism at high P/E levels has proved excessive and subsequent growth rates have disappointed.

It is true that glamour firms have grown faster than value firms and have continued to do so for a couple of years. Yet, growth rates are mean-reverting and the market underappreciates the true rate of mean reversion. Indeed, firms with low past sales growth or with low analyst forecasts of future growth tend to outperform in the future.

Figure 12.5. P/E ratios are linked to long-term growth expectations, both across stocks and over time.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/0721251b922cfad46ae83e1862a1f14f024354d5fa82fe4a423b40c63e4ba5ee.jpg)

Sources: Ghayur et al. (2010), Bloomberg, Citigroup, Credit Suisse.

Glamour firm earnings tend to disappoint in the future while value firm earnings tend to surprise on the upside. On average, glamour stocks earn negative returns on days of future earnings announcements, while value stocks earn positive returns. Such evidence of surprise effects makes it hard to argue that investors had rationally expected the outperformance of value stocks (as a reward for their greater risk compared with growth stocks).

One study sorts firms on their 5-year growth rates and finds that ending valuations (P/B) are clearly higher for high-growth firms but beginning valuations display, if anything, the opposite pattern. Valuations appear to respond to the realized growth rate instead of anticipating it. Another study finds that the market is able to identify firms with a future growth edge but it overprices this advantage by a factor of 2.

The generic rational line is that value stocks outperform because they are inherently riskier. The challenge is to find a meaningful risk concept that covaries with the value premium. Ultimately, finance theory states that any risk factor justifies a high risk premium if its losses covary with bad times. Due to the joint hypothesis problem, the proponents of a rational explanation can always hope that the true risk-based explanation will one day be uncovered.

- Behaviorists counter that there is no evidence that value stocks are riskier than growth stocks. The VMG factor has had a negative equity market beta for the past 50 years and average VMG has been positive in both expansions and recessions. Lakonishok has argued that under any but a metaphysical definition of risk, value stocks' outperformance cannot be attributed to risk exposures. The fact that much of the outperformance occurs on earnings announcement days further hints at disappointed expectations rather than rationally accepted low returns.

Early rational explanations emphasized the evidence of systematic covariation among value stocks and growth stocks. Value stocks covary positively with a common risk factor (VMG). One strand of the literature examined whether the characteristics of a stock's low P/B ratio or its covariance with the VMG factor better explains the cross-section of average stock returns; characteristics usually win such horse races. However, estimated rolling value factor betas also have significant ability to explain the value premium.

- Behaviorists tend to interpret any evidence of systematic covariation as sentiment-driven comovement. Value stocks are not fundamentally similar to each other but their comovement reflects their "out-of-favor" nature (and glamour stocks' comovement reflects their "in-favor" nature).

These are just examples of how both sides of the debate can always present an alternative interpretation to the supportive evidence the other side comes up with.

These rational stories still had little to say on why value stocks are riskier. The first explanation that FF proposed was distress risk. Value firms tend to be unprofitable with low past growth, often in declining industries. Distress risk was an argument based on a stock's standalone characteristics rather than an argument about covariances, which are central to academic theories about risk premium determination. Early empirical evidence gave some support to the distress idea, but recent studies, which use firms' debt ratings or various bankruptcy indicators to directly quantify distress risk, find evidence that is hard to reconcile with a rational risk story. Apparently, distressed firms load positively on the VMG factor but tend to earn exceptionally poor returns (and even poorer in bad times, which are proxied by a rising VIX). Thus, many value investors try to weed out distressed firms from the universe of low-multiple stocks and pick the remaining value stocks. Using various quality, profitability, or momentum filters enhances value portfolio returns.

Recent research suggests that the underperformance of distressed firms may reverse over a longer horizon. Avramov et al. (2010) show that many anomalies—including value and momentum—are concentrated on the subset of stocks with high credit risk (ratings below investment grade, constituting less than  $10\%$  of market cap). Moreover, losses for stocks with negative momentum and high credit risk occur exclusively during periods of deteriorating credit (a one-year window around a credit-rating downgrade), whereas the value strategy is more profitable outside this window. Predictable multi-month losses of distressed (low-rated) firms around rating downgrades look like an underreaction effect that is hard to exploit due to illiquidity and short-selling challenges. Delayed buying of such firms may be a better strategy, because long positions in surviving low-rated firms later earned relatively high subsequent returns, thus contributing to the value effect. Parallels to the performance of fallen angel bonds are very close.

Additional risk factors and time-varying betas have been academics' main rational stories that hold some promise for explaining the value premium. Novel risk factors highlighted in the literature include long-run consumption risk, consumption of luxury goods, labor income, good vs. bad betas, and an investment-in-new-projects factor. I briefly discuss the last two:

- Campbell-Vuolteenaho (2004a) split the equity market beta into two parts, the cash flow beta and the discount rate beta. The discount rate beta is viewed as the "good beta" (bad news is more temporary because shocks that raise required discount rates reduce current prices but also improve future reinvestment opportunities) while the cash flow beta is viewed as the "bad beta" (poor cash flow news has a more permanent price impact). In theory, the bad beta should have a higher discount rate than the good beta. Empirically, value stocks are more exposed to "bad betas" while growth stocks load more heavily on "good betas". This finding could explain why a rational investor may not want to exploit the value premium.
- Chen-Novy-Marx-Zhang (2010) present a three-factor model that can explain the cross-section of stock returns better than the Fama-French three-factor model. One of their novel factors is investment by the firm in new projects. This factor has a 0.51 correlation with VMG. Stocks with a low investment-to-assets ratio tend to have higher future returns, lower past returns, and lower current valuations. Moreover, the low average returns of distressed firms may be explained by such firms having lower returns on assets and a lower sensitivity to risk factors related to returns-on-assets shocks.

Time-varying betas. The key idea is that while value stocks do not always have higher betas than growth stocks, the former are riskier when it matters: in bad times. Some empirical evidence supports this idea but it does not jump out from the data. One study claims that value stocks have higher equity market beta than growth stocks in recessions and when the equity market premium is high (and lower in expansions and when the market premium is low). However, recall my evidence of VMG's losses in the 1930s and 2008 but not in most other recessions.

Finally, Asness-Moskowitz-Pedersen (2009) explore whether the success of value (and momentum) strategies in several asset classes reflects macroeconomic and liquidity risks. They document mild value underperformance when future long-run consumption growth is falling and the current economy is weak as well as when liquidity conditions dry up and credit spreads widen. These empirical relations help justify the value premium by the correlation between value strategy losses and bad times—but they also leave most of the value premium unexplained. The authors speculate that part of the value premium reflects mispricing—cheapness of value assets—which may persist due to trading costs and liquidity risks that limit the activity of arbitrageurs. When liquidity is especially scarce, arbitrageurs need to de-lever, and cheap assets get even cheaper, so value strategies fare poorly (and momentum strategies excel).

# 12.5 DOES THE VALUE STRATEGY WORK IN EQUITIES BEYOND INDIVIDUAL STOCK SELECTION OR IN MARKET OR SECTOR SELECTION IN OTHER ASSET CLASSES?

The short answer is "Yes", although the most popular application is in stock selection. In other asset classes, carry strategies often work better than value strategies, but quant managers often use both inputs, and they are related. Value also works, to some extent, in timing individual asset classes and trading across asset classes. Even discretionary investors in a variety of areas use value anchors as one input to their decision making.

Asness-Moskowitz-Pedersen (2009) present the broadest evidence yet published of outperformance by the value approach: stock selection strategies in four geographic regions as well as country allocation strategies in four asset classes. They choose deliberately naive value indicators to avoid overfitting. Outside the U.S., sample periods start in the 1980s and end in early 2008. For stock selection they use the price/book ratio without imposing sector neutrality. For commodities they use the price 5 years ago as the value anchor, for currencies the 5-year excess return, and for bonds the real yield (10-year yield minus a consensus forecast of 12-month inflation). Commodity and currency indicator choices highlight the similarity between value strategies and long-term return reversal strategies. If no obvious ex ante value indicator is available, multi-year average return is often a reasonable substitute. The consistency of the results is impressive: all strategies have positive Sharpe ratios, ranging between 0.1 and 0.9. Value strategies worked especially well for stock selection in Japan and for equity country allocation.

Other studies document the success of value strategies among emerging equity markets and among corporate bonds. Many fixed income arbitrage strategies employed in hedge funds and bank proprietary trading desks are based on mean-reverting spreads or related value anchors. Statistical arbitrage in equity markets—pairs trading and more complex variants—is also based on relative value (i.e., mean reversion in the pricing relationship between two assets). The profitability of such "arbitrage" (really: relative value trading) strategies waned significantly in the past decade as the technologies to exploit them became widely available. Low-hanging fruit were eliminated years ago. In country allocation, weighting countries by GDP rather than by market cap in a global index is one way to infuse a value bias. This approach is gaining popularity thanks to its historical success.

In this book, I have discussed simple value indicators for timing various asset classes (e.g., earnings yields for the equity market, real yields for duration timing, and credit spreads for corporate bonds). But value indicators also work in global tactical asset allocation across asset classes.

In the long run, value strategies seem to work in every context we turn to, if we find suitable value indicators. However, they certainly do not work over all short time periods that we study:

- I highlighted earlier the underperformance of equity value strategies in 1998-1999 and 2007-2008. I discuss some ideas for style timing in Section 12.3, as well as the inherent difficulty of the task.
- Apparent misvaluations can be surprisingly persistent—and can keep getting worse. In equity markets, bull markets can rise higher and bear markets fall further than value investors can imagine. In currency markets, prominent examples of widening misvaluations include growing dollar strength in 1983–1985, euro weakness in 1999–2001, and yen weakness in 2002–2007.
- In addition, financial crises involving liquidations of levered positions often see cheap assets get cheaper. Autumn 1998 and autumn 2008 are the most extreme examples. Thus, there is some tendency for losses to coincide with periods of high marginal utility.

By construction, mean reversion and value strategies are short a structural break. That is, if an asset becomes secularly cheaper or richer, with no mean reversion, value indicators point to ever larger counterpositions (e.g., these indicators suggest shorting the asset if it becomes richer). Yet, on occasion those dangerous words—"this time is different"—turn out to be true. Accordingly, a stop-loss rule or a cap on active position size may inject an important dose of humility into value strategies.

Because this book has a contrarian leaning, it is important to keep in mind some blunders contrarians have made when structural changes took place:

- Investors buying into declining industries might never see a reversal. Railroads were the classic example where a generation of equity-holders and debt-holders lost fortunes as the automobile won over Americans. (Warren Buffett's recent interest in the industry suggests that even railroad stocks may shine once more—but the buggy-whip industry never recovered and never will.)
- As the great investment writer Peter Bernstein often reminded us, in 1958 the unthinkable happened: equity (dividend) yields fell below Treasury yields. Through recorded history, bond yields had always acted as a floor for equity yields, so it was widely believed that this anomaly had to be temporary. Well, it took half a century for equity yields to rise again above bond yields, and then only for a few months in the winter of 2008–2009. Many contrarian investors stumbled for years after 1958, underweighting stocks before capitulating to the new reality that equity yields were going to be lower than bond yields for a very long time but that equity total returns would be higher. Only with hindsight did they learn the importance of distinguishing between nominal concepts (Treasury yield) and real concepts (dividend yield) in an inflation-prone world.
- Investors who were timing their bond duration exposures based on the real yield level chalked up losses for over two decades as global government bond yields kept falling from their mid-1980s' highs. In particular, macro-traders looking for Japanese bond yields to rise have been kept waiting for a decade or more, thus the moniker "The Widowmaker" for this trade. Admittedly, one can make a good case that we are now close to a secular turning point (see Section 27.3).

As a current example, if the ongoing rerating between advanced and emerging market investments is sustained in the coming years, contrarian investors fighting the relative Richening of emerging markets will lose money.

# 12.6 RELATIONS BETWEEN VALUE AND OTHER INDICATORS FOR EQUITY SELECTION

Virtually all equity quant managers go beyond value and try to take advantage of a diverse set of return sources in their stock selection and portfolio construction algorithms [3]. The two most popular equity selection indicators are value and momentum (I discuss the latter in Section 14.5.) Conveniently, these two are natural opposites: one typically buys stocks after falling prices, the other after rising prices. The complementary nature (negative correlation) of value and momentum strategies means that combining the two can sharply reduce portfolio volatility and boost Sharpe ratios:

- One of my favourite clichés is "when value and momentum clash, value almost always loses first." This aphorism suggests patience when responding to value signals, perhaps using them only when they are extreme or when anti-value momentum is stalling.

Growth and profitability indicators may also be negatively correlated with value. Such indicators can serve as control variables that extract the parts of valuation ratios which reflect expected cash flows (and not just expected returns). Combining value with such indicators boosts average returns by filtering out persistent losers.

Other indicators are positively related to value, and thus are less useful diversifiers:

- Carry indicators such as dividend yield contain overlapping information with value.
- Long-run reversal strategies buy assets that have low current prices compared with their own history 3 to 5 years earlier; these strategies have an unsurprising empirical link to value strategies (which buy assets that have low current prices compared with some simple fundamental metric). In contrast, short-run reversal strategies buy assets that have low current prices compared with their week-ago or month-ago level; such strategies are more related to market making (liquidity provision) than to long-run value. Likewise, pairs trading strategies (which buy assets that have recently underperformed their closely related peers) are less related to value.
Distress and default risk are positively correlated with value but they are associated with low returns. Thus, many value investors try to filter out this subset of low-multiple stocks, either systematically or discretionarily.
- Valuation models differ from value strategies in that they buy stocks whose market prices differ from their intrinsic or "fundamental" (fair) values after incorporating estimates of firms' profitability and growth prospects. (In contrast, value strategies may buy "dogs" with obviously poor growth prospects.) The "economic value added (EVA) model" created by the firm of Stern Stewart is the best known implementation of a residual income valuation model.
- More surprisingly, the volatility puzzle overlaps empirically with the value premium. Stocks with low return volatility tend to outperform high-volatility stocks. Low-volatility stocks are often boring value stocks, while high-volatility stocks are often exciting growth stocks with high price multiples.

There are still other indicators that appear fundamentally uncorrelated with value strategies: quality (e.g., balance sheet strength or low accounting accruals) and net share issuance. However, there is some evidence of rising correlations across all quant strategies, presumably due to common positions among leveraged traders.

# 12.7 NOTES

[1] Like many others, I prefer to use economic intuition as one guard against data mining, but the virtues of such intuition can be overstated as our intuition is inevitably influenced by past experiences. Purely data-driven statistical approaches are even worse, but at least then statistical models can help assess the magnitude of data-mining bias.

[2] Here are some additional points on VMG:

—No trading costs or financing costs related to shorting are subtracted from VMG returns. This is typical for academic studies because such costs are trade specific and/or investor specific and, moreover, such data are not available over long histories.

—VMG is constructed in a deliberately conservative ("underfitted") manner. It uses a simple sorting approach and long publication lags. Virtually all U.S. stocks are included in the universe. No sector adjustments are made, so the portfolio may have persistent industry concentrations.

—A monthly VMG series is available since 1926 and a daily series since 1963. Given its public availability, VMG has been used in thousands of academic studies—often with its original name HML ("high minus low"). In recent articles FF have begun to use the more revealing "value-minus-growth" name. Ken French's website (http://mba.tuck.dartmouth.edu/pages/faculty/ken.french/data_library.html) regularly updates these data and similar returns for other valuation ratios and other cross-sectional stock selection indicators, as well as some non-U.S. value returns since 1975. Since a composite value strategy (B/P, CF/P, D/P, E/P) in the U.S. performs much like B/P (VMG) alone, I focus on the latter here.

—See Appendix B on data sources for other details.

[3] Quant managers' optimizers help them access (and smartly weight) desired exposures and hedge away unwanted risks, while controlling trading costs. In this book, I focus on simple portfolios constructed by sorting stocks based on some indicator. Smarter approaches take into account the interaction between indicators. For example, cross-sectional multi-factor regression implies particular portfolio weights that have desirable characteristics for return factors: each portfolio has unit beta to the factor it mimics and is uncorrelated with other factors.

Source notes. There are plenty of practitioner books on value investing in equity markets, from Graham-Dodd (1934) to Montier (2009). Classic academic articles, though by no means the first ones on value investing, are Fama-French (1992) and Lakonishok-Vishny-Shleifer (1994). A subset of LSV subsequently co-authored important articles on value investing with La Porta (1997), Barberis (1998, 2003), and Chan-

Karceski (2003). Fama-French later extended their analysis to global markets (1998) and to earlier histories (with Davis, 2000), and they explored links to the CAPM (2006a), to profitability (2006b), and to changing stock characteristics (2007a, b).

On sector neutrality, style timing, and other refinements, see Asness et al. (2000), Barberis-Shleifer (2003), Cohen-Polk-Vuolteenraho (2003), Qian-Hua-Sorensen (2007), Lancetti-Nordquist (2008), Phalippou (2008), and Mezrich (2010).

On fundamental index debate, see Arnott-Hsu-Moore (2005), Arnott-Hsu-West (2008), Arnott et al. (2010), as well as Kaplan (2007) and Perold (2007).

These referenced studies (and this book's Section 6.3) discuss reasons for the value premium, but see also Barberis-Shleifer-Wurgler (2005) for further behavioral explanations, and Campbell-Vuolteenaho (2004a), Petkova-Zhang (2005), Asness-Moskowitz-Pedersen (2009), and Chen-Novy-Marx-Zhang (2010) for rational explanations. On the link to distress and credit risk, see Campbell-Hilscher-Szilagyi (2008) and Avramov et al. (2010). On value strategy's success outside equity selection, see Arnott-Hsu-West (2008), Asness-Moskowitz-Pedersen (2009), and Blitz-van Vliet (2009).

# 13

# Currency carry

- Buying high-yielding currencies and shorting low-yielding currencies has been a profitable strategy for  $50+$  years.
- Academic theories suggest that any initial cross-country yield advantage should be offset by future currency depreciation. In practice, this has not happened on average, and naive yield seeking has paid off. Forward rates have been biased predictors of future spot exchange rates.
- Carry strategies within G10 markets have an attractive long-run Sharpe ratio—which is even higher in emerging markets and higher if refinements or carry timing are used. As a result, the strategy became very crowded before 2008.
- Success increasingly looks like compensation for the strategy's riskiness, however. A carry strategy resembles selling lottery tickets that pay off in bad times. The combination of negative skew (frequent small gains punctuated by infrequent large losses) and terrible timing of losses warrants a large risk premium.
- Alternative explanations include biased investor expectations and so-called peso problems.
- Yield seeking adds value in the long run in many other asset classes besides currencies. Yet, the reward-to-risk ratio has been noticeably higher in cross-country carry strategies than in other carry strategies, such as within-country credit or term carry strategies.

# 13.1 INTRODUCTION

The second dynamic strategy I study is yield-seeking carry trading, with a focus on FX (see cube). A currency carry trade involves investment in a high-yielding currency such as the Australian dollar, funded by borrowing in a low-yielding currency such as the Japanese yen [1]. This chapter surveys various aspects of carry trading: I document the empirical performance of a naive carry strategy, explore various refinements, review alternative explanations for the historical success of the carry trade, and compare the currency carry strategy with other carry strategies.

Currency carry strategies in developed markets have been embarrassingly successful for over 50 years. In the past 15 years they have fared even better in emerging markets. Despite recent losses, long-run Sharpe ratios for currency carry strategies are higher than for equities, fixed income, or credit.

The embarrassment partly reflects the sheer naivety of these strategies. Academics have long argued that simply equating nominal interest rate differentials with prospective returns fails to take into account the market's expectations of exchange rate changes. According to the uncovered interest parity (UIP) hypothesis, these expectations involve currency depreciation and capital losses for high-yielding currencies that exactly offset any initial yield advantage. UIP implies that a yield which is  $5\%$  higher in Australia than in Japan reveals the market's expectation of  $5\%$  depreciation in the AUD/JPY spot exchange rate over the next year (thus equalizing ex ante returns across currencies, by assumption). But the academics turned out to be wrong and the naive carry-seekers right. Empirical studies from about 1980 onwards have persistently told the story that subsequent currency depreciation did

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/fd32067870e8afe2a478026d2f4c70fbec8a50e7d0d06e394511acc961faeb7b.jpg)

not, on average, fully offset the carry advantage (and thus equalize ex post currency returns). This finding is called forward rate bias. Often, the carry advantage was even augmented by capital gains from short-term appreciation of the higher yielding currency! Thus, the UIP hypothesis failed empirically in an even more dramatic and consistent way than its close cousin—the "pure expectations hypothesis"—did in interest rate markets [2].

# 13.2 HISTORICAL AVERAGE RETURNS

Figure 13.1 shows that G10 currencies with the highest average yields since 1983 (NZD, AUD) also earned the highest dollar returns. For example, NZD yields averaged  $9.5\%$  while U.S. yields averaged  $5.5\%$ ; instead of the  $4\%$  annual depreciation that the UIP predicted, the NZD actually appreciated mildly against the dollar between 1983 and

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/8e717c4f152860172cbc79148c0890a3e196d5f1b761be0053a896c48ad36be2.jpg)

Figure 13.1. Pairwise average returns for dollar pairs vs. average carry, 1983-2009. Sources: Bloomberg, Citigroup, own calculations.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/5ca7802e1d64701dffe395b020946778a2bb17f565e65170dc76965bbb9f1bcb.jpg)

Figure 13.2. Performance of G10 carry strategy: buy three, sell three each week, 1983-2009. Sources: Bloomberg, Citigroup, own calculations.

2009. At the other end of the spectrum, the main low-yielding currencies (JPY, CHF) earned the lowest dollar returns among non-U.S. deposits. At least these low-yielders managed to strengthen so much against the USD that it more than offset their average yield disadvantage.

Let us study the performance of a typical currency carry strategy. Figure 13.2 focuses on my baseline variant of a G10 carry strategy, without bells and whistles except that simple diversification is allowed. The strategy involves, each week, ranking G10 currencies by the level of the short-term interest rate (thus this is called a "ranking model"), buying the top-three currencies and selling (shorting) the bottom-three currencies using  $50\%$ ,  $30\%$ ,  $20\%$  weights. The full sample statistics over 1983-2009 are  $6.1\%$  annual excess returns [3],  $10.5\%$  volatility, and a Sharpe ratio  $(SR)$  of 0.61. Despite crises in every decade, performance consistency has been impressive. For each of the  $1980s'$ ,  $1990s'$ , and  $2000s'$  decadal subsets, the SR is in the narrow range of 0.58 to 0.63. For more benign windows such as 1994-2006, the SR exceeded 1. I show below that some variations to the carry indicator—and, especially, extension of the carry trades strategy to include emerging markets—would have improved my baseline results. The 3-year rolling SR has always ranged between  $-0.5$  and 2. Yet, drawdowns have been deep:  $-36\%$  in 2008,  $-28\%$  in 1993, and  $-26\%$  in 1986.

The period since 1983 coincides with the benign Great Moderation period, suggesting that carry trades may have had exceptional tailwinds behind them. However, performance seems to be nearly as good for the preceding 30 years. Lustig-Verdelhan (2007) make available simulated carry-ranked portfolio returns going back deep into the Bretton Woods regime when most exchange rates were fixed, with occasional devaluations. They sort a broad and growing country set into eight portfolios, with only annual rebalancing. Applying my top-three, bottom-three weighting to their carry-ranked portfolios, I find a 0.51 SR for 1953–1982. Other sources also document positive carry returns for the post-Bretton Woods period before 1983. Over all these windows, currency carry provides better risk-adjusted returns than static asset class premia in equities or fixed income.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/9d9293179ba4e20a17b4cb1f19e2ce19d31783573f9b2d43b5ebad3a4fa7b66f.jpg)

Figure 13.3. Average excess returns of G10 carry-ranked single-currency portfolios, 1983-2009. Sources: Bloomberg, Citigroup, own calculations.

How consistent is the pattern of higher long-run average returns for higher yielding currencies? Figure 13.3 shows the performance of single-currency portfolios where G10 currencies are ranked each week based on their yield level. The relation is not quite monotonic but the highest yielding portfolio earned the highest average return and the lowest yielding portfolio earned the lowest return. Moreover, all single-currency portfolios ranking in the top half by yield have higher returns than all portfolios ranking in the bottom half.

The height of the columns (excess returns) in Figure 13.3 tends to exceed the height of the triangles (interest rate differentials) because the dollar was the worst performing G10 currency during the sample period, so dollar-denominated average FX returns were positive. Since the gap between the highest yielding and lowest yielding portfolios is similar, at  $8\%$  to  $9\%$ , for both average carry and average returns, we can say that currency portfolios pretty much earned their carry, no more, no less. There was no offset (UIP would have predicted that all columns should have a height of zero; the carry advantage is just offset by FX depreciation) but also no augmenting (which would have added more capital gains to high-yield portfolios than to low-yield portfolios).

Note that these results for currency portfolios ranked by yield also reflect currencies' migration across ranks over time—not just their average ranks. Looking at the static holdings of each dollar pair individually in Figure 13.1 shows that typical high-yielders outperformed typical low-yielders but by much less than their average yield spread implied. In contrast, a long-short portfolio that is dynamically formed by each week re-ranking currency holdings by their initial yields earned nearly double the return of a static (not re-ranked) carry portfolio. The static carry portfolio captures an average relation—currencies like NZD and AUD have relatively high yields in every decade while JPY and CHF tend to have low yields—but it wholly misses time variation in the relative yields of currencies. For example, USD was the highest yielding currency in 2000 and the lowest yielding from 1991 to 1993. This is important; the significant predictive ability documented in classic forward bias regressions was all about time variation in one currency pair's yield spread. The dynamically re-ranked portfolio incorporates this time variation and thus offers a higher ex ante yield spread and a higher ex post return gap than the static carry portfolio.

# Comments on my baseline variant

Selection biases. Note the hindsight in picking the currently active "G10" universe: USD-CAD-AUD-NZD-JPY-EUR-GBP-CHF-SEK-NOK. As in most other studies, I exclude currencies that were fixed or pegged to another currency, along with currencies that have or had significant capital controls. Due to this selection bias, I might overstate returns by excluding hyperinflated and/or defaulted markets that fell out of my universe. In practice, this has not been a major issue because the past two decades have been characterized by global disinflation and improving policy discipline. More likely, I may understate returns by excluding many emerging currencies (that performed especially well) as well as former EMU currencies. (In my analysis, only the DEM proxies the euro before 1999 although there were many currencies that were combined to create the euro. Many currencies that would later become part of the euro had higher yields than the DEM, so that excluding them biases the result downward by excluding EMU convergence trades, which—with hindsight—were profitable.)

Diversification. Some diversification across carry pairs tends to improve risk-adjusted returns and certainly protects against catastrophic hyperinflation and/or a default event in one country. This is why we use a buy-three, sell-three basket. Diversification does imply dilution away from the strongest signals; to balance this we give higher weights to the highest yielding and lowest yielding currencies. Over my full sample, an undiversified first-tenth trade (i.e., buying the single highest yielding and shorting the lowest yielding currency) gave a moderately higher excess return  $(8\%)$  but much higher volatility  $(16\%)$ , resulting in an SR of 0.51—compared with the base case  $6\% / 10\% / 0.61$  noted above. In the other extreme, a diversified portfolio that buys equal amounts of the highest yielding five currencies and that shorts the lowest yielding five currencies earned  $4\%$  with a low  $7\%$  volatility, resulting in an SR of 0.62. Thus, in risk-adjusted terms, diversification across signals is somewhat more beneficial than exploiting the strongest signal.

Ranking model vs. pairwise carry trading. Many studies analyze a diversified basket of USD pairs instead of a ranking model. If the pairs are simply added up, the effective portfolio of positions is similar to that achieved with this ranking approach, especially if U.S. interest rates are near the middle of the G10 pack. One difference is that ranking model positions cannot be only "one-way" (say, short the USD against all other currencies in 1991-1993).

Costs. In line with the literature I focus on a pre-cost estimate. Fortunately, turnover is modest (due to slow-moving interest rate gaps) and major currencies have very low trading costs for institutional investors. Adjusting for the bid-ask spreads observed in the 2000s reduces the SR by 0.1 at most. Admittedly, trading costs were higher in the first decade of the sample.

# Comments on alternative variants

Other horizons. Carry trading could be implemented using different trading horizons. Cross-country spreads are typically so slow moving that we get broadly similar results using weekly, monthly, and quarterly trading frequencies. When changing weekly trading frequency to a monthly trading frequency, there is no decline in long-run performance—the SR may rise or fall by 0.05 at most, depending on the specification.

Other maturities. In principle, it is appropriate to implement carry trades using the same trading horizon (maturity of deposit or term of a forward currency contract) as the trading signal. In practice, cross-country spreads are so similar across maturities that this hardly matters. Historical performance is broadly similar whether we use one-week, one-month, or 3-month deposit rates as signals. Once we move beyond money markets for carry signals, the results tend to deteriorate.

Other carry indicators. Some academics favor real interest rate differentials on theoretical grounds, but using real carry instead of nominal carry would have dropped the full sample SR from 0.60 to 0.47. The more popular and successful variant of carry trading involves dividing carry by some volatility measure, effectively giving us an ex ante Sharpe ratio as the trading signal. For my full sample, selecting currencies based on the carry-to-volatility ratio—dividing yields by the recent volatility of a trade-weighted currency index—would have raised the SR from 0.61 to 0.70.

Dynamic portfolio construction. Scaling position sizes inversely by recent historical or option-implied volatility tends to improve Sharpe ratios. Using mean variance optimization in sizing carry positions is another potential approach, but its chief benefit is to take advantage of such volatility normalization. Scaling position sizes dynamically by signal strength does not seem to help much. I don't use any of these refinements in my deliberately conservative baseline approach but use constant dollar sizes each period instead.

# 13.3 IMPROVEMENTS/REFINEMENTS TO THE BASELINE CARRY STRATEGY

The previous section considered small variations to my baseline currency carry trading approach. Here I focus on three more intrusive ways to improve carry trading returns:

- broadening the currency universe to emerging markets;
- combining carry with other indicators; and
- timing the carry strategy with ex ante opportunity measures, seasonals, and various conditioners.

# Broadening to emerging markets

A broader opportunity set should improve results. Instead of extending the G10 universe to include more countries, it is common to analyze and trade the emerging market universe separately. I have studied a similar carry strategy among 15 emerging currencies plus USD. The sample only begins in 1994 and covers, despite various crises, an exceptionally benign window for emerging markets. Improving fundamentals in emerging countries and easy liquidity conditions in developed countries gave markets huge tailwinds and make this a biased sample. Indeed, the period since 1994 is so benign that the only indicator that works even better than carry is inflation: high-inflation currencies consistently outperformed low-inflation currencies, because inflation rates fell in countries with high initial inflation. Among reasonably liquid currencies without capital controls, there were no cases of countries drifting into sharp devaluation via hyperinflation. The 1997-1998 Asian devaluations, for example, occurred after a period of low inflation, were followed by a spike in inflation, and subsequently benefited from disinflation and real exchange rate appreciation. (I return later to the important point that no country in my universe suffered from hyperinflation during the period studied.)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/becb9104bcaede2fe1994a0afbd10610d691db4173ad6444bf321a3b5b3fb02c.jpg)

Figure 13.4. Cumulative excess returns of emerging market and G10 carry strategies, 1994-2009. Sources: Bloomberg, Citigroup, Haver Analytics, own calculations.

As Figure 13.4 shows, emerging market carry strategies earned much higher returns than G10 carry strategies. Average excess returns over cash were  $16.4\%$ , with  $12\%$  volatility, and a Sharpe ratio of 1.30: even including the heavy selloff in October 2008, which was the worst drawdown during this sample. This high SR surely is overstated by the abnormally benign nature of the sample and cannot be seen as an unbiased guide to the future. Moreover, trading costs are higher for emerging market currencies.

Larger profits partly reflect wider yield gaps across emerging markets. The highest yielding emerging market portfolios have average yield spreads over the U.S. of more than  $15\%$ , while the lowest yielding portfolios have slightly negative yield spreads (see Figure 13.5). During this sample period, average yield gap translated fully into outperformance on average. This is hardly an equilibrium outcome, given very different inflation levels across countries, but another sign of the abnormally benign sample period. As an aside, many of the bars in Figure 13.5 are below the line because emerging currencies tended to depreciate against the USD over this sample period.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/16f67f207b5e54e8f32c80e242773d785e2d0e164ec63a50b603f45ac3aae938.jpg)

Figure 13.5. Average excess returns of emerging market carry-ranked portfolios, 1994-2009. Sources: Bloomberg, Haver Analytics, own calculations.

# Combining carry with other indicators

One idea is to use a broader measure of expected return than carry. As an accounting identity, cross-country yield spreads (carry) reflect some combination of the market's expectations of future spot rate changes  $(E\Delta S)$  and ex ante excess return (required currency risk premium). Conversely, ex ante excess return reflects both carry and  $E(\Delta S)$ . If we can isolate the unobservable  $E(\Delta S)$  we can better capture ex ante excess return. The random walk hypothesis states that  $E(\Delta S) = 0$ , so carry equals expected excess return. Various theoretical models imply other drivers of  $E(\Delta S)$ . One shortcut is to use survey-based consensus forecasts of future exchange rates as a direct proxy for  $E(\Delta S)$ . However, empirically such survey-based expected returns do not predict near-term returns as well as simple carry, perhaps due to the noise in survey data and perhaps due to irrationality in expectations.

Formal theoretical models have had little success in explaining, let alone predicting, exchange rate movements. Thus, systematic currency-trading models tend to be purely atheoretical or only loosely guided by formal models. Many such models amount to combining carry with other indicators. Candidates for additional indicators include

- Carry plus value. One could proxy  $E(\Delta S)$  with some expected normalization toward a "fair value" exchange rate that can be estimated based on purchasing power parity (PPP) or other valuation models. Or one could use valuation filters that rule out carry trading when it conflicts with extreme mispricing signals.
- Carry plus momentum. Exchange rates tend to trend, or at least they used to; trend-following strategies within the G10 have become much less profitable during the past decade. One could add a trend-following signal as a proxy for  $E(\Delta S)$ . Using stop-loss rules can serve the same purpose as using trend-following models.
- Carry plus yield changes. Besides the yield level, recent changes in yields—especially at maturities beyond money markets—have empirical predictive ability, perhaps because they reflect the market's evolving monetary policy or growth expectations.

Delayed overshooting models explain the apparent underreaction of currencies to yield changes and suggest recent yield changes as yet another proxy for  $E(\Delta S)$ .

# Box 13.1. FX as an asset class

The debate about whether FX is an asset class remains unresolved:

- The positive view stresses that FX is a source of return fluctuations for many assets. For unhedged bond investments, exchange rate changes are often a greater risk than bond yield fluctuations. Moreover, active FX trading may be a source of return—most obviously based on carry but potentially also based on other indicators.
- One counterargument is that FX is only a partial investment decision; if you invest in a currency you still need to decide which asset you buy; conversely, if you buy a foreign asset, you can decide whether you keep or hedge FX risks. Moreover, in the long run, FX returns appear to roughly wash out. Even in the short run, no FX risk premium can compensate both counterparties in an FX trade for exchange rate uncertainty. FX is a zero-sum game in the sense that currency appreciation benefits one side of an FX trade and hurts the other.
- However, academic theories imply that risk premia should reflect covariances rather than standalone FX volatility. For example, a JPY-denominated deposit may serve as a safe haven asset for both Japanese and Australian investors because the yen tends to appreciate during financial crises; thus all global investors may require a lower return from yen-denominated deposits than from some higher yielding counterparts. (I will not dwell on small nonlinearities, such as Jensen's inequality.)

Economists concede that their models can explain or predict fractions of actual currency movements that are so trivial that today's spot exchange rate may be a better forecast of future spot rates than anything else. Proximate determinants from order flows are much better than any fundamental determinants at explaining FX changes. However, over time, various predictability patterns (potentially related to time-varying ex ante FX premia) have been documented, and certain indicators are able to contemporaneously explain FX moves.

Inflation differentials across countries influence exchange rate changes, but purchasing power parity holds only approximately and even then only in the very long run. Relative interest rates (levels and changes, short and long, nominal and real) have some impact on exchange rates, perhaps because they are proxies for monetary policy or economic growth. Productivity and real-growth differences, terms of trade, and current account and/or capital flow developments also matter. Yet, many of these statistical relations are weak and are hardly stable over time or across countries. I will not discuss either theories or empirical evidence on exchange rate determination further in this book, nor will I cover the role of global and local risk factors in largely integrated world markets, prospects for dollar, or alternatives to the dollar as global reserve currencies.

# "Timing" the carry strategy with ex ante opportunity size, seasonals, and various conditioners

# Ex ante opportunity

If carry were the only source of expected return, the evolution of ex ante carry opportunity (signal strength) could be measured by the dispersion of deposit rates or of carry-to-volatility ratios across G10 markets. Intuitively, if yields range across countries between  $0\%$  in Japan and  $10\%$  in Australia, dispersion is higher and ex ante carry opportunity is better than if all yields are in a narrow range between  $0\%$  and  $3\%$ . Figure 13.6 shows the stable and relatively narrow ex ante carry opportunity since the mid-1990s, compared with the 1980s and especially compared with 1992 when European currency crises forced some central banks to defend their currencies with sky-high interest rates.

It turns out that these ex ante measures have (only) modest ability to identify good and bad times to load up on the carry trade. One reason is that they only give a partial picture of ex ante opportunity.

Both lines were flat between 2002 and 2007, despite the growing popularity of the currency carry trade over that time period. In this case, different inflation and growth experiences across G10 countries kept policy rate dispersion quite wide, while dwindling ex ante opportunity for carry trading manifested itself elsewhere, through spot exchange rate Richening of high-yielding currencies. As noted above, a broader expected return measure would add to carry the slow expected normalization of overvalued and undervalued currencies  $[E(\Delta S)]$ . According to various simple valuation metrics, the high-yielding AUD and NZD were about  $20\%$  undervalued against the USD in 2001-2002; by mid-2007 they were about  $20\%$  overvalued. Meanwhile, the low-yielding JPY became the most undervalued G10 currency. Overall, the correlation between G10 carry ranks and value ranks shifted from positive in 2001-2002 to strongly negative in 2006-2007—and then back to positive after the 2008 carry crash. (Positive correlation between carry rank and value rank means that a currency that is attractive from a carry, or relative interest rate, perspective is also attractive from the point of view of fundamental value, typically measured by PPP.)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/12b085ca2f6485da30934915cb65eca5b2b9728f542b03ec33402cfa9a712ea6.jpg)

Figure 13.6. Evolving ex ante carry opportunity among G10 currencies, 1983-2009. Sources: Bloomberg, Citigroup, own calculations.

# Seasonals

FX carry strategy returns exhibit strong seasonal patterns that have not been widely reported. They also match better known seasonals in equity markets and in corporate credit markets. Over the past three decades, the average monthly return of the carry strategy has been about  $2\%$  in January and about  $0.5\%$  in other months. January excess return has been negative in only four years since 1976: in 1992, 1995, 1998, and 2008—each of which saw much worse drawdowns from the carry strategy later in the year. This outcome may be a coincidence but it echoes the finding that January equity market performance has some predictive ability for rest-of-the-year returns (further details in Chapter 25). Incorporating these two seasonal biases would easily improve backtested FX carry strategy performance—for example, doubling position sizes for January and halving sizes for the rest of the year if the January return had been negative would have boosted the Sharpe ratio since 1983 from 0.6 to 0.8. Any such backtest improvements are subject to data-mining bias, so some skepticism is warranted.

# Conditioners (regime indicators)

As we have seen, ex ante opportunities and seasonal effects have some ability to predict carry returns. However, the jackpot question of carry "timing" relates to carry crashes. Given the characteristic pattern of small gradual gains and rare violent losses, avoiding carry crashes would be hugely valuable.
