(Violent losses are often, but not always, associated with position unwinds.) For the same reason, the task is hugely difficult.

How do investors approach this task? Slow-moving warning signs include overvalued high-yield currencies and overcrowded carry positions. Possible triggers include falls in other risky assets (although such contagion or spillover effects tend to be nearly instantaneous, limiting their usefulness); rising volatility and correlations; and tightening global liquidity conditions (especially in low-yielding "funding currency" countries). Predicting the start of a crash is nonetheless a mug's game. Slow-moving market-timers try to prepare for a crash when warning signals flash yellow. Faster market players only respond to the triggers and/or actual carry losses, knowing that they will be too late to catch the exact market turn (but they also benefit from missing several false alarms).

Historically, carry unwind episodes have lasted long enough to make some backward-looking indicators useful for predicting next week or next month carry performance. Buy-side and sell-side traders and strategists (including your author) have developed various indicators of global risk aversion, typically (over-)fitting them to previous crash episodes. Of course, such regime indicators are not robust. For example, the 1997-1998 carry unwinds emanated from emerging markets while the 2007-2008 unwinds emanated from the United States. Some crashes coincided with central bank tightening while others occurred despite central bank easing, reflecting the tightening of broader liquidity conditions; 2008 is an example of the latter. Even if regime indicators (happen to) work at the right time, they will raise numerous false alarms before a hit.

I will not delve deeper into such models but instead, in Figure 13.7, just showcase a few of the most popular risk aversion indicators that have had some historic success. One significant result is that when past month carry losses are in their top (worst) decile, average return over the following week is negative, at an annualized rate of  $-3\%$ , while in "normal times" (the remaining  $90\%$  of time) annualized next week return is  $8\%$ . Thus, carry profits exhibit short-term persistence; historically, it has been worthwhile to

Figure 13.7. Next week carry profits following recent losses or turbulence vs. normal times, 1986-2009.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/65d32a03842ebbf96afe72c0898b01f729b37f74f35c86b431e696548f25f049.jpg)

Sources: Bloomberg, Citigroup, own calculations.

look in the rearview mirror and use a stop-loss discipline [4]. Volatility data may also help style timing. The strongest relation is that if implied currency volatilities have trended sharply higher during the preceding month (an up-move ranking in the top decile), predicted carry return for the following week is negative  $(-7\%)$  annualized). These risk aversion indicators often flash red at the same time. They unambiguously reveal that we have seen a carry crash; more tentatively, they suggest that the poor performance is likely to continue.

Apart from such "timing" indicators that focus on the global environment for carry trading, one can also develop country-specific vulnerability indices. Combining a variety of economic and financial indicators that have been able to predict subsequent devaluations has been an especially popular exercise in emerging markets analysis.

# 13.4 WHY DO CARRY STRATEGIES WORK?

Empirical evidence showing high historical returns or predictability of returns can always be interpreted in different ways. Main proposed explanations in the past 30 years involve currency risk premia, irrational expectations, and peso problems. My interpretation straddles all three but is closest to a risk premium story. I argue that currency carry strategies can be characterized as selling lottery tickets that pay off (to the buyer) in really bad times. This class of trading strategies performs especially well in prolonged good times. Persistent success in turn attracts return-chasing inflows, which improves Sharpe ratios further and results in overvaluation and overcrowding. Until that rare disaster materializes, many investors underestimate the risk and extrapolate from past performance (or hope that they can jump off the bandwagon before most others).

Mainstream academic explanation for the "forward premium puzzle" in the 1980s was that the profitability of naive yield-seeking currency strategies reflected a currency risk premium. However, neither the CAPM nor any other equilibrium asset-pricing model, including those focusing on exchange rate determination, could really explain why such a risk premium existed. For example, the equity market beta of carry strategies seemed to be close to zero. Proponents of a rational explanation argued that carry gains could still represent a generic risk premium. Even if we do not know or understand the economic reason for the risk premium in currencies (or other markets), we do know that any positive ex ante risk premium should imply positive carry.

Early alternative explanations based on irrational expectations were no more compelling. The main stories involve investor underreaction to yield changes but such underreaction needs to be quite severe because carry strategies really exploit yield levels.

The peso problem explanation seemed plausible at first. Perhaps markets were rationally assigning a small probability to a large depreciation—typically in a country with a higher inflation or a current account deficit—that simply had not (yet) happened in the observed sample. However, as decades passed and evidence of high-yield currencies' outperformance across various currency pairs accumulated, this explanation, too, lost credibility. One aspect of the rare event story did become more credible over time. Returns on carry-seeking strategies exhibited increasing negative skewness—an "up by the stairs, down by the elevator" pattern. Moreover, the largest carry losses occurred during flight-to-quality episodes. This observation led traders and strategists to develop carry-timing models based on global risk aversion indicators [5].

Academics, too, began to pay attention. There seemed to be a whiff of systematic risk in carry strategies, but the inherent rarity of adverse events made it hard to conduct any serious statistical analysis. Cochrane (1999) had the foresight to stress parallels among carry-oriented strategies in equity, bond, and foreign exchange markets as well as volatility selling and convergence trading. "All of these strategies can be thought of as (selling) catastrophe insurance. Most of the time, they earn a small premium. Once in a great while they lose a lot, and they lose a lot in times of financial catastrophe." The poor timing of losses is crucial because this characteristic really warrants a high risk premium. While the fair reward for these strategies may be high, observed outperformance before 2008 amounted to even more than a fair reward because the previous 50 years had been a benign sample without devastating financial panics. Thus the story had both risk premium and peso problem elements.

A few years ago, academics began to re-explore the puzzling success of currency carry strategies; their risk-adjusted returns appeared better than those that motivated the literature on the equity premium puzzle and the value puzzle. Trading costs and market frictions were among the early "explanations" but currencies are highly liquid and the carry strategy requires relatively low turnover. The focus then reverted to risk-based explanations and to addressing the peso problem hypothesis with a new twist—the use of options data.

Beta risk. The central idea in modern asset-pricing theory is that investors require a high risk premium for assets that perform poorly in bad times. Conversely, investors are willing to accept lower long-run expected returns for safe haven assets, such as Treasury bonds or the yen, that tend to do well in bad times. Noting that the CAPM more or less defines bad times as a decline in the equity market, the currency carry strategy's market beta has become more evidently positive over time. The Consumption CAPM associates bad times with a slowing of consumption growth (or, what is worse, an actual decline in consumption); the currency carry strategy also appears to have a positive Consumption

CAPM beta. Intuitively, low-yielding currencies like the yen can serve as useful recession hedges for U.S. investors and thus justify a negative risk premium.

Looking back, carry returns are not just beta like, they have especially ugly downside betas. Since the mid-1980s, the three worst months for the baseline FX carry strategy described above were

- October 1987 (equity market crash: the FX carry strategy returned  $-11\%$ );
- October 2008 (virtually all markets crash: FX carry returned  $-11\%$ ); and
- October 1998 (aftermath of the Russia/LTCM crash: FX carry returned  $-9\%$ ).

Obviously, these were all painful times to lose money. The relation to consumption data is weaker because, while all three months were characterized by recession fears, only the 2008 episode was classified as taking place within a recession. The relation is more obvious when "bad times" are associated with financial crises, liquidity droughts, and equity market selloffs. Carry returns are also linked to hedge fund returns and (inversely) to implied volatilities in equity and currency markets, especially when markets are distressed.

Skewness risk. As it gradually emerged that carry return distribution is asymmetric, academics began to explore whether this negative skew could explain high average returns and Sharpe ratios as a risk premium. The asymmetry in realized returns could reflect (time-varying) rare disasters, but it could also be partly endogenous, caused by carry trade activity (see Brunnermeier-Nagel-Pedersen, 2008). Carry positions are gradually built up while unwinds tend to be rapid and sharp when leveraged liquidity providers hit funding constraints. "Hot money" inflows into high-yielding countries also tend to reverse sharply during financial crises and funds get repatriated (typically to low-yielding, current-account-surplus countries).

The study of skewness effects is enhanced by considering the potential impact of option positions. Several studies show that investors can hedge the "crash risk" (left-tail risk) in currency carry positions by buying short-dated options and still be left with significantly positive returns. Unlike the unhedged carry trade, the "crash-neutral" or "hedged" carry trade exhibits positive skew. Yet, it has in many cases offered volatility-adjusted returns at least as good as the unhedged carry trade, which has negative skew. Such findings make it doubtful that a risk premium for negative skew can fully explain the success of the carry trade.

It is even possible to compare implied skewness in option markets with realized skewness. Risk reversals (i.e., relative implied volatilities of calls vs. puts) reveal implied skewness in FX option markets. Implied skews have, on average, patterns similar to realized skews: high-yielding currencies like NZD have higher implied volatilities on the depreciation side than on the appreciation side, while the reverse is true for the low-yielding yen. More surprisingly, implied skewness of carry trades predicts future realized skewness—but with the wrong sign. Following high returns for carry trades, implied skewness is higher (the cost of crash insurance is complacently low) while the empirical likelihood of a future crash is higher (future realized skewness is likely to be negative). Conversely, after a currency crash the price of crash insurance rises even though the amount of risk (likelihood of further crash) has declined [6].

Combination of the two. While negative skewness may be important, the timing of large losses may be even more so. The hallmark of a carry currency crash is not so much the magnitude of losses but their coincidence with a high market price of risk (bad times).

So we come back to central insight in asset pricing. Assets or strategies that perform poorly in bad times warrant a high risk premium—and currency carry strategies belong to this class. While repeated many times in this book, neither part of the previous sentence was obvious to most investors until recently:

1. Not recognizing that the timing of losses matters is less forgivable because academics had preached for decades that systematic risk drives risk premia, and diversifiable risk does not. Yet, much investor commentary and market pricing focuses on standalone risk (such as volatility or default losses).
2. Underappreciating the riskiness of carry trading is more understandable since there were few observations to learn from; data could only speak clearly after good times ended. It used to be plausible to claim that FX carry profits represented "alpha", but over time carry strategies became increasingly "beta like".

- The dark thick line shows the average correlation among four distinct carry trades—the top-two carry pairs in G10 markets and the top-two pairs in emerging markets. The rising average correlation highlights how much more synchronous the returns of distinct carry pairs have become.
- In addition to the correlated variability in returns from carry trades, there is substantial correlation with broader macro-risks such as that of equities. The other lines show that one-year rolling betas of G10 and EMG FX carry portfolios have risen from 0.1–0.2 in the past to about 0.5 in 2007–2009. Carry strategy systematic risk appears higher amidst high-volatility, low-liquidity regimes.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/9af9a006ce71703521a2bb889d7915d06b27ffe2fc575efc71b779698a490912.jpg)

Figure 13.8 illustrates two ways in which carry, even if it did not involve systematic risk in the past, does so now:

Figure 13.8. Alpha morphs into beta: carry becomes ever more like a systematic risk factor. Sources: Bloomberg, Citigroup, Haver Analytics, own calculations.

Why has alpha morphed into beta? Growing popularity, overcrowding, and commoditization may be the inevitable consequence of good performance for any investment strategy. As word of the success of carry strategies got around, typical return-chasing flows followed: first speculators, then institutional investors, and eventually even retail. Because carry strategy is very intuitive and does not require frequent trading, the threshold to entry is low. The makeup of carry was widespread, from Icelandic and Hungarian retail borrowers to Japanese savers ("Mrs. Watanabe"), and from corporations that borrowed in yen but kept excess cash balances in sterling, to trend-following CTAs who added carry to their menu and to the carry index products that many banks began to offer. I will return to the idea of endogenous risk (the perils of success) in Chapter 20.

Time will tell if these developments are partly reversible. Carry will likely remain a risk factor, but it will be more distinct from and less correlated with equity markets than it is now. It is conceivable that the carry-equity correlation peaked during an exceptionally systemic banking crisis in 2008 and that it will weaken in more ordinary times.

Besides the positive beta, high-yielding currencies are characterized by negative realized skew, while the reverse is true for low-yielding currencies. In addition, return asymmetry has intensified over time. Moreover, these empirical features—beta-like behavior and negative skewness—have been increasingly priced into derivatives markets in recent years. Implied correlations between carry-oriented currency pairs have risen and the skew implied in FX risk reversals has become more prominent.

# Interpreting the evidence

There is no consensus yet on the main reason for the profitability of currency carry strategies. My money is on the view that a strategy that resembles selling financial catastrophe insurance deserves a high risk premium. Selling insurance against natural catastrophes is much safer; earthquakes and floods do not tend to coincide disproportionately with equity market meltdowns and recessions. In contrast to such easily diversifiable and visible risks, carry strategies suffer from a particularly toxic and insidious type of systematic risk.

With hindsight, it seems clear that the success of carry strategies reflected more than the delivery of a well-understood risk premium. The dreadful timing of large losses was not easy to detect during good times, especially given the asymmetric nature of carry payoffs. I now elaborate how the peso problem and irrational expectations may have contributed.

The benign sample period was doubly biased. Prolonged good times and (by and large) abundant global liquidity during the Great Moderation meant that rare bad events became even rarer. This benign outcome in turn resulted in excessive optimism about sustainable carry returns and excessive complacency about carry risks and thus additional fund flows into the trade, making historical carry returns even more attractive. The long-run realized Sharpe ratio above 1 for carry strategies, widely reported in the mid-2000s, was never sustainable, as it included a period of overvaluation and overcrowding but not its unwind. The rare bad event finally materialized in 2007–2008. (Many investors thought that August 2007 was that rare event, the overdue carry trade implosion; now they hope that October 2008 was it.) The lower long-term Sharpe ratios for periods that include the implosion are more representative of "the full cycle" and are more unbiased guides for the future.

Part of the complacency was caused by the cost of eliminating the left tail having been (with hindsight) too cheap. Burnside et al. (2010a) stress that the cost of eliminating the left tail via option buying is much higher in equity markets than in FX carry trading; annualized equity index return falls from  $+7\%$  to  $-5\%$  in their sample (1987-2009), while FX carry return over the same period falls only from  $3.0\%$  to  $1.6\%$  when the left tail is eliminated. "Crash-o-phobia" had made hedging with equity index options (especially out-of-the-money puts) very expensive since the 1987 crash, but similar fears were not yet fully reflected in the pricing of other assets whose losses are concentrated in bad economic states. Coval-Jurek-Stafford (2009) present similar evidence about underpricing the risks of AAA-rated senior tranches of CDOs, best now understood in retrospect as economic catastrophe bonds, compared with pricing the risk involved in hedging equities with equity index options (see Box 19.1).

This underpricing of carry risk is a sign of irrationality in investor expectations and market pricing. An alternative, perhaps more charitable, interpretation of this evidence is that investors were gradually learning about structural change: morphing of the carry trade from alpha into beta.

Among other signs of irrationality, simple return chasing (i.e., extrapolative inflows into carry strategies) is obvious. Another feature worth recalling is the tendency for currency crash, like most forms of crash insurance, to be cheap in good times and richen up after a (currency or other) crash. Implied skewness is at best contemporaneous with crashes, or responsive to them; its ability to predict near-term carry currency returns or realized skewness is either limited or has the wrong sign.

# Other stories

While I regard the above explanations as the main drivers of the carry strategy's historical and future performance, I briefly highlight two other explanations that may have some relevance.

Hyperinflation is the one rare event that can destroy carry strategy performance even more effectively than a major unwind. A mechanical carry strategy would always go long the highest yielding currency and would quietly follow a hyperinflation country to its demise. Holding just one Zimbabwe (the country with the worst recent hyperinflation) in your portfolio would mean losing virtually the whole investment in that country and missing any subsequent wealth recovery. Zimbabwe is not a very realistic example given its capital controls, but Argentina is a country without capital controls that has had hyperinflation, albeit not quite on the Zimbabwean scale. Argentina's devaluation in early 2002 is thus a more plausible example, where international carry traders might have gotten their hands burned (although even here the impending troubles were very visible many months earlier).

I checked the impact of including Argentina in my emerging market universe. In an extreme version of this—trading only the top-ranked market against the bottom-ranked market—there would have been a huge drawdown with most of one's past accumulated wealth lost in 2002. The Sharpe ratio of the 2000s' decade would have been halved—but the losses would have already been offset within two years and long-term strategy performance still looks good with hindsight:

- To prevent hyperinflation-related currency losses, the first defense is smart country selection—ruling out from the trading universe any countries that might plausibly drift into hyperinflation. (This task is harder than hindsight suggests but not as difficult as many other types of market forecasting.) Other defenses include country-specific stop-loss limits and diversification across several countries.

Irrational expectations stories come in many forms. An early conjecture suggested that exchange rate flows and rates respond sluggishly to interest rate news. Nonsynchronous business cycles and monetary policy cycles across countries influence real rate and nominal rate differentials, and exchange rates appear to respond to these movements both contemporaneously and with a lag. Models based on this idea—sometimes called "delayed overshooting models"—can explain why recent changes in cross-country yield spreads are able to predict near-term currency moves and excess returns. Behavioral models relying on investor overconfidence on their inflation views have similar implications. However, such models are less well suited to explaining the issue at the heart of the carry strategy: why yield spread levels (which are more persistent than changes) can predict near-term currency excess returns.

Survey-based proxies of the market's exchange rate forecasts raise questions about investor irrationality. Consensus forecasts can be used to decompose yield spreads into expected exchange rate changes and required currency risk premia. Empirical studies suggest that survey-based risk premia are hardly correlated with yield differentials, suggesting that carry returns may have more to do with systematic expectational errors. When consensus predicts a large exchange rate move, the predicted move does not tend to subsequently materialize (recall random walk behavior). Survey forecasts are often influenced by some compelling fundamental story that argues against exploiting the carry advantage. High-yielding countries tend to have higher inflation and large current account deficits than low-yielding countries. Perhaps excessive belief in fundamentals has contributed to carry strategy success. Certain "unsustainable" macro-developments tend to last longer than market participants expect—and this repeated surprise benefits high-yielding currencies (e.g., USD in the early 1980s, NZD in the mid-2000s).

# 13.5 CARRY HERE, CARRY THERE, CARRY EVERYWHERE

Lastly, I look beyond currency markets and review carry trading in other asset classes. Carry is one of the few indicators that seem to add value in every market where it is examined. One reason carry indicators are useful almost everywhere you look is that carry can be a generic proxy for any risk premium or behaviorally induced undervaluation [7]. The resemblance between all carry strategies and selling financial catastrophe insurance is another reason. Carry indicators often line up well with our sense of required risk premia. These considerations not only suggest that carry strategies are risky but also that they likely will continue to produce positive returns in the future.

In many asset classes, we can decompose carry into (1) the required risk premium and (2) the market's expectations of future developments (changes in exchange rate, interest rate, credit spread, stock price, or dividend growth). There is often some hypothesis that presumes that investors are rational and risk neutral, and argues that expected returns are equated across assets if any asset's initial carry advantage is offset by expected capital losses (which reflect the market's expectations of future exchange rates, interest

Figure 13.9. Cumulative excess returns of carry-seeking strategies in four asset contexts, 1993-2009.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/d121fe5a5c5bfc683a3b2f5776b0fbb79e8ddea51a137a77839aecbe95b86feb.jpg)

Sources: Bloomberg, Brevan Howard, Barclays Capital, Citigroup, J.P. Morgan, own calculations.

rates, and so forth). Almost invariably, empirical evidence discredits such a hypothesis; naive yield-seeking seems to add value in the long run. In other words, on average over time and across strategies, simple and popular academic theories are wrong and the yield hogs are right. Chapter 22 delves deeper into this topic.

Recall that positive carry is agnostic as to whether high predictable return reflects rational risk premium or investor irrationality. Bacchetta-Mertens-van Wincoop (2009) explore consensus forecast data in several markets to decompose yield spreads into survey-based risk premia and expected rate changes; they find that predictability of excess returns is closely tied to systematic errors in investors' exchange rate forecasts and virtually unrelated to survey-based risk premia.

Figure 13.9 shows that carry strategies in several asset classes earned persistent profits between 1993 and 2007 and lost a bundle in 2008. Besides the two FX carry strategies, I show a cross-country fixed income carry strategy (go long duration in countries with steep yield curves, short duration in countries with inverted or flat yield curves) as well as a credit carry strategy (buy various credit-risky products and sell Treasuries short). Over the full sample, the emerging market FX strategy and cross-country FI strategies achieved SRs near 1, the G10 FX strategy 0.7, and the credit carry strategy only 0.3.

A volatility-weighted composite carry portfolio benefited from diversification across strategies and earned an SR of 1.1. Some carry traders venture into less liquid assets (municipal curve steepness, credit curve steepness, distressed debt, etc.), while others broaden the trades to include "cousins" of the carry strategy: stock or country selection based on dividend yields or other valuation ratios as well as volatility selling (discussed in Chapters 12 and 15). Also recall from Figure 11.8 how "positive carry" commodities (those with a backwardated term structure) outperform in the long run. However, greater diversification among yield-seeking strategies crystallizes—rather than diversifies away—systematic carry exposure: sharp losses tend to become concentrated in bad times. For example, my composite of four carry strategies lost half its value between the peak in 2007 and the trough in 2008, before recouping most of the losses in 2009. The best diversification to the generic carry trade comes from anti-risk positions or from trend-following strategies. The former tend to have poor long-run returns; they are a more expensive way to diversify but can be very valuable as tactical investments.

One interesting question is why certain carry strategies have performed much better than others over the past two decades. The contrast between the empirical success of cross-country carry trading and lesser long-run profits in within-country carry trading—notably in credits or securitized debt—is striking. I consider the convenience of carry trading and the degree of systematic risk as alternative explanations:

- One reason for the poor performance of credit carry is the headwind nature of the sample period (with some net widening of credit spreads between December 1992 and December 2009). Even if we study performance histories ending before the 2007–2008 Credit Crunch, however, long-run excess returns to credit carry are modest. I conjecture that corporate bonds have been the obvious easy way to chase yield, and as a result have tended to be structurally overpriced. Although the currency carry strategy did become overcrowded a few years ago, it was evident for a long time that less speculative capital was allocated to cross-country trading than to within-country opportunities.
- An alternative hypothesis is that some carry strategies have more toxic features than others, notably a greater propensity to experience large losses during "bad times". Both the credit carry strategy and currency carry strategy seem to share this empirical characteristic (unlike, say, government bond carry strategies). There seems to be little difference between the two to justify their very different long-run success. However, it may be relevant that the same credit strategies that offer the highest long-run SRs—buying short-dated top-rated corporates and selling Treasuries short—also have the highest ratios of systematic risk to diversifiable risk.

Finally, diversification helps—most of the time. Implementing carry style strategies in many different markets improves diversification and therefore risk-adjusted returns, though perhaps not as much as in the past. Correlation and skewness due to technical ("positioning") factors may arise when many multi-strategy investors apply similar strategies, resulting in overcrowding and synchronous attempts to rush to the exit when liquidity conditions deteriorate. Carry strategies are so "obvious" that they may be especially prone to fail as diversifiers during systemic events. It may be more useful to diversify into other active indicators and into other asset classes, and, especially, to identify opposite trading styles. For systematic traders, the returns of momentum-oriented strategies tend to be lowly or even negatively correlated with carry and value returns. For discretionary traders, being long carry strategies is not a good diversifier for existing risk-seeking positions (such as being long the equity market) but can diversify existing directional anti-risk positions (long Treasuries).

# 13.6 NOTES

[1] Investors can implement the carry strategy in many ways. A common way is to buy the AUD/JPY one-week forward contract instead of spot. Arbitrage activities ensure that (in normal times) the ratio of forward and spot exchange rates—the forward premium—is proportional to the cross-country interest rate differential. Thus, the profit and loss of the trade reflects the (locked-in) one-week interest rate differential plus the (uncertain) spot exchange rate change over the next week.

[2] Academics had already uncovered forward rate bias by the late 1970s, soon after the breakup of the Bretton Woods fixed-exchange-rate regime. Empirical studies showed that, contrary to the UIP, forward exchange rates were not unbiased predictors of future spot rates, so that yield-seeking strategies were surprisingly profitable. Academics debated alternative explanations for this "forward premium puzzle": rational time-varying currency risk premia, some form of investor irrationality, or a "peso problem" (some probability of a currency devaluation implied in the yield although no such devaluation had occurred in the past—see Chapter 7). No consensus emerged from the early debate. Academic interest in carry strategies revived in recent years, notably with new risk-based explanations that stressed the similarity of carry trading and selling financial catastrophe insurance.

[3] These are self-financed long-short strategies so I naturally report excess returns over cash interest income. The total return would also include any investor's local currency interest income. The denominator is the absolute dollar size of long (or short) positions. Given the average deposit rate of  $5.5\%$  over the time sample in the U.S., total return for a dollar-based investor would have been roughly  $11.5\%$ . For Japanese investors total return would be only  $8.5\%$ , given the lower average yen deposit rate of  $2.5\%$ .

[4] The costs of many false alarms would have been overwhelmed by the savings from avoiding carry crashes in autumn 1992, early 1995, and Octobers 1998 and 2008. However, taking a multi-month horizon changes the sign of this regularity because risk aversion episodes tend to be short-lived.

[5] Such risk aversion indicators frustrated investors for almost a decade because, with hindsight, it would have been better to stick with consistent carry positions. After 1998 there were no major carry crashes until summer 2007, and there were positive gains each year, despite short-lived drawdowns. Predictably, inflows followed this good performance and by 2005 experienced traders worried about overvaluations and overcrowdedness in high-yielding markets. By this time the markets' main interest had shifted to even higher yielding emerging market currencies. Many professional currency managers shunned carry strategies too early—and paid the price by losing assets under management before this anti-carry stance paid off. In 2008, when it really mattered, carry-timing indicators correctly kept flashing red through the year.

[6] This pattern echoes the catastrophe insurance literature: abundant capital makes insurance cheap after good times whereas erosion of capital after an adverse event raises the insurance cost or premium (closing the barn door after the horses have bolted). Limited memory or attention may contribute to this pattern. Such evidence favors a contrarian insurance strategy—buy crash insurance when it is cheap, not just because it is more affordable at such times but also because it is more likely to be useful then. Implied negative skew on the carry trade became even more pronounced after the carry crashes, first hitting record levels in August 2007 and then again in October 2008.

[7] The generic proxy role should work better in cases where a fall in an asset's price influences both its carry and valuation (e.g., a steeper curve indicates both better carry and value for long-term Treasuries, and so does a wider credit spread for corporate bonds). In contrast, a currency carry signal may be negatively related to an exchange rate valuation signal.

Source notes. The academic literature on currency carry had already begun by around 1980 but the renaissance occurred in recent years with notable papers by Lustig-Verdelhan (2007), Brunnermeier-Nagel-Pedersen (2008), Farhi et al. (2009), Jurek (2009), and Burnside et al. (2010a). The volatility link has been explored in Christiansen-Ranaldo-Söderlind (2010), among others.

Among irrational explanations, see Froot-Thaler (1990), Jylhä-Lyytinen-Suominen (2008), and Burnside et al. (2010b) on the delayed reaction story. Among practitioner work, see Bilson (1993) and Nordvig (2007). On other predictors than carry, see Ilmanen-Sayood (2002), Yilmaz (2009), and Ang-Chen (2010). On carry strategies in various asset classes, see Cochrane (1999), Asness-Moskowitz-Pedersen (2009), and Bacchetta-Mertens-van Wincoop (2009).

# 14

# Commodity momentum and trend following

- Many financial return series exhibit positive short-term momentum. Buying assets when their prices have risen and selling assets when their prices have fallen is profitable in many asset classes, including commodities.
- Momentum strategies can be traded on a single asset (trend following) or across assets (long-short trading). One can use many types of momentum signals (past returns, moving averages, breakout, consistency, etc.) and various lookback window lengths (to capture shorter and longer trends).
- For single commodities, the SR of trend-following strategies is typically between 0.0 and 0.5. For a diversified portfolio of them, the SR is between 0.5 and 1.0—at the higher end if volatility weighting is used. However, actual CTAs (commodity trading advisors) rarely have as good SRs as these simulated strategies.
- Momentum patterns likely reflect behavioral factors—investor underreaction to news and overreaction to recent returns (extrapolating past returns). Rational risk-based explanations are less compelling, but I highlight a link between commodity inventories and momentum.
- Trend-following and momentum strategies have been exceptionally good diversifiers to risky assets, performing well amidst equity meltdowns and rising volatility.
- Momentum strategies involve greater turnover than active carry and value strategies. Thus they are profitable only to those able to trade with very low transaction costs.
- Buying past winners using 3-to-12-month lookback windows tends to be profitable for most assets, not just commodities. However, reversal patterns sometimes dominate when shorter or longer windows are used (notably, in stock selection).
- Trend following with many assets boosts risk-adjusted returns, and combining momentum with value, carry, or risky asset strategies improves diversification further.

# 14.1 INTRODUCTION

Besides value and carry strategies, momentum or trend is the third popular strategy style (see cube). Momentum style strategies buy recent winners and sell recent losers. This approach can be implemented on single assets using only their own past history—trend following or on a cross-section of assets—by creating long-short momentum portfolios based on relative recent performance. Two simple examples: A trend-following strategy involves buying oil futures if recent performance is positive and selling if it is negative; the sign and size of the trading position could be based on past quarter returns, or on the difference between the current price and its 200-day moving average, or on some other metric. A momentum strategy among, say, ten commodities involves buying the three with the best recent return while selling short the three with the worst recent return [1].

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/06cf463466f4f479bfec8618eac3a2ebc82c3bcdbabf952dd59ef56b53b54549.jpg)

"Chasing returns" has a bad name. Yet it turns out that—using suitable window lengths to measure recent returns (3-12 months)—momentum style strategies have been historically successful in almost any asset class researchers and traders have looked into. Moreover, they may be exceptional diversifiers for risky asset portfolios. There are of course pitfalls and inconsistencies. Perhaps the main problem in return chasing is that investors seem unduly influenced by multi-year returns—herding into successful asset classes and strategies when they already have enjoyed a long uptrend and are more likely to experience a reversal. Statistical evidence suggests that many financial asset returns exhibit positive short-term autocorrelation (momentum bias) and negative long-term autocorrelation (mean reversion bias), as Exhibit 20.1 shows.

I focus here on momentum strategies in one asset class—commodities—but will briefly discuss evidence on other asset classes in the last section of this chapter.

Momentum vs. reversal. Published studies use different portfolio formation periods (window lengths to measure historical returns) and different holding periods, but they all focus on past returns. The common empirical finding is a return continuation tendency (momentum bias) except for very short and very long windows (a week or shorter, two to five years) where reversal biases may dominate.

# 14.2 PERFORMANCE OF SIMPLE COMMODITY MOMENTUM STRATEGIES

Several recent studies have presented evidence on commodity market momentum (see "Source notes" at the end of the chapter). All of the studies use the nearby futures contract as the proxy for the spot price, all include both roll return and spot return but do not estimate trading costs, and all ranking models diversify across a few commodities held long and a few held short. Most studies use monthly data. None of the academic papers adjusts momentum signals or position sizes for volatility, even though natural gas has three times higher volatility than gold (48\% vs. 16\%). The studies mainly differ in their historical data sample and in the details of their momentum strategy specifications.

Does it matter whether we employ single-asset trend-following strategies or multi-asset long-short strategies? A composite of trend-following strategies often closely resembles a momentum portfolio but there are differences. The former can be tailor-made to different assets; the latter can neutralize a common directional risk across assets. Both customization and hedging features can have positive and negative effects on performance. Net benefit can only be determined empirically.

Erb-Harvey (2006) show that for their sample period, December 1982 to May 2004, a strategy of buying each month the four best performing commodities during the past 12 months and selling short the four worst performing ones gives an SR of 0.55. They find an even higher SR of 0.85 if they implement a more diversified trend-following strategy: going long all positive-return commodities and shorting all negative-return commodities. Others study a variety of momentum specifications over even longer sample periods and find that long-short models that exploit relative return continuation within a one-year window perform consistently well and that the best profits have been attained using short windows (1-to-3-month return histories and/or holding periods). There is also some evidence of long-term and short-term mean reversion: high past returns one to five years ago or during the past week predict low future returns.

My data analysis below uses a shorter and more recent sample period (1990-2009) but agrees with published research. Analyzing a composite of monthly trend-following strategies for timing individual commodities and a cross-sectional long-short portfolio based on ranking commodities on their recent return, I find SRs very similar to those found by Erb-Harvey (2006): near 0.8 for the timing composite and 0.5 for the ranking composite. Timing models also allow directional commodity risk taking, say, if all commodities are trending down (1998 and 2008) or up (2003 and 2007) at the same time. During a sample period when all commodities were often trending synchronously, timing models understandably fare better. As shown below, trend-following models were profitable for most single commodities.

Most academic studies use past average returns as trading signals. Trend-followers often use a variety of measures other than return (listed below). My experience is that none of these variants systematically outperforms the others:

- The most common trend-following rules are based on moving averages where current market price is compared with an average of historical prices over some window. If current price is above (below) the moving average—or, perhaps, the moving average plus or minus a small band around it to avoid whipsaw trading—the rule gives a buy (sell) signal. When thinking about the difference between a simple return and a moving average signal, I like the following intuition: we know that current market price is the most relevant data point but we do not know whether the most appropriate comparison is the price a week, month, quarter, or year ago; thus it makes sense to dilute the choice of window starting point by taking a moving average. If momentum is defined by the ratio of current price to moving average (minus one), it corresponds to a weighted average of past one-period returns with declining weights as you move from the present toward the past.
- Some momentum signals ("moving average cross-over") also smooth the current observation with a short moving average. Others use exponentially weighted averages, further raising the relative weight of recent observations.
- Acceleration or breakout signals and filter rules focus even more on the distinction between recent price moves and preceding market action. These rules care most about signal strength—sharp market moves are viewed as a stronger signal than small moves.
- In contrast, consistency and relative strength indicators ignore and/or underweight the magnitude of market moves; instead, they emphasize the frequency of up moves or down moves.
- In addition to entry rules, there may be specific exit rules (specific stop-loss or take-profit rules). Alternatively, exit takes place when a new entry signal tells the trader to close or even reverse a position. Whipsaw trading may be reduced by adding a nottrading band around the signal level.

Trend-followers typically diversify not just across a large number of liquid assets but also across a range of history window lengths and trading frequencies. Slow-moving multi-month trends, fast-moving multi-day trends, and even within-day high-frequency data trends may be exploited. Trading costs are naturally a greater concern with faster moving signals. The empirical success of strategies with different window lengths has varied over time. It is rare that one specification is inherently superior, although some new evidence points to the best information being in returns from 6 to 12 months ago in several asset classes. Trend-followers often optimize or calibrate their models dynamically, based on the recent performance of each model specification. Even if some performance persistence across specifications exists, there is an obvious danger of overcrowding when many trend-followers make similar decisions using the same dataset. Whatever window lengths are used, most trend-followers rebalance their positions much more frequently than once a month.

Volatility weighting. One key decision is whether to adjust signals and/or position sizes for different volatilities across assets or changing volatility over time. There is some evidence that volatility targeting or mean variance optimization (whose main benefit may be volatility weighting) enhances the performance of commodity momentum strategies. My own examples apply volatility weighting but this has so far rarely been done in published studies:

- If return signals are not adjusted for volatility, natural gas (which is highly volatile) is much more likely to be at either end of the rankings than gold (which is three to four times less volatile). This bias effectively reduces the breadth of a given commodity universe.
- If position sizes are not adjusted for differences in volatility across commodities, the long-short positions in ranking models can be quite market directional. A dollar-neutral long natural gas vs. short gold position is not empirically neutral to generally changing commodity prices; it effectively has a long directional bias.
- Volatility weighting of positions also reduces nominal position sizes when recent overall volatility is higher. Such volatility stabilization and/or targeting over time appears to mildly enhance risk-adjusted returns.
- An extreme form of volatility weighting is to stop trend following after high recent volatility. Yet another approach is to let the specifics of the historical window (relative weights of fast and slow signals) be a function of volatility level. A related idea is to favor slow signals during bull markets and fast signals during bear markets.

Figure 14.1 plots the cumulative profits of a basket of commodity trend-following strategies. I use the commodity subset of the Mount Lucas Management trend-following index (MLMCCO in Bloomberg) for the long-term perspective and my own analyses for the past 20 years: trend following in three energy, three metals, and three agriculture futures. We go either long or short one unit of the nearby futures contract of each commodity for the next month depending on whether its current value is above or below the past 12 months' moving average. Even with such a simple approach, long-run performance is quite attractive:  $11\%$  average excess return at  $14\%$  volatility and an SR of 0.8—admittedly before any trading costs or fees are deducted. (Excess returns include both spot returns and roll returns but not riskless interest income.)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/85b4a49bfb67b13bf02dd79020e77cb1ad484d4e4452722382f72189fcac3294.jpg)

Figure 14.1. Cumulative excess returns of commodity trend-following strategies Sources: Bloomberg, Mount Lucas Management, Brevan Howard, own calculations.

Performance gaps over the past 20 years are worth exploring, however. The profits of the MLMCCO have tailed off, while my variants have remained profitable in the 2000s. It makes sense that the last variant (top line) fares best because it uses a simple volatility-weighting rule that improves diversification (as it makes each commodity position's volatility roughly equal) and slightly boosts returns (because reducing position sizes after high volatility tends to help). However, I cannot fully account for the performance difference between the low and middle lines [2].

Table 14.1 shows the results for each commodity. Again, a simple rule uses just the sign of current price minus the 12-month moving average to determine whether to go long or short a commodity for the next month. Trend following has worked best in the energy sector and for wheat, and it has really lost money only for one commodity (silver). In the simple composite, natural gas is allowed to have a much higher effective weight than gold (because of its higher volatility). Only in the weighted composite (last row) are position sizes adjusted dynamically, based on past year volatility. (Aside: The large gaps between arithmetic and geometric means are due to high volatility, but diversified composites have lower volatility and thus narrower gaps.)

How about other data windows and other momentum indicators? The patterns are robust. Volatility weighting boosts the SR by 0.1-0.3 in all of the variants I try. The composite strategies' Sharpe ratios are between 0.5 and 1.0 for moving average windows of 3 months, 12 months, and anything between. I also get broadly similar performance if
