I use past average returns or consistency or breakout measures. The results are slightly better for the 12-month window than for shorter windows. In the same vein, I find that shifting from monthly trading frequency to weekly trading frequency, or delaying the implementation by a day or two, has little impact on average returns even before costs. If I incorporate trading costs, slow-moving signals look even better because they imply lower turnover. Such a win-win outcome is convenient but does not hold for all asset classes and may not hold for commodities in the future.

# 14.3 TWEAKS

Where (in which asset subsets) does momentum or trend following work especially well? This evidence comes mainly from outside commodities but may apply well in commodities too:

- Less liquid assets that exhibit high information uncertainty are more likely to trend because "information travels slowly"—prices only slowly incorporate new information (i.e., there is an underreaction effect) and these assets are often harder to arbitrage. Such patterns may not be exploitable in practice, however. Thus, most trend-followers focus on highly liquid assets such as futures or currencies, to save trading costs.
- Investor reluctance to realize small losses (disposition effect) exacerbates momentum effects. Thus momentum and underreaction effects are related to the difference between current price and the price at which most investors' bought ("capital gains overhang").
- Poorly anchored asset prices allow greater trending, perhaps because they give more scope for sentiment-driven changes. For example, a lack of clear fair value anchors may have allowed energy prices to trend strongly in the past decade. A related point is that trends are less likely in the relationship between similar assets (good substitutes).

When (in which regimes or conditions) does momentum or trend following work especially well?

- Almost tautologically, momentum works better amidst sustained price moves and suffers in range-bound markets. Macro-trend reversals are the worst enemy as they influence many assets simultaneously.
- It is perhaps ironic that, unlike many financial assets and strategies, trend-following strategy return is the rare exception that does not exhibit positive short-term momentum.
- Seasonally, momentum and trend-following strategies tend to perform better in December and less well in January—in stark contrast to many carry and risk-seeking strategies (see Chapter 25). For example, my active commodity momentum strategies had the highest average excess return,  $1.6\%$  in December, compared with only  $0.4\%$  for January. High December returns are typically explained by year end tax loss selling and window dressing—institutions buying (selling) assets that outperformed (underperformed)—during the year so as to make their portfolio look better at the snapshot in time it is revealed in reports to investors.
- Momentum and trend-following strategies tend to perform better when volatility is rising. While the contemporaneous correlation with volatility changes is positive, these strategies tend to perform better following periods of low volatility. (The converse is true for reversal strategies: they perform better after high volatility levels and during falling volatility.) These patterns may reflect the long-volatility nature of momentum strategies (to be discussed below), together with empirical mean reversion in volatility levels.
- Momentum strategies tend to work well when risky asset prices suffer and liquidity dries up. The commodity momentum strategy was up in nine of the ten worst down months for the S&P 500 since 1990.
- Momentum and trend-following strategies tend to perform especially well in the runup to predictable "events" such as economic announcements. I have documented abnormally high Sharpe ratios for trend following in bonds in the week ahead of payroll reports (see Ilmanen-Byrne, 2003). I find a similar pattern for oil prices ahead of OPEC meetings and for agricultural commodities ahead of pre-scheduled major announcements about supply-demand conditions. My story is twofold: (1) traders become more cautious (risk averse) ahead of such announcements, justifiably so because these events tend to trigger large market moves and high volatility, and (2) trader response is asymmetric depending on their recent success; traders on the wrong side of the recent trend ("losers") are more likely to capitulate, thereby accentuating the recent trend in the days before the big event.

# 14.4 WHY DOES MOMENTUM—SUCH A NAIVE STRATEGY—WORK?

As always, strategy profits can be explained by rational or irrational stories. For momentum, the irrational stories ring more true, so I start with them. Several well-known behavioral models have been designed to explain the momentum and reversal effects observed in equity markets (see Section 6.3.2), and the same ideas may apply to commodities as well. Investor underreaction to fundamental news almost surely con tributes to the momentum effect but cannot explain long-term reversal. Underreaction may reflect conservatism, overconfidence regarding one's private views, or the tendency to avoid realizing losses and to take profits too soon. Extrapolation of past returns and overreaction to popular stories can explain both the momentum effect and subsequent return to fundamental value. (Herding and other social dynamics may reinforce these patterns, and even rational traders may want to jump on the bandwagon for a while.)

Rational risk premium stories may be based on broader asset-pricing models, such as time-varying risk premia related to business cycle conditions, but the empirical relations are weak. The theoretical models specific to commodities are the hedging pressure hypothesis and the theory of storage. Hedging pressure explanations do not seem to get much empirical support; certainly Commodity Futures Trading Commission data on hedger or speculator positioning have limited ability to predict future commodity returns. The theory of storage is more promising. This theory highlights the role of inventories, especially for commodities that are difficult and/or costly to store. Such commodities tend to have steeply backwardated term structures (the futures price is well below the spot price) due to high convenience yield of holding spot positions (inventories) that help prevent production disruptions, etc. Convenience yield may also vary over time and be highest when the commodity is scarce (low inventories), resulting in an exceptionally high spot price and backwardated term structure (positive roll return).

Many empirical studies show that roll returns and momentum profits are positively correlated. For example, commodities held long in momentum portfolios tend to have a backwardated term structure and commodities held short tend to have contangoed term structure. Gorton-Hayashi-Rouwenhorst (2007) take the interpretation one step further and argue that both roll and momentum portfolio profits are proxies for inventory effects; thus, the reasoning goes, the theory of storage can explain roll and momentum effects. Backwardation in a commodity price term structure and high prior commodity returns tend to predict low inventories and high future returns. Those high returns are presumably compensation for associated higher volatility—because low inventories make prices, rather than changes in inventory, the primary shock absorber to demand and supply news. Due to the uneven quality and relevance of inventory data (also published with lags and subject to data revisions), roll and carry data as well as momentum can be better proxies of "true" inventories than reported inventory levels and/or changes themselves. These proxies certainly predict future returns better but even normalized inventory levels have some predictive ability. Studying the performance of three long-short trading strategies between 1969 and 2006, Gorton-Hayashi-Rouwenhorst report average excess returns (shown with  $t$ -statistics) of  $13.4\%$  (4.9) for momentum,  $10.2\%$  (3.7) for roll, and  $8.1\%$  (3.2) for inventories.

Other rational stories for observed predictability patterns include data mining, investor learning, and a peso problem (unrepresentative sample). The strong performance of energy hints at the last explanation. It is puzzling that, among all commodity sectors, energy—which offers the best diversification against traditional asset classes and the best inflation-hedging ability—has also tended to offer the highest ex ante and ex post returns. At least a partial explanation for this "too-good-to-be-true outcome" is the benign sample period when increasing scarcity and growing demand (especially from China) kept surprising the markets about supply-demand fundamentals. Thus typical backwardation in the oil term structure may really have reflected expectations of gradual price declines or the hedging policies of oil companies, notwithstanding the trend toward higher prices.

These rational stories may have contributed to the observed momentum patterns but irrational explanations are much more convincing. One reason is that in momentum strategies long and short assets vary so fast that it is hard to come up with rational stories that could justify such frequent sign changes in risk premia, whereas naive extrapolation can easily justify the patterns observed.

Moreover, it is hard to argue that momentum strategies deserve a high rationally required risk premium when these strategies actually are great diversifiers. During financial crises such as 1998 and 2008, these strategies were among the few solid performers. Many empirical studies show that momentum strategies and CTAs (managed futures funds that focus on trend following) have negative or low correlations with equity markets and with value, carry, and reversal strategies. These other strategies tend to be empirically "short volatility" whereas momentum strategies and CTAs tend to be empirically "long volatility":

- The payoff profile of trend-followers resembles that of an unhedged long straddle position. Trend-followers are "long volatility" by gaining from large market moves in either direction, not by directly benefiting from volatility increases as a delta-hedged straddle position would. Using options jargon, trend-followers are long gamma—not long vega.
- Typically, long volatility strategies have an insurance characteristic that justifies low expected returns. This observation makes the high historical returns of momentum and trend-following strategies even more puzzling.
- Trend following is also closely related to stop-loss rules ("cut your losses and let your profits run") which many traders have found useful counterweights to the behavioral tendency to hang on to losers.

# 14.5 MOMENTUM IN OTHER ASSET CLASSES

Momentum strategies perform well in virtually all asset classes we study. Their ubiquity is illustrated in the title of the Asness-Moskowitz-Pedersen (2009) article, "Value and momentum everywhere" and in the finding of Moskowitz-Ooi-Pedersen (2010) that a simple trend-following rule appears profitable for each of the 58 liquid investments they examine.

The best known momentum pattern involves the relative performance of individual equities:

- Stock selection. Equities' dependence on past performance exhibits a strong multi-month momentum effect sandwiched between short-term and long-term reversal effects. "Recent winners" (best performing stocks over the past year) tend to subsequently outperform "recent losers" by roughly  $1\%$  per month.
- Industries and countries. Momentum partly reflects stock-specific dynamics and partly industry dynamics. Unlike stock-specific momentum, industry momentum also works over very short horizons. Trend-following strategies using equity index futures are profitable in many countries. Momentum works for country allocation.

Looking beyond equities, I have already shown that momentum works in commodities. It also works in fixed income, foreign exchange, and alternative assets, as well as across asset classes. However, momentum patterns in these other asset classes do not always share the particular reversal/momentum/reversal patterns observed at different window lengths in equity selection (less than one month, one to 12 months, more than 12 months):

- Fixed income country allocation based on momentum fares least well. Indeed, relative reversal patterns dominate in country allocation among long-dated bonds. (This observation is consistent with carry trade evidence in Chapter 13.) Momentum gains are more obvious in directional trend-following positions within a country than in cross-country trades or other spread trades.
- $FX$ . Trend-following strategies used to be profitable in G10 markets from the 1970s to the 1990s but less so in the past decade. In contrast, trend-following or momentum strategies among emerging currencies have remained highly profitable. FX also illustrates the principle that the best window lengths can change over time (and can only be known with hindsight). In G10 FX, relatively short windows worked better in the 1990s, but in the 2000s trend-following based on short windows lost money while long windows worked.
- Alternatives. Many alternative assets besides commodities—real estate, hedge funds, private equity—exhibit return persistence. This persistence may reflect some of the same influences that are seen in traditional asset classes, but stale prices and return smoothing are also likely to have contributed.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/7ba88784fd0bc808cd667a6fc52a40a8a068acec3ddf3c72fa6eeb0fca186a2e.jpg) within and across asset classes smooths returns (I target  $10\%$  volatility for each asset class). For example, all four asset classes have respectable SRs between 0.7 and 1.0, but the composite has a volatility of only  $6\%$  and an impressive SR of 1.4.

Figure 14.2 shows how well a simple trend-following strategy performed in four asset classes. In each class I simulate the one-year moving average rule for about 15 liquid assets (the interest rate and bond futures are all within G10 markets; currencies and equity indices also include a handful of countries outside the G10). The approach is again deliberately naive except that I apply volatility weighting. Diversification both

Figure 14.2. Cumulative excess returns of trend-following strategies, 1993-2009. Sources: Bloomberg, Brevan Howard, own calculations.

Figure 14.3. Contrasting Sharpe ratios for trading strategies that buy past winners and sell recent losers based on recent and intermediate momentum (in four equity market contexts and in two others).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/5f96573db6fd9c4bccd0ea96f41d16abeef57dc42d45106364d7e58ca744cc6f.jpg)

Source: Novy-Marx (2009).

Note that the strategy returns I plot are self-financed, so they are already excess returns, but trading costs, fees, and implementation slippage have not been deducted. The impact of these costs is not large, however (turnover is moderate given the 12-month moving average window); hindsight and overfitting are more relevant concerns. The 12-month window worked better than either shorter or longer windows during the past decade. Still, it is puzzling that such simple strategies could deliver a long-run SR in excess of 1. It is worth noting that, while a few successful CTAs have achieved a long-run SR near 1, the actual trading performance of broad trend-follower indices reveals much lower SRs (below 0.5), despite reporting biases that may overstate such statistics.

Novy-Marx (2009) shows that, for momentum strategies in many asset classes, the most valuable predictive information is in past returns between 6 and 12 months ago—not in the most recent months (see Figure 14.3). This finding challenges the idea that momentum reflects positive short-term autocorrelation. In addition, there is a sharp dropoff in information in past returns beyond 12 months ago.

# Box 14.1. Takeaways from the vast literature on equity momentum

- As noted above, past performance dependence across equities includes a multimonth momentum pattern as well as short-term and long-term reversal patterns. On paper, the strongest result is that past month winners tend to strongly underperform past month losers over the subsequent month. This first-month reversal effect is sometimes attributed to market microstructure effects such as bid-ask bounce, or to price overreaction to firm-specific news, but the most compelling explanation is price concession caused by large trades. Liquidity providers facing low trading costs can exploit this pattern. Return reversals are even stronger over shorter horizons (day and week), for less liquid stocks, when using industry-adjusted returns, and amidst high volatility.
- Popular momentum indicators skip the most recent month to avoid being contaminated by the short-term reversal effect. For example, the momentum factor in Kenneth French's data library is based on the stock return from 12 months ago to one month ago.
- There is also a long-term reversal effect for 1-to-5-year lagged returns, often seen as the flip side of the first-year momentum effect (overreaction followed by a return to fair value).
- Gross momentum profits have been higher and more consistent than profits from other cross-sectional factors. However, turnover and trading costs can be much higher for momentum than for value strategies with slower moving signals. Using longer window lengths to produce momentum signals can mitigate this problem but doing so increases the danger that positions are still held after their long-term reversal has begun. The return series in Figure 14.4 would not be as steep and smooth as it was between the 1940s and the 1990s if trading costs were included.

Figure 14.4. Cumulative returns of momentum and reversal strategies in stock selection, 1927-2009.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/8fced0791e80fa67f8766db3e46b87d35f671d5c9d389f305ab9c7c11081dedc.jpg)

Source: Ken French's website.

- Past monthly returns exhibit an interesting periodicity pattern at quarterly and yearly cycles. When studying individual months for forming long–short momentum portfolios, the return 12 months ago has the strongest positive relation to the current month's return, followed by (comparable relations for) 6 months, 9 months, 24 months, 36 months, 48 months, and 60 months. Surprisingly, this phenomenon is not concentrated in earnings announcement months.
- Price momentum is closely related to earnings momentum (the observation that—as a result of many analysts recently revising their earnings forecasts upward—equity prices tend to predictably rise in the subsequent weeks and months). These patterns certainly are suggestive of investor underreaction. Interestingly, both effects have weakened since October 2000 when the SEC's Regulation FD (Fair Disclosure) required U.S. companies to disclose material information to all investors at the same time.
- Lead-lag effects across markets tend to flow from liquid core markets to less liquid periphery markets. Information travels slowly from large-cap stocks to neglected small caps, which are hard to value and hard to arbitrage. Trends form more easily in the absence of clear valuation anchors, and arbitrageurs find it harder to trade against perceived misvaluations if trading costs are high. Momentum profits are higher in volatile credit-risky (low-grade) firms and for stocks with high earnings estimate dispersion.
- Another interesting lead-lag relation is between the returns of economically correlated firms—a company and its customer or supplier firms. Given that this looks like an underreaction due to investor inattention, it is not surprising that the patterns are stronger for small stocks. Similar evidence is also found at the industry and country level.
- Momentum profits appear to be larger after market gains and when investors are optimistic. Positive sentiment can accentuate overconfidence among stock-pickers, which (with short-selling constraints) leads to upward momentum (and later reversals). Momentum profits are lower after market losses, turning points, and periods of heightened volatility (high market volatility or wide dispersion across equities).
- After decades of apparently consistent performance—although profitability eroded in the 2000s—equity momentum strategies suffered a huge drawdown in spring 2009 that echoed the 1932 turnaround. As financial markets moved away from the edge of the abyss and confidence recovered, previous year winners—typically conservative, quality, growth stocks—sharply underperformed past year losers—typically risky, junk, value stocks.

# 14.6 NOTES

[1] In contrast to technical analysts or chartists who analyze price patterns in a discretionary fashion, most trend-followers and momentum-traders follow purely systematic rule-based approaches.

[2] The correlation between monthly returns is 0.83 but since 1990 the MLMCCO has an SR of only 0.3 compared with my strategy's 0.6. My simulation results are virtually the same whether or not I include a one-day delay as MLM does. MLM apparently uses a broader set of commodities than the nine liquid futures I include, which could explain the difference. Perhaps MLM incorporates some costs and fees that I do not, but I estimate that trading costs should reduce the SR by 0.1 at most.

Source notes. Many authors have in recent years analyzed commodity momentum strategies: Erb-Harvey (2006), Ribeiro-Normand-Loeys (2006), Gorton-Hayashi

Rouwenhorst (2007), Miffre-Rallis (2007), Fuertes-Miffre-Rallis (2010), and Shen-Szakmary-Sharma (2010). For a broad survey, see Schneeweis-Kazemi-Spurgin (2008).

On momentum or trend-following strategies in broader contexts, see Asness-Liew-Stevens (1997), Griffin-Ji-Martin (2005), Bhojraj-Swaminathan (2006), Ribeiro-Loeys (2006), Blitz-van Vliet (2008), Pukthuanthong-Levich-Thomas (2007), Asness-Moskowitz-Pedersen (2009), and Moskowitz-Ooi-Pedersen (2010). The last two studies also review explanations of why these strategies work so well.

On momentum in stock selection, see Jegadeesh-Titman (1993, 2006), Grinblatt-Moskowitz (2004), Grinblatt-Han (2005), Sias (2007), Figelman (2007), Novy-Marx (2009), and Wang-Xu (2010). On lead-lag relations across economically related firms or countries, see Cohen-Frazzini (2008) and Rizova (2010).

# 15

# Volatility selling (on equity indices)

- Selling volatility using equity index options is the purest example of selling lottery tickets that pay off (to the buyer) in bad times. Such strategies deserve high risk premia because large losses (spiking volatility) tend to coincide with market crashes. Understanding this strategy is important even if one does not trade it. Many investors do trade related strategies, such as carry.
- The strategy's high SR over 20 years between the 1987 and 2008 crashes also reflects a peso problem—the absence of that rare large-loss event from the sample. A proximate reason for the strategy's long-run success is the persistent pattern that implied volatilities of index options tend to exceed realized volatilities by  $2\%$  to  $4\%$ .
- For single-stock options, the average gap between implied and realized volatilities is nearer to zero. This contrast is suggestive of a correlation risk premium rather than a variance (volatility) risk premium. Correlation trading—selling volatility on an equity index and buying volatility on its single-stock constituents—has been historically profitable but loses money when realized correlations spike up.
- In addition, covered-call-writing strategies, which are in the category of volatility selling strategies, have been profitable in the past but their performance partly reflects long equity market exposure and not just volatility risk exposure.
- The theoretical sign of skewness preferences can be debated but the market pricing of options across strike prices suggests that most investors prefer positive skewness: protective, out-of-the-money index puts ("crash protectors") and speculative OTM calls on single-stock options ("lottery tickets") are especially expensive.
- As always, both rational and irrational explanations are possible for any observed regularities.

# 15.1 INTRODUCTION

The last dynamic strategy style I describe is volatility selling (see cube). Volatility selling is a form of offering financial insurance (tail risk insurance) that warrants some long-run compensation. In particular, selling volatility on equity indices provided historically attractive payoffs for two decades from the crash of October 1987 until the fall of 2008—with high but misleading long-run Sharpe ratios. Selling volatility on other asset classes has also earned reasonably consistent profits, whereas it is less clear that selling volatility on individual equities is rewarded:

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/efec00dd68813bf459a2a399745d804c1471dd5d2d90275dd5b31331d746ee12.jpg)

- Index volatility selling is the archetype of "selling lottery tickets whose payoffs tend to materialize during bad times." Negative skewness (losses are rare but large) and the timing of losses justify a positive risk premium and explain why the Sharpe ratio overstates the attractiveness of such strategies.
- Volatility selling is obviously risky but can be a valid business strategy for investors who can really afford to provide such insurance. Most investors do not directly trade this archetype but they do trade its close cousins that come in many guises. Credit and FX carry strategies, various hedge fund strategies, the insurance business, and even the equity risk premium (based on rare-disaster motivation or simply on the limited liability feature in equities) are all related to volatility selling.
- While relatively few investors trade options, the simplicity and nonlinear payoffs of options can make them more informative about investor expectations and preferences than the prices of more bundled linear assets. We can especially learn about the pricing of higher moment risks (volatility, skew, kurtosis/fat tails/jumps, correlation) in option markets and try to extrapolate this knowledge to the pricing of other asset markets (see also Chapter 19).
- This chapter is a bit more technical than most of the rest of the book, while cutting corners in the eyes of some experts. Readers can also skip this chapter without loss of continuity.

# Trading options vs. trading volatility

Options are the classic example of derivatives with nonlinear payoffs (compared with the underlying asset), while futures are the classic example of derivatives with linear payoffs. Only capturing the upside (buying calls) or protecting from downside (buying puts) sounds appealing but these benefits come with a cost: you pay a premium that you can fully lose and that—in a large proportion of cases (depending on the strike price)—you actually expect to lose. In long option positions, buyers' losses materialize through the time decay of the paid option premium, akin to a negative carry. If "nothing happens" over the life of the option, it expires worthless. This cost may be offset by capital gains if "something happens" and the option ends in the money, and the option buyer can pocket the difference between the market price and strike price, minus the purchase price of the option:

- If investors buy only calls or puts, directional exposure is typically more important than volatility exposure. The payoff profile is asymmetric.
- A straddle position—long both at-the-money (ATM) calls and puts—has no directional bias and a symmetric payoff profile. A straddle is a type of long-volatility position, but it is not a pure bet on high volatility if it is bought and held. It will be profitable if the price of the underlying asset makes a large shift in either direction, but it can lose money despite high interim volatility if the underlying price ends near its starting level. A buy-and-hold straddle is a bet on a large price move—not on high volatility; in options jargon, gamma risk rather than vega risk.
- Purer volatility or variance exposure (i.e., exposure to vega risk) can be achieved via trading delta-hedged straddles or variance swaps. Both approaches capture the difference between implied volatility/variance (IV) and realized volatility/variance (RV) over the life of the contract.

I assume that the reader is familiar with basic option-pricing terminology. Here is a recap on option pricing using the replication argument: in the classic Black-Scholes-Merton (BSM) world [1] option traders can continuously delta-hedge the market-directional (delta) risk of their options by trading offsetting amounts of the underlying security, and thus create a synthetic risk-free asset. No-arbitrage arguments imply that the option should be priced so that the expected return of the delta-hedged option position equals the risk-free rate. This is the reason that derivative pricing is consistent with risk-neutral probabilities even when real-world investors may be risk averse. Specifically, risk-neutral pricing of a derivative off its underlying asset does not require assuming that real-world investors are risk neutral. Risk aversion does not matter if positions could be hedged. A standalone position in an equity option should earn a risk premium for its equity market risk, say based on its CAPM beta (positive for calls, negative for puts). But for the delta-hedged position, the betas of the option and the stock just offset each other, leaving a zero premium for a zero-beta position. This observation, together with assuming a particular asset-pricing model, allows analysts to switch between (i) real-world pricing where expected cash flows are discounted with an appropriate discount rate that includes a risk premium (which may be hard to define and measure) and (ii) risk-neutral pricing where expected cash flows are discounted with the riskless rate.

Delta hedging involves offsetting a call option's delta through dynamic stock trading in a way that eliminates directional exposure. Delta hedging a sold (short) option position is a "buy-high, sell-low" strategy that will be more costly the longer the absolute distance that the stock price moves during the life of the option (between discrete hedge rebalancings). Consequently, short option positions benefit from lower realized volatility. Under idealized BSM conditions, a delta-hedged short at-the-money straddle's return reflects the difference between contracted implied volatility and realized volatility over the option's life. In reality, market price moves are not continuous, hedging is costly and done discretely, and volatility is not constant—all of which make delta hedging imperfect. Therefore, delta hedging does not capture pure volatility risk as cleanly as variance swaps do.

A variance swap is a contract that pays the difference between the subsequent realized swap rate (based on actual variance) and the contracted or fixed swap rate (mainly based on the market's expectation of variance at the time the swap is entered into). A sold variance swap is profitable if future realized variance turns out lower than the contracted variance swap rate. By construction, variance swaps are not subject to various unintended risks to which delta-hedged straddles are exposed—gamma risk between discrete rebalancings, the possibility of jumps (model risk), and the impact of market frictions such as illiquidity and trading costs. Variance swaps have become popular in recent years but are not yet available for all assets.

# Interpreting implied volatilities

It is interesting for a book on expected returns that the famous Black-Scholes option-pricing formula does not contain expected returns. Under BSM assumptions, options deserve no risk premium beyond their equity market beta exposure, and delta-hedged positions deserve no premium, full stop. When BSM assumptions fail, however, the option may need to offer a (volatility) risk premium beyond that earned by its relation to the underlying. Delta-risky positions do warrant a risk premium proportional to their delta (and equity market beta, if the CAPM holds).

The cross-sectional implication of BSM assumptions (constant volatilities, no market frictions, etc.) is that all options across all strike prices should have the same implied volatility. Thus, real-world deviations from BSM assumptions should explain observed smile effects and other apparent violations of no-arbitrage pricing. (The smile refers to the appearance of a plot of implied volatility on the  $y$ -axis against strike prices or option moneyness on the  $x$ -axis, where moneyness is the degree to which an option is in the money.)

The BSM analysis taught investors to infer implied parameters such as implied volatility from market prices, always given a particular model or set of assumptions. In the risk-neutral world, implied volatility reflects the market's volatility expectations. In the real world, this implied parameter may also reflect some risk premia in addition to volatility expectations.

Thus, analogous to the way a term spread reflects the market's rate expectations plus required bond risk premia, implied volatilities also reflect the market's volatility expectations plus some volatility-related risk premia. Any decomposition requires a specified model and empirical estimation, and any resulting estimates are noisy. As a convenient simplification, it is common to interpret excess implied volatility over realized volatility as a volatility risk premium. Empirically, this approximation works well, at least for assessing historical average premia. If we recognize the time-varying nature of the volatility premium, careful estimation of the two components becomes important, preferably using more robust "model-free" approaches.

By market convention, implied volatilities are often quoted based on the B-S formula and reflect its underlying assumptions. Unequal B-S implied volatilities across strike prices (smile or smirk) may be interpreted as market recognition of prevalent non-normalities and other deviations from B-S assumptions. A symmetric volatility smile could be explained by fat tails in the real-world asset return distribution; while an asymmetric skew/smirk could be explained by greater downside volatility (the market's greater propensity to jump downwards rather than upwards). Since the 1987 equity market crash, the skew for equity index options has been so extreme that investor preferences likely have contributed to it. Either a rational risk premium (such as portfolio insurance preferences leading to greater risk aversion after down markets) or investors' irrational crash fears could explain the excess of skew implied in index option prices over that observed in realized market returns.

# 15.2 HISTORICAL PERFORMANCE OF VOLATILITY-TRADING STRATEGIES

Covered-call-writing vs. volatility/variance-selling strategies

Perhaps the most popular option strategy is covered call writing (CCW): holding a stock or a stock index and writing a call against it. Effectively, the call writer sacrifices some upside exposure in exchange for receiving option premium income, while maintaining the downside (except, of course, for the cushion from the premium income). For CCW using ATM calls on the S&P 500 index (where the overall position has a beta near 0.5), roughly half of the strategy's long-run return is related to directional risk (the equity risk premium) and half to the fact that implied volatility is typically higher than realized volatility (the volatility risk premium). Between 1994 and 2005, each component earned 24 bp per month, the latter component reflecting the  $2\%$  average IV-RV gap (see Figelman, 2008). For out-of-the-money (OTM) options written, the equity beta component is higher (more upside exposure is kept) and the volatility component is lower. There is some evidence that the strategy is more profitable if OTM options are used, perhaps due to a better capture of the equity premium and more expensive options. The strategy is more profitable if very-short-dated options are used (wider IV-RV gap, on average) and is likely to be less profitable if covered call writing is done on individual stocks (narrower IV-RV gap, on average).

CCW strategies performed extremely well during the long bull market since the 1980s, resulting in various products offered to institutional and retail investors. Figure 15.1 and Table 15.1 analyze the performance and risk statistics of various strategies. The BXM and BXY indices, produced by the Chicago Board of Exchange, simulate CCW strategies using ATM and OTM call options. BXM and BXY look especially attractive in historical Sharpe ratio terms (having a higher return and lower volatility than the S&P 500), partly because they truncate the right tail and make return distribution more negatively skewed. Another benchmark index, PUT (launched in 2007), simulates collateralized index put selling, again effectively giving up the upside on the equity index in exchange for receiving an option premium. Historically, these benchmark indices outperformed the S&P 500 on a volatility-adjusted basis, but their tail risk exposures became evident only in 2008.

CCW strategies are hardly diversifiers away from equity indices—their correlations are very high, indeed nearly perfect for down moves. CCW return volatility is mainly driven by equity returns (directional exposure), especially if done with OTM options. Absolute return seekers who look for less correlated return sources should prefer variance swaps that largely avoid directional exposure. Variance swap returns are mainly driven by the difference between implied and realized volatilities (squared). However, even though variance swaps are designed to be mathematically delta neutral, they exhibit empirical market directionality (a mild positive equity market beta) because falling equity markets tend to coincide with rising volatility. As noted above, delta-hedged straddles were the best (but noisy) way to buy or sell volatility until variance swap contracts were created and their OTC markets became liquid.

Traditionally, CCW has been more common than variance selling. However, in 2005-2007, several sell-side firms offered their customers structured products that were short volatility or variance, often simply by selling one-month or 3-month variance swaps on

the S&P 500. Excess return for such a product is proportional to the difference between squared implied volatility and squared realized volatility over the life of the contract. Backtested results were extremely impressive until 2007 but the losses in autumn 2008 were dramatic—and traumatic, prompting many investors to leave these strategies. The Merrill Lynch volatility arbitrage strategy, shown below, lost 12 years' gradually earned excess returns in less than two months. (All index volatility-selling strategies plummeted in autumn 2008, but leverage made the losses of this index exceptionally high.) Although implied volatilities were high, realized volatilities exceeded them, reaching levels only seen during the 1987 crash. Indeed, volatility-selling strategies have suffered serious losses mainly on these two occasions, but these losses have been extremely large and concentrated in the worst possible times.

Table 15.1 (based on weekly data) illustrates how 2008 "revealed" the riskiness of various option-trading strategies, while at the same time long-run performance statistics became less appealing. The downfall was even worse for the "pure" volatility arbitrage strategy than for covered-option-writing strategies. Over 1989-2009, the latter still display higher returns and Sharpe ratios than a simple long-equities strategy (first column), but this advantage comes with less appealing tail risk or higher moment exposures: more negative skewness and higher kurtosis. The covered-PUT-writing strategy comes with the best performance statistics and worst risk statistics.

A pioneering empirical study by Coval-Shumway (2001) shows that selling ATM straddles on equity index options offered a  $3\%$  weekly return in their sample. Even with  $19\%$  weekly volatility, the annualized SR was 1.2. There are four reasons for hugely positive (negative) returns to volatility selling (buying):

- high leverage and high volatility (typically higher annual volatility than annual return; in many weeks, option buyers lost  $100\%$ , but sometimes they made 10 times their outlay or more);
- beta risk and high non-obvious risks (double-digit betas but also correlation risk);
- a benign sample;
- high trading costs (bid-ask spreads are near  $5\%$  for an ATM straddle).

A more recent study by Broadie-Chernov-Johannes (2009) uses a longer sample period (1987-2005, including the 1987 crash) and documents massive negative average returns for being long equity index puts. Average monthly return is  $-30\%$  for ATM puts and  $-45\%$  to  $-57\%$  for OTM puts. Buying these options, you can expect to lose half your outlay every month. (These high percentages are on admittedly low initial outlays that imply levered exposure to an underlying asset.) The study argues that these large losses are not necessarily due to mispricing. Given high volatilities, annual Sharpe ratios are near  $-1$ , and many further arguments (see below) can make the real-world performance of such a strategy unappealing.

The proximate reason for the success of volatility-selling strategies is the pervasive gap between implied and realized volatilities (see Figure 15.2). The sign of the gap does occasionally flip, but the one-year average has almost always been positive and the 24-year average is near  $4\%$  (20\% vs. 16\%). Similarly consistent gaps exist for other major equity indices but not for single stocks.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/555b54fb9660be3d6127037379e90675154df94d30d0716a68af2db05a289130.jpg)

# 15.3 TWEAKS/REFINEMENTS

Certain types of volatility exposures appear better rewarded than others. I introduce correlation and skewness premia and return to these topics later in this chapter and in Chapter 19.

Correlation premium. Equity index option selling is much more profitable than individual equity option selling. The implied volatilities of equity indices trade consistently at a premium over realized volatilities, but the same is not true for single-stock options. This difference may be explained by the correlation risk premium. Traders may hedge index option sales with purchases of single-stock options in the same index basket. The resulting correlation or dispersion trades have attractive historical returns but they suffer when correlation across stocks rises—typically during crises and in volatile bear markets.

For readers unfamiliar with correlation trading, basic statistics may give helpful intuition. Recall that market volatility reflects weighted average volatility across stocks and the weighted average correlation between them. We can infer higher implied correlation when index option volatility rises faster than the average volatility of single-stock options constituting the index.

Given that a dispersion trading strategy can hedge away delta and vega risks (equity and volatility risks) and has a reasonably symmetric return distribution, correlation risk seems to be the main explanation for the richness of equity index options and thus the profitability of option selling. The correlation premium could be motivated by deterioration of investment opportunities when diversification becomes harder or by time-varying disagreement among investors. Buraschi-Kosowski-Trojani (2010) document a higher SR for correlation selling than for variance selling (roughly 3 vs. 1.5) over a 13-year sample period, which includes the autumn 2008 crisis, as well as smaller losses during crises. SRs may be overstated as they are based on estimated synthetic variance and correlation swap prices or mid-market quotes, which may differ from traded market prices. Also, Driessen-Maenhout-Vilkov (2009) report a higher SR ("only" 0.73) for the correlation strategy than for index volatility selling, as well as better skew and kurtosis characteristics. Limits-to-arbitrage arguments (trading costs, margin requirements, etc.) may prevent most investors from exploiting the correlation premium.

Skewness premium. OTM index put options have a larger gap between implied and realized volatilities than ATM options, they appear systematically expensive compared with various fair value metrics, and their historical average returns are particularly negative. Option traders already recognized in the 1970s that implied volatilities across strike prices exhibit a smile pattern—higher volatilities for OTM puts and calls than for ATM options. After the 1987 crash, a highly asymmetric smirk or skew replaced a symmetric smile for index options.

Single-stock option pricing provides further hints of lottery seeking. Ni (2006) estimates that OTM calls on individual equity options have had large negative average returns over 1996-2005: around  $-30\%$  per month based on mid-market (average of bid and ask) prices. They also underperformed calls with lower strike prices by a similar amount. High transaction costs and noisy price data on single-stock options may undermine these results, however.

Other types of refinements include timing volatility positions based on the size of ex ante opportunities and/or hedging volatility positions via selective long-short strategies. Timing overlays on volatility selling could include simple rules that have been effective in the past—say, do not sell volatility if current implied volatility is well below the "normal" (past average) implied, recent realized, or predicted volatility, where predicted volatility is based on a GARCH-type model or other forecasting model. Hedging may involve long-short strategies that sell volatility in markets where it is rich and buy volatility in markets where it is cheap.

It is not clear whether volatility selling is profitable for all single stocks, because many studies show no consistently positive IV-RV gap, unlike the situation that prevails with index options. However, Cao-Han (2009) show that covered call writing on high-volatility stocks earned a  $2\%$  higher monthly return (between 1996 and 2006) than covered call writing on low-volatility stocks. Moreover, delta-hedged long option positions tend to have negative average returns that are monotonically lower the higher the volatility of the underlying stock. This evidence is consistent with a volatility risk premium that is proportional to the level of asset volatility. Option selling is thus more profitable on more volatile stocks (which tend to be less liquid and more costly to trade), on stocks with a large IV-RV gap (expensive options), on stocks with large end-user demand, and especially profitable on past loser stocks, while exposure to market volatility risk is not important.

# 15.4 THE REASONS VOLATILITY SELLING IS PROFITABLE

# Should we expect to make money by option selling or option buying?

Taking a step back, it is not clear a priori whether systematic option buying or option selling is a better strategy. The answer depends on the empirical pricing of options—"Are they actuarially fair prices?" or "Do they provide an edge to buyers or sellers?" and ultimately on investor preferences. The intuitive idea that volatility sellers provide insurance and should receive some compensation seems correct. Returns on volatility-selling strategies exhibit negative skew and high kurtosis (fat tails), as frequent small gains are interspersed with rare large losses. Extensions of mean variance analysis to higher moments typically suggest that natural investor preferences are to like high average returns and positive skewness (first and third moments of return distribution) and to dislike high volatilities and kurtosis (second and fourth moments). Skewness preference and kurtosis aversion may thus make long options "expensive" and volatility selling generally profitable. Even without such preferences, finance theory suggests that volatility selling should carry a positive risk premium if its losses tend to coincide with equity market losses and other bad times.

A tendency for volatility selling to be profitable does not make it always profitable. Competitive forces will limit any systematic richness of options. Speculators will be attracted by the excess profitability of protection selling and yield-seeking investors will do more covered call writing. Moreover, Taleb (2004) argues for an opposite skewness preference bias that may have become more relevant as delegated fund managers control a larger share of global wealth. The case of liking negative skewness is more plausible for delegated managers whose performance-based compensation may induce them to seek volatility. Less scrupulous managers may also prefer a high probability of positive returns so that they can accumulate large fees before a rare disaster materializes, especially since overstated smoothness of returns can boost the Sharpe ratio and attract larger AUM until the blowup occurs.

# Main challenges and main explanations

The three best historical trading opportunities and thus the three main empirical challenges for theories to explain are (i) profits from index volatility selling or dispersion trading, (ii) richness of OTM index puts, and (iii) richness of OTM calls on single stocks. Here are some rational explanations and alternatives for each challenge:

(i) The persistent significant average difference between implied and realized volatilities (or variances), often defined as a volatility risk premium, may reflect a combination of

- compensation for systematic risk (volatility risk, and maybe also jump, skew, or correlation risk):
- price pressures from investor supply and/or demand;
- biased forecasts of realized volatility (a peso problem, an unrepresentative sample period, or learning amidst structural uncertainty).

Carr-Wu (2009) make the case for a systematic variance risk premium. They regress single-stock return variances on S&P 500 index variance to estimate variance betas and find that stocks with higher variance betas are associated with higher variance risk premia (a wider gap between implied and realized variances). Apparently, investors are willing to accept some losses in exchange for hedging away market volatility spikes. The variance risk premium may reflect (1) the empirical linkage between stock return and variance and/or (2) stochastic volatility as a distinct risk factor. Stock market variance tends to be higher during down markets; thus long (short) variance positions have a negative (positive) stock market beta. Because this beta risk can explain empirically only a small portion of observed profits from variance selling (variance risk premia), an independent variance risk factor seems to be the key contributor. This evidence is relatively weak; stronger evidence for a volatility premium comes from the finding that volatility selling is more profitable for stocks with high volatility.

However, the contrasting profitability of volatility selling in index options (very profitable) and in single-stock options (unprofitable) argues against a simple variance risk premium. Stated differently, the presence of a significantly positive gap between implied and realized volatilities for index options, and the absence thereof for single stocks, suggests that it is correlation risk rather than volatility risk that is priced. Other evidence in Chapter 19 about the ambiguous relation between expected returns and volatilities points to the same explanation.

From a theoretical perspective, the tendency for a strategy of selling volatility and/or correlation risk to lose money in bad times suggests that these risk premia should be negative (so that selling these risks is profitable in the long run).

(ii) In modeling terminology, the smirk in implied volatilities (higher Black-Scholes implied volatilities for OTM puts and lower implied volatilities for ITM puts than for ATM puts) corresponds to skewed risk-neutral distribution for index returns, in contrast to relatively symmetric realized return distribution. This finding suggests that a greater real-world frequency of downward jumps, or higher downside volatility due to higher downside correlations across stocks, is not the primary reason for option-pricing asymmetries. Main alternative explanations are analogous to those above:

- A risk premium for volatility, correlation, and/or skew exposure may reflect investor demand for downside protection. So-called portfolio insurance preferences imply greater risk aversion after down moves (wealth-dependent risk aversion), while crash-o-phobia refers to aversion specifically to crashes.
- Supply-demand imbalance due to an exaggerated need for downside protection may also have less rational causes, such as investors overestimating the likelihood of low-probability events or liking lottery tickets.
- A peso problem may be a partial explanation. No crashes of the magnitude of that of October 1987 have occurred since but the market has assigned a small probability to such an event. Some observers view this as an irrationally high crash expectation but one can also argue that realized outcomes between 1988 and 2007 were unrepresentatively benign. (A crash then occurred in 2008 that was roughly on the same scale as that of 1987 when monthly returns, rather than daily returns, are considered.) Was market pricing due to irrational memory or rational learning? Benzoni-Collin-Dufresne-Goldstein (2010) present a model that uses jumps and learning to explain the persistent richness of OTM index puts after the 1987 crash.

(iii) The richness of OTM calls on single stocks looks like a story of lottery ticket preferences. Ni (2006) estimates large negative average returns for a strategy of buying these calls, and finds that the strategy underperforms when compared with a strategy of buying lower strike calls. She argues that mispricing due to skewness preferences is the likely explanation. An equity premium explanation has the wrong sign (OTM calls have high equity betas that should imply high ex ante equity premia) and a peso problem explanation can be countered by the fact that the equity market trend was benign during the study's sample decade and the fact that there were more upside surprises than during previous decades. The findings are consistent with skew-seeking investors overpricing OTM calls that resemble lottery tickets. When calls are sorted each month into quintiles based on strike prices, skewness rises monotonically with strike price. When calls are sorted based on expected idiosyncratic skewness, the high-skew quintile has large negative returns  $(-21\%)$  per month while quintiles 1-3 earn mildly positive returns.

# Assessing irrational and rational explanations

Irrational explanations rely on (1) the demand from irrational investors (end-users) distorting option prices and (2) the inability of rational risk-neutral arbitrageurs or market-makers to offset these distortions. In contrast to the ideal BSM world, in the real world market-makers cannot perfectly hedge their option positions. Non-continuous pricing, jumps, stochastic volatility, and trading costs as well as capital constraints make market-makers sensitive to unhedgeable option risks and cause them to require compensation for bearing them. Since market-maker actions no longer offset end-user demand biases, it is important to assess what those biases are. New academic studies document many demand regularities that are consistent with observed pricing regularities:

- End-users tend to be net long index options and net short single-stock options. This pattern partly explains the richness of equity index options.
- End-users are most active in single-stock OTM calls and index OTM puts. Perhaps not surprisingly, both option types are shown to produce especially low future returns. Single-stock calls may be used both in covered call writing and in speculative trading. Index puts are primarily used as protection vehicles.
- End-user demand for index options exhibits a more pronounced skew than their demand for single-stock options. This is consistent with the exceptional richness of OTM index puts.
- More generally, there is a positive cross-sectional relation between option demand and option richness.
- Only a small portion of option market activity is related to volatility trading. Rather, options are used as a means to take directional positions—say, by facilitating short selling or leverage—or as a source of additional income.
- Poor institutional investor sentiment coincides with more negative skew for index options, consistent with greater protection demand. In contrast, good sentiment among individual investors coincides with higher demand for single-stock call options, especially lottery-like OTM options, making them more expensive.

Analysis of end-user demand does not reveal their motivations or rationality. Most studies conclude that irrational mispricing contributes importantly to observed option market regularities. The rational camp responds that risk stories can explain a surprisingly large part of observed returns without resorting to irrationality—and that various market frictions can make exploiting any remaining opportunities difficult.

Specifically, Broadie-Chernov-Johannes (2009) argue that options are often thought to be mispriced because the performance metrics that are used (Sharpe ratios and CAPM alphas) are ill-suited for option analysis, especially over short samples. After documenting the huge challenge for rational models—massively negative average returns for long index puts, losses of  $30\%$  per month, or worse, as noted earlier—they proceed to show that standard option-pricing models can largely explain these average returns. OTM puts are especially highly levered positions on the underlying index; during a period of high realized equity premium, OTM puts with large negative betas can be expected to have large negative returns. (Annualized returns are on the order of hundreds of percent, but so are annual volatilities.) Option returns are also sensitive to volatility: lower volatility reduces expected put returns (makes them more negative). Because this relation is nonlinear (concave), time-varying volatility further reduces expected put returns. These patterns, combined with the difficulty of estimating expected option returns (sampling uncertainty is exaggerated for nonlinear payoff distributions), imply that extremely negative average returns for OTM puts are not statistically different from those generated by the simple B-S model. Low average returns on ATM straddles (consisting of long position in both an ATM call and ATM put)—roughly  $-15\%$  per month—are more challenging to explain because these positions have zero equity market betas, so their returns cannot be explained by their levered directional exposure. Authors need to go beyond the B-S model (or a stochastic volatility model without risk premia) to explain ATM straddle returns. Incorporating a jump risk premium and estimation risk can generate double-digit negative expected returns and thus explain ATM option evidence without mispricing. Beyond these explanations, there are wide bid-ask spreads and other market frictions to consider.

# 15.5 OTHER ASSETS

Volatility selling appears to be profitable outside equity markets, but not as profitable (or risky) as with equity index options. Trolle-Schwartz (2008) study variance risk premia for energy commodities. Between 1996 and 2006 average implied volatility exceeded average realized volatility by  $4\%$  for crude oil and by  $3\%$  for natural gas. The SRs from shorting estimated variance swaps were 0.59 for oil and 0.35 for natural gas (compared with 1.02 for the equity index). Duarte-Longstaff-Yu (2007) document SRs ranging between 0.0 and 0.8 for strategies that short interest rate volatility.

# 15.6 NOTES

[1] I will credit Black-Scholes (B-S) and Black-Scholes-Merton (BSM) interchangeably because Robert Merton's role as one of the fathers of modern option pricing is well known. Separately, I will not make a distinction between variance selling and volatility selling, or between volatility premia and variance premia. Volatility typically refers to standard deviation (of returns). Variance is the square of volatility, and thus large market moves have an even greater impact on variance trading.

Source notes. There are many good textbooks on options and trader books on volatility. Practitioners with some background may like the book Volatility as an Asset Class (Nelken, 2008). On the performance of covered call writing, see Hill et al. (2006), Figelman (2008), and Cao-Han (2009). On other volatility trading, see Coval-Shumway (2001), Bondarenko (2007), and Carr-Wu (2009).

On explaining strategy profitability, see Han (2008), Lemmon-Ni (2008), Broadie-Chernov-Johannes (2009), Garleanu-Pedersen-Poteshman (2009), and Benzoni-Collin-Dufresne-Goldstein (2010). On refinements, see Foresi-Wu (2005), Ni (2006), Deng (2007), Buraschi-Trojani-Vedolin (2009), Driessen-Maenhout-Vilkov (2009), Buraschi-Kosowski-Trojani (2010). On volatility trading profits in other asset classes, see Duarte-Longstaff-Yu (2007) and Trolle-Schwartz (2008).

# 16

# Growth factor and growth premium

- The next four chapters discuss four underlying factors that I consider most important for determining long-run returns: growth, inflation, liquidity, and tail risks. All these factors tend to offer larger ex ante risk premia following adverse shocks.
- The growth factor is closest to the equity market factor and the equity premium. It captures general economic growth prospects but is also closely related to market risk aversion.
- Long-lived risky assets reflect both secular and cyclical growth prospects, neither of which is easy to predict.
- Overall economic growth sets some speed limits to aggregate profit growth although the profits-to-GDP ratio can make very large and long-lasting swings around its normal level. However, the speed limit that is relevant for stock investors is earnings-per-share growth—not aggregate earnings (profits) growth. The real trend rate of growth in earnings or dividends per share is only  $0\%$  to  $2\%$  over long periods, lagging the real GDP growth rate and aggregate profit growth rate because of dilution (the necessity for companies to issue more shares to raise the capital needed for growth).
- The link between equity market returns and economic growth is surprisingly tenuous. Even over long time windows, countries with high economic growth have not had particularly high equity returns. The predictive relation from growth to returns is even worse than the contemporaneous relation because abnormal growth appears mean-reverting. Buying the fastest growing countries, sectors, or stocks of the preceding 5 to 10 years is often a bad investment idea. One reason is excessive extrapolation and overpricing of growth.
- Stock market returns and yield curve steepness are the best known predictors of future economic growth.

# 16.1 INTRODUCTION TO UNDERLYING FACTORS IN CHAPTERS 16-19

The previous eight chapters (8-15) described a variety of expected return drivers—static asset class premia and dynamic trading strategies. It is often useful to shift thinking away from expected asset or strategy returns and instead focus on their underlying return drivers (factor risk premia). Each asset or strategy can be viewed as a bundle of relevant characteristics or factor sensitivities. For example, corporate bonds are exposed to interest rate, credit, and liquidity risks—and even more fundamentally to growth, inflation, and volatility risks. Data for estimating such underlying exposures do not arrive disentangled, but need to be extracted from asset market data with the help of statistical cross-sectional or time series analysis:

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/3d361ac6a84802382a868032e2534b01ce644edaf02811aaf7bdbed0b8f6712b.jpg)

- These underlying characteristics may be priced inconsistently across assets, leading to an opportunity to "arbitrage" them in cross-asset long-short trades, especially for HFs that can isolate, hedge, lever, and/or short exposures.
- Sometimes a given factor sensitivity or a characteristic appears to offer exceptionally high or low rewards in all assets exposed to it (whether due to a risk premium or to mispricing); this circumstance leads to an opportunity for factor timing or market timing.

What are the underlying risk factors? There is no uniquely correct answer but here are some useful perspectives. A single story is not enough, given the versatility of assets and their drivers. The factor choices are inherently subjective though guided by both theory and experience. Relevant underlying factors (1) give rise to common covariation across asset returns and (2) are "priced" (i.e., they influence expected asset returns). I deem the four most important factors growth, inflation, liquidity, and tail risks (where the last consists mainly of volatility, correlation, and skew) and discuss them in this chapter and Chapters 17-19 (see the new face of the rotated cube).

Table 16.1 shows correlations between asset and/or strategy returns and factor proxies between 1990 and 2009. For factor proxies, I use monthly change in the consensus forecast of next year real-GDP growth and next year inflation rate in the U.S., change in a Bank of England composite liquidity index (which I must extend from both ends with other proxies because the BoE series covers 1992-2008; see Appendix B), and change in equity index volatility (the VIX index).

I will use these underlying factors to analyze and explain the returns of 15 asset classes or strategy styles:

- Four traditional asset classes: developed market equities, emerging market equities, excess return of 10-year Treasuries over short-dated bills ("duration"), and excess return of IG corporates over duration-matched Treasuries ("credit").
- Four proxies for alternative assets: the S&P GSCI commodity futures index, the (admittedly non-tradable) HFR hedge fund index, a listed REITs index to proxy private real estate, and a listed PE firm index to proxy private equity investments.
- Excess returns on the four dynamic strategies I have just covered: global stock selection based on value (combining U.S. and non-U.S. value composites), G10 currency carry, commodity momentum, and equity index volatility selling. (Carry and momentum results would be broadly similar if I used the broader multi-asset-class carry and momentum proxies introduced in Sections 13.5 and 14.5, instead of specific indices.)
