- The three most prominent long-short strategy returns from Kenneth French's data library, proxying for size (SMB), value (VMG), and momentum (MOM) strategies in U.S. stock selection.

In Table 16.1, I sort the 15 assets or strategies based on their in-sample market beta (vs. the S&P 500, as shown in the rightmost column). The results make intuitive sense, which is especially gratifying when I relate asset returns to "pure fundamentals"—changing consensus views on GDP and CPI—instead of other financial market series. Risky equity assets have most growth exposure, while only trend/momentum, value, and duration are good at diversifying growth risk. Commodities benefit understandably most from rising inflation, while long Treasuries suffer the most. All risky assets suffer when market illiquidity or volatility escalates—volatility selling strategies and credit-vs.-

Treasury strategies most of all—while momentum-oriented strategies tend to serve as good diversifiers in such times as well.

I stress that the correlations shown are contemporaneous, unlike the predictive correlations shown in the timing models discussed in Chapters 8 through 10. The goal here is to explain the underlying determinants of various investments' monthly returns—not to predict them.

Correlation statistics are admittedly a little bland. To give more color, in each of the next four chapters I will show a bar chart that contrasts the average returns of these 15 assets or strategies when each factor is "high" or "low" (above or below its median). Such analysis can help identify each investment's role in the portfolio in terms of economic scenarios (e.g., revealing whether, and to what extent, nominal government bonds perform in their expected role as deflation hedges and commodity futures as inflation hedges).

Even though correlations between the four factors are not high, the way that assets or strategies rank, based on each factor, is surprisingly similar. Assets or strategies with high positive equity market correlations tend also to have high growth factor correlations, negative illiquidity correlations, and negative VIX correlations. Especially after the systemic shock of 2008, the single S&P factor can explain a lot of historical cross-asset variation. In more ordinary environments, a multi-factor perspective is essential. In any case, this analysis is just an appetizer. Multiple regressions or partial correlations are needed to tease out the distinct roles of the four underlying factors (see section "Combining factors" on p. 326).

# Other factor choices

This type of analysis on asset return drivers could be made extremely granular. Practitioner risk models may include a long list of risk factors for equities, another list tailor-made for bonds, maybe another for currencies, yet another for hedge funds, and so forth. Going to the other extreme, academics try to find one or a few common underlying risk factors that ideally apply to all assets—not just one asset class. The CAPM and the Consumption CAPM define risk as covariance with market return or with consumption growth, respectively. In between, multi-factor models include macro-economic factors (Chen-Roll-Ross, 1986 with factors related to growth and inflation), asset return factors (Fama and French's market, size, and value factors, expanded in Fama-French, 1993 with duration and credit factors to explain stock and bond returns), or purely statistical factors. Other academics add stochastic volatility, liquidity risk, investments, and human capital to their models. Purely statistical approaches, such as principal components analysis, identify important common return drivers without naming them; these statistical factors may then be loosely labeled by correlating them with observable factors.

Here, I take the middle ground and try to pick four underlying risk factors that can explain both conceptually and empirically much of the cross-sectional variation among assets and asset classes as well as time variation in expected returns. I doubt that rational risk factors can explain everything. My description of the nature of the four exposures is thus deliberately ambiguous. I do not know whether they are sensitivities to rationally priced systematic risk factors, on the one hand, or common key characteristics related to investor irrationality or learning about structural changes, on the other. I will focus below on risk-based explanations but also comment on alternative interpretations related to behavioral biases and learning.

Detour to the literature on characteristics vs. covariances. Irrationality stories argue that an asset's characteristics are what is priced: investors dislike value stocks, small caps, illiquid stocks, poor diversifiers, etc.—which makes these assets cheaper and gives them the potential for higher future returns. For example, a stock with a high book/price ratio is deemed a value stock by this characteristic. Standard finance models do not suggest that characteristics should determine assets' required returns; instead, their sensitivities to (or covariances with) common risk factors should determine them. Stocks with a given set of characteristics often have high sensitivities to shocks affecting a factor that corresponds to the characteristic (value factor, size factor, liquidity factor, or correlation factor, respectively). One aspect of a stock's riskiness is captured by its beta to the VMG factor (i.e., its typical covariation with excess return of value over growth stocks). In rational multi-factor models, a risk factor is priced if assets with higher sensitivities to it tend to earn higher long-run returns as a compensation for bearing factor risk. In general, these characteristics and covariances are positively related (high book/price stocks tend to move together and be positively related to the VMG factor), but empirical horse races suggest that the characteristic tends to carry a higher risk premium than covariance. Such evidence seems to favor irrationality stories but the rationalists argue that the characteristic might be a good proxy for imperfectly estimated factor beta.

# Risk premia applied to factors

Expected returns or risk premia for factors are no more observable than they are for assets. In academic models, different risk factors may each have a different market price of risk  $(\lambda_{j})$ , reflecting their different covariances with marginal utility (bad times). Among the four premia, the inflation premium should then be the lowest, given inflation's less consistent link to bad times.

Moreover, these covariances may not be constant over time, so factors have time-varying (and maybe sign-varying) premia. Examples: (1) The BRP and inflation premium were very high in the early 1980s, not just because inflation was high and volatile (standalone risk) but because high inflation and consequent bond losses coincided, during that era, with recessions and low valuations of assets other than bonds (bad times). (2) The correlation of liquidity conditions with bad times became especially noticeable during the 2007-2008 Credit Crunch and liquidity drought, resulting in sharply wider liquidity premia. (3) The ERP and growth premium were high for decades following the Great Depression. Maybe this is in the nature of the beast—extreme conditions in any risk factor tend to coincide with bad times even if normally the link is weak.

Memory effects may also contribute. It is not surprising that various risk premia peak soon after stress events and decay gradually thereafter, albeit at varying speeds. Stress events inherently imply an elevated amount of risk, and such times often trigger enhanced risk aversion. Irrational feedback effects can also be influential. The persistent success of any factor may lead to extrapolation and active marketing of investment products based on the factor feeding return-chasing fund inflows. Such dynamics were apparent with equity value, FX carry, and volatility-selling strategies in 2006-2007, just as they were with static asset classes (equity, real estate, and credit booms). Related overcrowding made each selloff worse when the turn finally came. Chapter 20 will focus on such feedback effects.

# Combining factors

I will not focus on ways of "putting the pieces together" to any great degree except for introducing one workhorse financial economists often use, the multi-factor model. The relation between expected returns and factors could be assumed to be linear, as in equation (5.5):  $E(r)_i = \sum \beta_{ij} \lambda_j$ . Model specification and estimation then involves identifying relevant factors and proxies for them, estimating asset sensitivities to them, and rewards the market offers for bearing each risk. (Asset risk premia can be partly overlapping but the same is true for factor risk premia. Statistical techniques exist to help avoid double-counting or incorporating correlations across factors.)

One approach would be to use return-based factors (asset classes and strategy styles as factors). In the spirit of Chapters 8-15, I sketch a plausible model which proposes that expected excess return for any asset reflects the summed product of various betas (asset sensitivity to a given factor) and corresponding factor risk premia (market price of risk for the factor) for at least the following six factors:

Asset expected excess return (over cash)

$$
\begin{array}{l} = \text {A s s e t} * \text {E q u i t y} \\ + \text {A s s e t T r e a s u r y m a k e t} * \text {B o n d} \\ + \text {A s s e t c r e d i t b e t a} * \text {C r e d i t r i s k p r e m i u m} \\ + \text {A s s e t v a l u e b e t a} * \text {V a l u e p r e m i u m} \\ + \text {A s s e t} * \text {C a r r y p r e m i u m} \\ + \text {A s s e t m o m e n t u m b e t a} * \text {M o m e n t u m p r e m i u m} + \dots \tag {16.1} \\ \end{array}
$$

The proposed model is non-standard because such analysis tends to focus on one asset class at a time. For an analysis to encompass multiple asset classes, broader style proxies may be more relevant. Moreover, if we think that expected returns reflect asset characteristics more than risk sensitivities, we can replace the last three factor betas with some measures of valuation, carry, and momentum. A cross-sectional regression could be run, say, every month between many asset returns against their various betas or characteristics, to estimate the independent market reward (premia) for each factor during this period. Averaging these premia over time would show which factors are consistently rewarded. More heroically, these average premia can be used to estimate each asset's expected excess return. Empirical analysis may focus on short-term (say, monthly) expected returns—not on long-term expected returns. I do not necessarily endorse this approach as practically useful. A consistent framework across different investment types is appealing but may lose some valuable detail in each asset class or strategy.

Instead of return-based factors, we could specify underlying risk factors, such as growth, inflation, and illiquidity. Ideally, we could design investable proxies but the factor proxies used in the following chapters are not investable:

Asset expected excess return (over cash)

$$
\begin{array}{l} = \text {A s s e t g r o w t h f a c t o r b e t a} * \text {G r o w t h p r e m i u m} \\ + \text {A s s e t i n f l a t i o n b e t a} * \text {I n f l a t i o n p r e m i u m} \\ + \text {A s s e t i l l i q u i d i t y b e t a} * \text {I l l i q u i d i t y p r e m i u m} + \dots \tag {16.2} \\ \end{array}
$$

A key advantage in using multi-factor models is that they can consistently assess the expected returns of diverse assets that are influenced by multiple drivers. However, their use requires simplifying assumptions and resulting expected return estimates come with noise.

# 16.2 INTRODUCTION TO THE GROWTH FACTOR

I start with the factor that is closest to the equity risk premium. Equity investments are the main way that investors participate in economic growth and reap long-run reward for bearing uncertainty about growth. "The growth factor" is related to aggregate wealth changes and to economic growth. The net present value of aggregate wealth capitalizes current and expected future cash flows in the economy, which in turn depend on growth created by productive investments. Listed equities constitute the largest and probably most volatile part of aggregate wealth. Moreover, equity market returns appear positively correlated with other risky assets and with less measurable wealth (such as human capital and privately held assets), so these too are sensitive to the growth factor. Time variation in cash flow forecasts and in market risk appetite or sentiment may well be procyclical as well.

At first blush, the link between growth and risky asset returns seems obvious, both across assets (faster growing stocks, industries, and countries should have higher returns) and over time (faster growth should coincide with higher risky asset returns). The empirical relations are surprisingly ambiguous, perhaps due to overpricing of growth. No investment or characteristic is attractive at any price. Furthermore, economic growth may benefit labor and consumers more than capital owners or it may benefit new or privately held enterprises more than listed companies.

Growth can be related to both unexpected and expected (required) asset returns. Since equity prices are forward looking, equity returns and equity valuation ratios should have a stronger positive relation with future growth than with past or contemporaneous growth. But forecasting growth is not easy.

What growth are we talking about? There are many angles to growth (growth of wealth; GDP or consumption growth; earnings, dividends, or profit growth; credit growth that may have links to liquidity, risk appetite, and default prospects, etc.). The source of economic growth matters. GDP growth reflects both productivity growth and labor force growth. Certain types of growth are of greatest benefit to investor-shareholders while other types accrue more to consumers, labor, and private entrepreneurs. One important empirical finding is that trend growth rates in real GDP and real earnings per share or dividends per share have not been equal, even though this assumption is commonly made. The latter series have historically lagged GDP growth rate, and by substantial amounts.

It can be useful to distinguish secular (e.g., trend productivity) and cyclical growth and their impact on asset returns:

- Secular frequency. Slow-moving or secular fluctuations are important for long-term assets like equities. It is difficult to measure trend productivity growth in real time but it is almost certainly of first-order importance. Productivity growth rates and corporate profit share in the economy appear to share common trends. Both series were high in the 1950s and 1960s, much lower from the early 1970s to the early 1990s, and again higher from the mid-1990s until recently. Such variation could reflect the uneven pace of technological progress; the shifting pendulum between markets and the state; the degree of globalization and financialization; inflation trends; demographic and institutional changes; and even war vs. peace. Despite common trends, it seems useful to distinguish (trend) GDP growth and fluctuating profits/GDP share, and the relations of each of these to asset returns.
- Cyclical frequency. Fluctuations related to business cycles and monetary policy cycles are surprisingly important, even for the prices of long-term assets, although such fluctuations could conceivably wash out over time. For example, stock returns have been historically low in early to mid-recessionary periods while both stocks and bonds have fared well late in recessions and near the cyclical trough. Some assets may be more sensitive to the stage of the business cycle (and the level of resource utilization or slack) than to the economy's growth rate. Chapter 26 will describe average asset returns at various stages of the U.S. business cycle. Cyclical variation in asset returns may not only reflect cyclical cash flow forecasts or default probabilities but also time-varying discount rates or risk appetite.

# 16.3 THEORY AND EVIDENCE ON GROWTH

Theory associates high expected growth rates with high asset prices today, not with high expected returns

Recall that an asset's price reflects its expected future cash flows (in the numerator of equation (5.1)) and a discount rate that incorporates the riskless rate and risk premia (in the denominator). Finance theory says that, if markets are in equilibrium and prices are fair, then expected asset returns equal the required discount rate (thus expected returns are in the denominator—not in the numerator). If expected cash flow growth (in the numerator) is considered exceptionally high, this may not boost expected asset returns at all but just raises today's asset price. Comparing two stocks, a firm with fast-growing future cash flows deserves a higher price than a firm with poor growth prospects. If the firms are deemed equally risky, they should in theory have the same discount rates [1]. In reality, future growth is uncertain. More optimistic forecasts of future growth imply higher stock prices and valuation multiples.

In theory, the riskless real interest rate reflects economy-wide growth prospects—the marginal product of capital or a related number based on consumption smoothing logic (because a high riskless real rate is needed to smooth consumption over a high-growth period) [2]. Classic finance theories assume constant risk premia over time (even if they state that assets that tend to perform poorly in bad times warrant high risk premia). However, some recent models contain the idea of countercyclically time-varying risk premia.

# Feasible trend output growth sets speed limits

Before turning to shorter term growth prospects and their impact on asset returns, I stress how important long-term growth is to long-lived assets such as equities. Any empirical analysis that involves discounting expected future cash flows uses some concept of long-term GDP growth to anchor cash flow growth. Most equity analysts make subjective assessments of a firm's ability to earn abnormal profits over the next few years but then assume that profit growth gradually converges toward the GDP growth rate. Thus it seems that the overall growth potential of an economy sets important speed limits for all different types of growth (GDP, profits, earnings, dividends). What is the speed limit?

- Successful firms can have real double-digit growth for some years but not for several decades. I will not provide firm-level evidence here. Successful countries face even tighter constraints; for example, average real GDP-per-capita growth over 1900–2006 ranged between  $1\%$  and  $3\%$  across 40 countries for which such data exist.
- The global economy has never, before or since, grown as fast as the  $4\%$  annual real growth pace after World War II. (Slightly over half of this was per-capita growth, the rest population growth which was unsustainable and which has not been sustained.) Indeed, most of human history has been characterized by near-zero trend growth. The long-run real GDP-per-capita growth rate in Western economies has been estimated by Angus Maddison (2007) at between  $0\%$  and  $0.1\%$  p.a. for millennia until the 19th century. The Industrial Revolution and other important developments triggered much faster growth:  $1.5\%$  for 1820–1900 and  $1.9\%$  for 1900–2000.
- The hope of new age optimists is that no speed limits apply in the future. Besides Internet-driven optimism, such hope is based on the idea of increasing returns from expanding knowledge and technological progress. Pessimists raise questions about sustainable growth based on climate change, environmental pollution, and scarce natural resources. More mundane issues—the stretched finances of consumers and the public sector after the Credit Crunch, greater regulation and state involvement, and upcoming demographic trends—also point to slower trend growth than that seen in the 20th century. Many observers assume trend growth near  $2\%$ , in line with evidence from the past century, but there are stories to justify more optimistic and pessimistic paths. These speed limits obviously differ between advanced (mature) and emerging economies.

# How do speed limits on economic growth translate to speed limits on long-term asset returns?

Diermeier-Ibbotson-Siegel (1984), in a classic study on speed limits, note that the supply of total investment returns equals income returns for all assets, plus growth in the aggregate market value of investable assets, minus new issues. If the aggregate value of investable assets is assumed to be a fixed proportion of social wealth, they should in the long run grow at the same speed. The trend growth rate in real GDP, adjusted by current yield and net new-issue data, should guide investor views of sustainable long-run real returns. Note that stock returns can persistently outpace economic growth because these returns include dividend income—not just dividend growth.

In the very long run, profits cannot grow at a faster (slower) rate than the overall economy. Otherwise, the profits/GDP ratio would explode (implode). This ratio appears to be mean-reverting over time, but it can contain quite long trends, which makes trend GDP growth a relatively weak anchor for profit growth.

Most observers may have genuinely thought that the trend GDP growth rate would be a floor to any estimates of trend earnings growth, but it actually has proved to be a ceiling. Arnott-Bernstein (2002) shocked many readers by documenting empirically that, over more than a century, the trend earnings-per-share (EPS) growth rate (below  $2\%$  per annum) has been well below the GDP growth rate (above  $3\%$ ) and even lagged the GDP-per-capita growth rate of  $2\%$  (see Figure 8.12 and Table 8.4). Another study showed that real dividend growth over the 20th century averaged near  $0\%$  across countries and lagged real GDP growth in 15 of the 16 countries studied.

The persistent decline in the EPS/GDP ratio and evidence of a mean-reverting (rather than declining) profits/GDP share can be reconciled by making a distinction between aggregate earnings growth and EPS growth. Aggregate corporate profit growth has better matched the long-run output growth rate than has the EPS growth rate, but only the latter matters for valuing the market. Aggregate corporate profits are measured for all equities—listed and unlisted. Current shareholders only benefit from EPS growth in listed shares and miss the entrepreneurial dynamism and presumably faster profit growth in the unlisted sector. If economic growth requires capital expenditure, this reduces current dividends or requires new issuance; higher future earnings from these higher investments are of no benefit to current shareholders. Ritter (2005) summarizes: "Empirically, what matters for stock returns is how much of an economy's growth comes from reinvestment of earnings into positive NPV investments in existing publicly traded companies, versus how much of it comes from personal savings that are then invested in private companies or new issues of equity from existing companies." As noted in Section 8.4.3, of the  $3\%$  compound average aggregate real earnings growth in the past 60 to 80 years, existing investors have enjoyed only about  $1\%$  (real EPS growth), while  $2\%$  has been diluted by new equity issuance. This fact is not widely understood, partly because Wall Street has little incentive to highlight such findings.

Finally, speed limits are set on all investable assets—not just equities. A given trend GDP growth rate may imply a certain feasible long-run return for all assets (equity and debt) together, but this return could be divided between a low real riskless rate and a high equity premium or a high real rate and a low equity premium—depending on investor preferences (impatience, risk aversion, consumption smoothing, and liquidity needs); the relative sizes of different asset classes; and tax policies, laws, and regulations.

# Determinants of growth

Not surprisingly, an extensive literature studies the determinants of long-run growth for countries or firms. This fascinating topic would take us too far from this book's central themes but I summarize some key observations.

Economy-wide growth is supported by good institutions (property rights, lack of corruption, capital markets), market-oriented policies that sustain macroeconomic stab ility (including low inflation), investing in infrastructure (including a healthy and educated labor force), and expanding knowledge (via technological progress and openness to the global economy).

Firm-level earnings growth reflects investment in new assets and improving the efficiency of existing assets. Not all growth is profitable; some empire-building acquisitions and projects may be value destroying. Exploiting a firm's competitive advantage is the main sustainable source of abnormal profits.

# 16.4 ASSET MARKET RELATIONS

Stock market returns and yield curve steepness are the two financial series whose relation to economic growth has attracted the most attention. I discuss each of these before reviewing sensitivities to growth of various asset classes.

# 16.4.1 Relations between equity market returns and earnings or GDP growth rates

This question can be studied from several perspectives: cross-sectional or time series relations, firm-level or aggregate relations, and contemporaneous or predictive relations. Empirical findings are quite ambiguous, many relations are weak, and some relations are even negative. The most prominent one is underperformance of growth stocks.

# Cross-sectional relations across firms and countries

Growth stocks with high valuation ratios and high past earnings growth rates have lower future returns than value stocks. The same is true for stocks of firms with high analyst forecasts of long-term earnings growth. After a few years, growth stocks and value stocks have similar earnings growth rates although market P/Es and analyst forecasts had expected wide growth rate gaps to persist. The main explanation for outperformance of value stocks is overpricing of growth. Fast growth is an appealing characteristic but not at any price. Past growth is not as predictive of future growth as the market seems to believe: exceptional growth rates tend to normalize faster than market P/E ratios imply (see Chapter 12). However, certain other growth and profitability measures exhibit a mild positive relation with future returns.

Another surprising cross-sectional finding is the absence of a positive relation across countries between long-term economic growth and stock market returns. Dimson-Marsh-Staunton (2010) find that countries whose real GDP per capita grew faster than average over a 110-year history (1900-2009) did not have higher real equity market returns than their slower growing peers even during the same period. Depending on how growth rates and returns are defined, the relation was mildly negative or mildly positive. More tactically, stock markets in countries with especially high or low GDP growth rates over the preceding 5 years subsequently earned similar Sharpe ratios as markets in countries with middling past growth.

Figure 16.1 shows similar evidence regionally over a 22-year window. Real GDP growth in Asia ex-Japan was roughly three times faster than in other regions, but equity market returns there were unexceptional. In contrast, Latin America's GDP growth was ordinary but equity performance stagnering (partly thanks to very cheap valuation levels in the 1980s and a shift over time to more capital-friendly economic policies). Japan is the one place where slow growth and poor equity returns did go hand in hand (following decades of abnormal growth and high equity returns).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/d6afab2696294e77e25fe0dec2109a893b4d0c52a50876e24b09bf26d6163a9a.jpg)

Figure 16.1. Long-run economic growth and equity returns may not go hand in hand. Sources: Bloomberg, International Monetary Fund, MSCI Barra.

How about that investor darling China? MSCI only has Chinese equity return data since 1993. By the end of 2009, after wild swings, a USD-based investor would have earned a slightly negative total return—and this during a 17-year period when China's real annual GDP growth averaged  $10\%$ . Using slightly different units: China's economy grew fivefold, plus inflation, over 1993-2009 and equity index investors still lost money.

Speidell et al. (2005) show that shareholder dilution as a result of new capital raising by firms is much more severe in emerging markets than in developed markets and that the damage to investors is worst in Asia ex-Japan. Over an 11-year period when emerging market capitalization grew, on average, by  $13.1\%$  annually, existing shareholders enjoyed annual price (total) returns of only  $3.2\%$ $(5.4\%)$ . Remaining market cap growth was diluted by new and secondary issuance boosting the number of shares. (The average price dilution rate is thus almost  $10\%$  in emerging markets, compared with the  $2\%$  dilution rate in the U.S. over the same period and over longer periods analyzed by Bernstein-Arnott, 2003 and Cornell, 2010.)

Confusing market cap growth with investor returns is a dangerous mistake. Recall that market capitalization reflects stock price times the number of shares (for any single firm or aggregated across all firms in a market). The price return that shareholders experience thus equals market cap growth minus dilution, and total return equals the price return plus dividends. Total return for investors equals the rate of market cap growth only if shareholders can maintain their ownership position in all companies simply by reinvesting their total returns into share issuance (Dilution = Dividends). This condition may be roughly satisfied in advanced economies over the long run, but it tends to be glaringly off in fast-growing emerging economies that have high capital needs and are investor favorites.

# Box 16.1. Emerging markets as a growth thermometer

Emerging market equities have often served as a thermometer of financial markets' growth optimism. Critics argue that they offer limited diversification, just a high-beta play on general risk appetite or global growth expectations. However, emerging markets have their own story or stories. After a bull market for emerging economies in the early 1990s, this story turned negative amidst successive crises in 1994-1998 (Mexico, Asia, Russia), before giving way to sustained positive developments in the 2000s. The secular rise of the developing world, led by the BRIC countries (Brazil, Russia, India, China), is a compelling story—especially since it coincides with what could even be an absolute decline in aging G7 economies. The growth edge of emerging markets will be especially pronounced in the coming years as developed economies de-lever while emerging economies benefit from global monetary policy that is easier than they need, in addition to a productivity boost unleashed by better economic policies and benign demographics.

Yet, valuations matter and this is such a consensus trade that the story may already be fully in market prices. I have already noted that a sustained growth edge does not always translate into equity market outperformance. Figure 16.2 offers something to both camps. It shows that, historically, emerging markets have outperformed developed markets when starting from cheap valuations. Late-2009 relative valuations are high but not yet at the peak levels seen in the early 1990s. It is conceivable that emerging markets will be sustainably re-rated so that their P/E ratios again lie above those of developed markets. In the past, the low relative P/E ratio reflected a high risk premium (for greater political risk, less investor protection, lower liquidity, etc.) which more than offset emerging economies' growth edge. In the coming decade the additional required risk premium may be lower and the growth edge higher. Despite my value orientation I would not bet against emerging markets' continued outperformance.

Figure 16.2. Relative valuations and relative performance of emerging vs. developed market equities.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/573dcc75185af89a987b6f8fc3bdb16a7d394aca76c7fa3e409816588a14130a.jpg)

Sources: Bloomberg, Brevan Howard, own calculations.

Incidentally, most emerging market indices start in the late 1980s when these markets were relatively cheap. Over a longer history (1976-2009) which includes the early-1980s' underperformance, emerging markets have actually lagged developed markets (9.5\% vs. 10.5\% annual return).

# Time series relations between equity returns and growth surprises/growth rates

Perhaps the cleanest way to assess different assets' sensitivities to economic growth is to study the market's reactions to surprises in economic announcements. Any other approach involves almost insurmountable challenges in matching asset returns to macroeconomic data. (The challenges include temporal lining up of the series, which is ambiguous due to publication lags and the forward-looking nature of asset prices; proxying unobservable expectations; and dealing with data revisions.) As expected, equity markets and other risky assets respond positively to news of strong economic growth, while government bonds benefit from news of weak growth [3]. Unexpected growth news influences stock prices partly through revision of the cyclical cash flow growth outlook but also partly through time-varying risk aversion and sentiment. Figure 16.3 shows that the U.S. equity market has tracked cumulative growth surprises in key macroeconomic announcements startlingly well. Several regular announcements related to real activity are tracked; actual releases are compared with consensus forecasts; a binary surprise value  $(+1$  or  $-1)$  is assigned to each release; and these values are summed over time. (The graph uses an in-house growth surprise index but many Wall Street economists offer variants of the same idea.)

Another strand of literature focuses on contemporaneous relations between earnings news and equity returns. At the firm level the relation is clearly positive: positive earnings surprises trigger significant equity price appreciation—and even some post-announcement upward price drift (apparent underreaction). However, at the aggregate level the contemporaneous relation between earnings news (realized past quarter and announcement quarter earnings) and stock market returns is negative. This finding may reflect the forward-looking nature of stock prices (they reflect cash flow news several quarters and years ahead) or the offsetting impact of changing required returns. If analysts' earnings forecasts are used instead of actual earnings, the contemporaneous relation to aggregate stock returns is positive. This positive relation may not be fully rational: in the late 1990s, analysts' and investors' overoptimism about equity markets, and especially about tech stocks, bloomed together.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/88b08226f1d5b47c60465ae6413187923c2072d46a8c24c6f1673dadb6c3fb80.jpg)

Figure 16.3. U.S. equities have tracked economic surprises since the mid-1990s well. Sources: Bloomberg, Brevan Howard.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/ba09f6fed396035857dd2d8f684ac427de945402a010044d6459b78e8aa8a36b.jpg)

Figure 16.4. U.S. equity market as a growth predictor, 1926-2009. Sources: Haver Analytics, Federal Reserve Board, National Bureau of Economic Research.

Time series relations between equity market returns and GDP growth rates also show mixed signs. Dimson-Marsh-Staunton (2005) present empirical evidence over a century in a large number of countries. The sign of the contemporaneous relation is inconsistent across countries but tends to be insignificantly positive. The lead-lag relation of GDP growth predicting future stock returns is again inconsistent but the typical relation is insignificantly negative. The most consistent relation is in equity market returns predicting (with a positive sign) next year GDP growth (in many countries a significant relation). For the U.S., I find a positive correlation of 0.24 since 1926 (see Figure 16.4). The pattern is broadly similar in the 19th century (not shown)—the positive relation is pretty robust outside major wars (the Napoleonic wars, the U.S. Civil War, World War II).

Table 16.1 showed that the contemporaneous correlation between monthly stock market returns and the change in economists' consensus forecasts of next year economic growth is mildly positive (0.33) and comparable for other risky assets.

Finally, some words on market valuation ratios' limited ability to predict future cash flow growth. Over the past century, dividend yields have been much better predictors of future market returns than of future dividend growth [4]. More generally, it is surprisingly difficult to forecast earnings or dividend growth. New research suggests that dividend growth may be predictable but that correlated variation between expected growth and expected returns may conceal this predictability. For example, in a recession dividends may be temporarily depressed and expected to grow faster in the future, but this effect may be offset by a higher required risk premium. The former effect reduces dividend yield while the latter raises it. Using a control variable besides dividend yield in a regression can uncover such cyclical predictability (e.g., the consumption/wealth ratio or the consumption/dividends ratio).

# 16.4.2 Relation between yield curve shape and economic growth

One series used to be an even better growth predictor than equity market return. Yield curve steepness has had an impressive ability to forecast GDP growth since World War II and inverted yield curves have been the most successful recession predictors. Many other financial series, notably credit spreads, also track business cycles but in a more contemporaneous fashion. The best explanation for the yield curve's predictive ability is that it proxies for the Fed's monetary policy stance: a steep (flat/inverted) curve reflects easy (tight) monetary policy. However, some evidence of flat or inverted curves predicting recessions go back to the mid-19th century, predating the Fed's inception in 1913.

Most impressively, since 1960 an inversion has preceded each recession, with no false predictions (except 1967 using some curve measures); see Figure 16.5. The signal worked again with the 1989 and 2000 inversions and even with the inversion in the fourth quarter of 2005 and the first quarter of 2006; however, the lag from the last inversion to the beginning of the recession in December 2007 was abnormally long. This empirical relation may be stronger when Fed tightening causes recessions and Fed easing leads the recovery, typical features of postwar business cycles. The story may be different in balance sheet recessions caused by financial de-leveraging, as in the 1930s and 2008, where the Fed has less power to affect the economy. Not surprisingly, many firms have developed composite financial conditions indices that go beyond the monetary policy stance. Such indices have been superior predictors of real activity during the past decade. I should also add that the zero interest rate boundary (nominal rates cannot fall below zero or investors prefer their mattresses to banks) complicates yield curve analysis, including the relation between curve steepness and economic growth prospects.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/fb44bbd8437789ccf748006c0b880fd9d3dbda0c6779cc18d46e4e0e691cb0bb.jpg)

Figure 16.5. Yield curve steepness as a growth predictor, 1926-2009. Sources: Haver Analytics, Federal Reserve Board, National Bureau of Economic Research.

A horse race between the stock market's ability and the yield curve's ability to forecast next year GDP growth indicates that the curve did a better job during most postwar business cycles (0.56 correlation over the period from the 1950s through the 1980s), but the stock market has been the better predictor since the 1980s (0.46 correlation vs. the curve's 0.13). For the whole 1926-2009 period, equities have an edge (0.24 vs. 0.18), which they kept in the postwar period (0.35 vs. 0.31).

# 16.4.3 Many asset classes' growth sensitivities

Figure 16.6 contrasts average returns on various financial assets and strategies since 1990 in two time environments, corresponding to months when the economic growth outlook is revised up or down. Assets and strategies are sorted here by their correlation with the growth factor, which is proxied by monthly change in the consensus forecast of next year U.S. real GDP growth. Most investments, starting with listed private equity and corporate-vs.-Treasury trades, are positively exposed to growth news; long Treasury positions (shown as "duration") and momentum-oriented strategies are the main exceptions.

Some new derivative assets are even more directly related to investors' growth views than the equity market index. Dividend swaps reveal the market's dividend growth expectations, though their pricing also reflects risk and liquidity premia and technical supply-demand factors. Similar interpretation problems arise when using the break-even inflation rate for inflation-linked bonds (vs. nominal Treasuries) as a proxy for investors' inflation expectations.

Among single stocks, Goetzmann-Watanabe-Watanabe (2009) show that procyclical stocks earn higher long-run returns. Specifically, stocks that are highly correlated with

Figure 16.6. Average monthly returns when the growth factor is above or below the median, 1990-2009.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/ca49a89170635fbbcf3791034b0cf394dfaf69ef489b70d565e9875c9b520412.jpg)

Sources: Bloomberg, Consensus Economics, LPX, MSCI Barra, FTSE, Bank of America Merrill Lynch, Hedge Fund Research, Barclays Capital, S&P GSCI, Ken French's website, Brevan Howard, own calculations.

the consensus forecast of near-term GDP growth have delivered higher average returns over 45 years.

# 16.5 TIME-VARYING GROWTH PREMIUM

Because the growth premium is so closely related to the equity premium, much of the discussion about a time-varying equity premium in Chapter 8 applies here.

Let us recall the debate regarding the countercyclical pattern in risky assets' expected returns. Many academic studies find that risky asset returns are predictably high (low) after business cycle troughs (peaks). These studies identify expected returns by the ability of some countercyclical indicator (such as yield curve steepness or consumption–wealth ratio) to predict near-term returns—and assume that fitted realized returns in a regression are the expected returns of a rational market. Based on this logic, rational investors may foresee low expected returns near a cyclical peak and accept these as fair, thanks to their higher risk tolerance amidst abundant wealth.

However, even if predictability regressions capture objectively feasible near-term returns, survey evidence raises doubts about whether most investors subjectively expect such countercyclic patterns. Individual investor surveys, especially, suggest that at the end of expansions (near cyclical peaks), optimistic investors have subjectively high expected returns or a high risk appetite, which boosts today's prices for risky assets and reduces their prospective feasible returns. Conversely, at the end of recessions (near cyclical troughs), pessimistic investors extrapolate further trouble or require especially high risk premia, hurting today's asset prices. Procyclical market sentiment and procyclical risk appetite are alternative explanations of observed empirical predictability patterns; the former involves investor irrationality while the latter is consistent with rational expectations and time-varying risk premia.

# 16.6 NOTES

[1] In equilibrium (if investors are rational), the higher price today and the related higher valuation ratio just offsets growth stock's advantage of faster expected earnings growth. If investors are not fully rational, then maybe more than offsets: proponents of investor irrationality argue that hoped-for growth is overpriced.

[2] The theories are so general that it is not clear which real rate and which growth rate should be so tied. However, empirical support for the tie is weak. A cursory look at the data in Tables 3.6 and 3.7 reveals no obvious tendency for high real yields or real bond returns to coincide with decades of fast real growth. Or simply recall that fast productivity growth in the 1950s and 1960s as well as around 2000 coincided with relatively low real yields, while slow productivity growth in the 1980s coincided with high real yields. Other influences seem to matter more.

[3] One exception is that the equity market impact of employment (and more broadly growth) news varies over the business cycle. When there is abundant slack in the economy, equity markets respond positively to news of stronger growth or rising employment. However, when an expansion is more mature, equity markets respond negatively. The main explanation is that further growth is seen as inflationary amidst tight capacity utilization and likely to prompt monetary policy tightening. Another, related explanation (that does not involve forecasting monetary policy) is that, in an overheated economy, the cost of scarce labor and materials is expected to grow faster than revenues.

[4] Stock market valuation ratios reflect changing expected future cash flows and discount rates (required returns). Early studies that documented dividend yields' ability to predict future equity returns prompted several papers that highlighted econometric flaws in the analysis. However, as John Cochrane notes in the aptly titled "The dog that did not bark" (2008a), under reasonable assumptions dividend yields must forecast either future returns or dividend growth—the only question is how much of each. Thus, dividend yields' inability to predict future dividend growth implied new evidence about their ability to predict future market returns. Paraphrasing Sherlock Holmes, the curious incident that dividend yields do not predict future dividend growth is strong evidence in favor of return predictability. Yet, Chen (2008) provides evidence to challenge these results in different sample periods.

Source notes. Probably the best known multi-factor models are Chen-Roll-Ross (1986) with macro-factors and Fama-French (1992, 1993) with financial factors. On multifactor models in general and on links between financial assets and real economy, see Ferson-Harvey (1991), Cochrane (2005, 2006), and Melas-Suryanarayanan-Cavaglia (2010). On the speed limits of economic growth, see Diermeyer-Ibbotson-Siegel (1984), Arnott-Bernstein (2002), Bernstein-Arnott (2003), Ibbotson-Chen (2003), Maddison (2007), and Barro-Ursua (2008).

On the muted relations between equity market returns and economic growth, see Bernstein-Arnott (2003), Dimson-Marsh-Staunton (2005, 2010), Ritter (2005), Speidell et al. (2005), Cornell (2010); also see Section 8.4.3. On the relation between curve steepness and business cycles, see Harvey (1989), Fama-French (1989), and Estrella (2005). On the average returns of procyclical stocks, see Goetzmann-Watanabe-Watanabe (2009). On the relation between dividend yield and future growth, see Lettau-Ludvigson (2005), Santos-Veronesi (2006), Chen (2008), and Cochrane (2008a).

# 17

# Inflation factor and inflation premium

- Inflation developments are the main drivers of nominal (fixed income) asset performance but they also influence stock markets and other supposedly real assets.
- Long-dated nominal Treasuries have the most consistent negative inflation sensitivity. The inflation risk premium (IRP) is the key driver of nominal bond risk premium (BRP). This IRP may reflect both standalone inflation uncertainty (which appears to vary with inflation level) and inflation's covariance with bad times. Both aspects made the IRP especially high in the early 1980s (when the BRP reached between  $3\%$  and  $4\%$ ) and especially low in the 2000s (when the BRP hovered near zero).
- Stocks have been negatively related to inflation in the short term but positively in the long term. The negative relation has both rational and irrational explanations. This relation flips sign in pre-World War II data, however, perhaps due to the changing nature of inflation or the detrimental impact of deflation not seen since the 1930s. Stock market valuation ratios are highest during low but positive inflation.
- Inflation-linked bonds and energy futures, as well as (to a lesser extent) some other commodities and real estate, are good hedges against inflation—which makes them attractive diversifiers.
- Deflation is an even more challenging risk for investors because it hurts all assets except nominal bonds.
- Well-anchored inflation expectations have kept inflation-related premia negligible for over a decade now, but the situation may change in the 2010s.

# 17.1 INTRODUCTION

The other central macro-based factor, besides growth, is inflation or changes in the general price level (see cube). For the large asset class of nominal bonds, inflation is the main risk: rising inflation hurts them, falling inflation helps them, and deflation makes them excel. High or rising inflation also hurts equity markets, at least in the short term. However, this relation is more complex—equities also suffer amidst deflation—and the reasons for why a conceptually real asset, such as equities, is empirically inflation sensitive are debated. A few asset classes such as inflation-linked bonds, commodities (especially energy-related products), and real estate benefit from higher inflation, or at least are insensitive to it. What explains asset sensitivity to inflation and why should such inflation exposure carry a risk premium?

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/ab37fcda1ffd6e3f6ae72582307161ff469d44549bbc1e07a5f94d0aeccb4753.jpg)

Why an inflation relation for nominal assets? The answer is obvious for realized returns—rising inflation expectations raise bond yields and reduce their prices. The answer is less mechanical for expected real returns of nominal bonds. Empirically, it is clear that bond yields contain a level-dependent inflation risk premium (IRP); rising inflation expectations also boost required IRP and hurt current bond prices, beyond the mechanical rate expectation impact. The IRP may reflect standalone inflation risk (a reward for sheer uncertainty about inflation that varies with inflation level) or more "theoretically kosher" risk explanations: inflation's covariance with marginal utility (bad times).

Research into this question used to be split into two directions. The traditional/empirical wing analyzed standalone inflation risk while the theoretical wing studied covariances (the correlation between growth and inflation rates or between real asset and nominal asset returns). Some new research combines the two drivers behind inflation premia. The worst of both worlds occurred in the early 1980s (both premia were record high when inflation peaked) and the best of both worlds occurred in the mid-2000s, with low premia on both counts (inflation uncertainty was low, thanks to well-anchored expectations, and stock-bond correlation had turned negative).

Measuring inflation's correlation with bad times is challenging. The correlation between, say, consumption growth and inflation rates is not terribly interesting due to measurement errors in economic data and to the fact that the important correlation is more about expected broad movements than about short-term noise. Let us first glance at decade average inflation and real growth rates over 200 years in Figure 17.1. It is not obvious whether the relation is positive or negative. Indeed, the correlation between these two series is  $-0.3$ , while the correlation between annual inflation and growth rates is  $+0.3$ . Table 16.1 showed that the correlation between monthly changes in consensus forecasts of next year real growth and inflation is  $+0.4$  (since 1990).

The inconclusive results are underlined when I plot 12-year rolling correlations between growth and inflation (year-on-year rates). These correlations were positive from the 1870s to the 1950s and then turned negative until very recently. Broad comovements may be better captured by asset market data, such as the correlation between quarterly stock and bond returns (see Figure 17.2). The relation between long-lived real and nominal assets was mildly positive most of the 20th century, especially in the 1970s when both bond and stock valuations suffered from high inflation, and in the 1980s when both benefited from the decline in inflation. Stock-bond correlation has been negative since the late 1990s and tends to be lower amidst low inflation, deflation scares, and flight-to-quality episodes (also see Figure 9.10).

Another way to assess inflation's correlation with bad times involves studying its typical evolution over the business cycle. Figure 17.3 contrasts the cyclical pattern of inflation between 1964 and 2009 with its pattern over the two preceding 46-year periods.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/22cc879e993aa5825b98e7d607012f16b83c85df341549185b701b9c0a93ba8d.jpg)

Figure 17.1. Decade average inflation rate and real GDP growth rate in the U.S., 1814-2009.

Sources: Arnott-Bernstein (2002), Haver Analytics.

Figure 17.2. Rolling (48-quarter) correlations between growth and inflation and between real stock and bond returns, 1814-2009.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/334ee20202163ceebb1cbc647a6b6178f20e21e08a450cfeb7c36a312ed35458.jpg)

Sources: Arnott-Bernstein (2002), Haver Analytics, Bloomberg, own calculations.

Figure 17.3. Average business cycle pattern of inflation over the three 46-year subperiods since 1872.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/ca47e769afcb1979bfd9aa31e939bd3974839be67280459a091415dbc0cde115.jpg)

Sources: Robert Shiller's website, National Bureau of Economic Research, own calculations.

(The construction of these business cycle graphs is described in Chapter 26.) In the past, inflation used to be procyclical, rising during expansions and falling during contractions, perhaps because it was primarily demand driven and because money supply was relatively fixed due to the use of a gold standard. In recent decades, high inflation has coincided with contraction, perhaps because energy shocks caused both rising inflation and recession (thus stagflation), and because the Fed responded to threats of inflation with tight money policies designed to cause a recession. Supply-driven (scarcity-related) inflation looks especially noxious, prompting high inflation to coincide with bad times, unlike demand-driven inflation.

Why any inflation relation for real assets such as equities? One can study the relations between expected, unexpected, or realized stock returns and expected, unexpected, or realized inflation, over either short or long horizons. Over a short horizon, the empirical relation between stock returns and inflation is clearly negative. In Section 8.4.2, I reviewed three main explanations: inflation's ability to predict slow economic growth, an inflation-related risk premium, and irrational money illusion. Over a long horizon the empirical relation may be positive; at least ex ante return, proxied by earnings yield, tends to be elevated during periods of high expected inflation (but also amidst deflation).

Why is inflation negatively related to real growth? Theory highlights the real economic costs of inflation. Coping with even fully anticipated inflation requires some effort—so-called "shoe leather" costs from more frequent visits to banks, for example. Larger costs come from unexpected inflation: wealth redistribution, the impact of inflation uncertainty on consumption and investment decisions, and the tendency to confuse overall inflation with relative price changes. Overall, inflation and inflation uncertainty lead to misallocation of resources and thus hamper growth. Empirically, a higher inflation level is associated with greater inflation variability and uncertainty as well as with slower productivity growth. Time series analyses on major economies and cross-country comparisons agree: Barro (1995) studies the experience in over 100 countries over a 30-year period and documents a positive link between inflation level and inflation variability as well as a negative link between both of these and economic growth.

The above linkages are slow moving. More cyclically, monetary policy has been an important driver of recessions between 1950 and 2001. The Fed tightens monetary policy at a mature stage of expansion when inflation pressures intensify, and eases policy after a recession has cooled those inflation pressures down. Thus, higher inflation predicts slower growth due to central bank responses, a pattern investors recognize and act upon.

In general, postwar experience has not been at all consistent with the extreme monetarist view that the real and nominal parts of the economy are unconnected (i.e., that inflation has no real effects). Inflation level appears to have been a key driver for many financial and macroeconomic series postwar—including ex ante real yield, real GDP growth, real profit growth, productivity, the profit-GDP ratio, the equity market's P/E ratio—or at least has shared major trends with them. The peak in inflation (1973-1982) coincided with adverse real outcomes and asset valuations. However, many relations have opposite signs in prewar data.

# 17.2 INFLATION PROCESS—HISTORY, DETERMINANTS, EXPECTATIONS

It is important to recall that sustained inflation is a 20th-century phenomenon. Over centuries there were long periods of inflation but these were matched by long periods of deflation (see Figure 17.4 extracted from Reinhart-Rogoff, 2008). For example, the net annual inflation rate in the U.K. between 1600 and 1900 was near zero, as was the U.S. annual inflation rate in the 19th century. Other studies suggest that the only period of

Figure 17.4. Sustained inflation only became pervasive in the 20th century: Median inflation rate (5-year moving average) for all countries, 1500–2010.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/c62286910fa96368d64b28cc50fc7080a7692c5bb7446c606fb4f6ccbb8b79d9.jpg)

Sources: Carmen M. Reinhart and Kenneth S. Rogoff (2008), "This time is different: A panoramic view of eight centuries of financial crises," NBER working paper 13882, March 2008. Carmen M. Reinhart (2010), "This time is different chartbook: Country histories on debt, default, and financial crises," NBER working paper 15815. Reproduced by permission of Carmen M. Reinhart and Kenneth S. Rogoff.

sustained inflation in Europe since the 13th century was during the gold discovery period of the 16th century. The main drivers of pre-industrial European inflation were demographics (population expansions boosted demand and caused food and energy prices to rise) and wars (debt monetization was an alternative to debt default).

During the gold standard (Britain adopted it in 1717, other developed countries in the 19th century), inflations and deflations took turns with no persistence, and long-run inflation expectations were likely near zero most of the time. Figure 17.1 showed that bouts of inflation coincided with major wars. Annual inflation rates reached double digits around the American Civil War and both world wars (but I only show smoother decade averages). Figure 17.5 compares U.S. government bond yields with estimates of expected long-term inflation [1]. High real yields (as shown by a wide gap between yields and inflation expectations in the graph) in the 19th century partly reflected riskier public debt in the emerging United States; sovereign defaults were not infrequent.

For those interested in even more distant histories, long-dated bond yields ranged between  $5\%$  and  $7\%$  from 1800 to the 1870s, while inflation—and likely inflation expectations—averaged zero. Default risk rather than inflation risk was bond investors' primary concern. The huge debts of Britain, a legacy of the costly Napoleonic wars, also kept global real yields high; the superpower of the time worked its way out of these massive debts slowly over a century. Especially during the gold standard, real yields likely did not contain much of an inflation uncertainty premium—as any inflation was seen as temporary and reversible. This mixture also made yield curves flatter in the 19th century than in the 20th century.

After World War I and suspension of the gold standard, inflation became more volatile everywhere, but prices could still go down as well as up. However, in the decades after World War II, the paper (fiat) money regime and a growing public sector made realized inflation virtually always positive. This experience caused investors to gradually expect more persistently positive inflation rates. In the U.S., wars (Korea and Vietnam) again coincided with rising fiscal deficits and rising inflation; aggressive demand man

Figure 17.5. Long-term bond yield and (statistical/survey-based) expected long-term inflation, 1871-2009.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/963a9fe829f5185470d491dad67eba7f25e4dac4355b3105738d3032a675895e.jpg)

Sources: Homer-Sylla (1991), Ibbotson Associates (Morningstar), Bloomberg, Blue Chip Economic Indicators, Federal Reserve Bank of Philadelphia, Consensus Economics, Sharon Kozicki, own calculations.

agement and insufficient understanding of expectations also contributed. After the Bretton Woods regime (a quasi gold standard) collapsed in 1971, the major economies were clearly in a fiat money regime and inflation expectations became further unanchored. The two 1970s' oil shocks coincided with productivity decline, while the Fed's overestimation of trend output and therefore slack in the economy resulted in overly loose monetary policy, contributing to the Great Inflation. Under Paul Volcker's leadership, which began in 1979, the Fed finally ended and reversed the decades-long uptrend in inflation—at the cost of deep recessions in 1980–1982. The decades of the Great Moderation followed, although inflation scares (1984, 1987, 1990, 1994) did still occur until the Fed's anti-inflation credibility was firmly established. Since the late 1990s, long-run inflation expectations have been remarkably well anchored near  $2.5\%$ .

The broad developments above have been global. The most important difference is that the U.S. never experienced real hyperinflation. Niall Ferguson (2007) adapts Milton Friedman's famous quote (that inflation is always and everywhere a monetary phenomenon) by arguing that "hyperinflation is always a political phenomenon," preceded by political instability and unrest. Other developed countries suffered hyperinflations after the world wars (Germany in the early 1920s, Japan in the late 1940s) [2]. Given this background, it is not surprising that these countries had more anti-inflationary central banks than the Fed during the Great Inflation. Since 1950, hyperinflations occurred exclusively in developing countries—and, even there, they were increasingly rare. Disinflation became a global phenomenon in the 1990s and 2000s as the range of inflation outcomes across countries narrowed and inflation levels converged toward low single-digit levels. Enhanced central bank credibility, agreement that inflation hurts growth, as well as tailwinds from globalization, deregulation, competitive pressures, and new entrants to the global labor force contributed to the low and relatively stable inflation rates in the past decade.

The global recession of 2008-2009 brought deflation back for the first time in well over a generation. The slack in output and de-leveraging pressures make deflation rather than inflation the near-term concern for policymakers. However, dramatically higher public debt and the waning of disinflationary forces make many observers suspect that the next secular move in inflation is higher.

# Understanding inflation and its drivers

Monetarists link inflation to money supply growth, assuming stable money demand over time; all other influences are second order or temporary. Economic historians emphasize the role of wars and demographics. Keynesian economists model inflation using the expectations-augmented Phillips curve: inflation is a function of inflation expectations, and the output gap (or employment gap vs. the natural rate). A standard Phillips curve relation suggests that an economy growing near or beyond its full capacity creates inflationary pressures while growing slack predicts disinflation or deflation. Besides demand-driven inflation, there is a possibility of supply-push inflation through energy and currency effects.

Inflation expectations are critical because well-anchored inflation expectations can stabilize the whole inflation process. There will always be relative price changes or temporary inflation impulses, but only second-round effects—requests for price or wage hikes that compensate for perceived inflation—transform these into persistent inflation. Central bank credibility is essential to contain second-round effects and to keep any shocks transitory. Well-anchored inflation expectations had a benign influence during the Great Moderation: empirical studies show that inflation has become less sensitive to the output gap, and to exchange rate and energy price developments. The gravitational pull of inflation expectations means that any deviation of the current inflation rate from the long-run anchor is seen as temporary, causing inflation shocks to be more transitory and inflation expectations to be mean-reverting. Besides central bank credibility, a lucky environment has also contributed to the virtuous cycle of disinflation and expectations anchoring. One topical challenge to central bank credibility is the market's understanding that high and rising levels of public indebtedness raise incentives to monetize debt.

Modern central banks recognize the essential role of expectations—as well as their own limited understanding of what determines inflation expectations. Thus, central bankers always worry about an unhinging of inflation expectations that could reverse virtuous developments in recent decades. Some of this concern is self-serving but justifiable. Well-anchored inflation expectations depend crucially on central bank actions and credibility. In the past two decades, a growing number of central banks have become independent and/or have begun to explicitly or implicitly target a particular inflation rate. Inflation management is not the only goal for central banks, however [3].

# Evolving inflation process

For our understanding of inflation expectations and the IRP in the past 50 years it is important to note that the mountain shape of inflation levels since the mid-20th century was matched by similar uptrends and downtrends in inflation uncertainty and inflation persistence. Time-varying inflation uncertainty contributed to evolution of the inflation premium during the period; both series broadly tracked the inflation level. Time-varying inflation persistence had a structural impact on inflation expectations, as explained

Figure 17.6. Inflation uncertainty series exhibit the same postwar mountain shape as inflation level.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/84020cd657a8a13b90fdb9cffaeb9365f6868214a85055c260b36db787ebcfc0.jpg)

Sources: Haver Analytics, Bloomberg, own calculations.

below, making those expectations first more sensitive and later less sensitive to the recent inflation level. Given structural instability, it seems harsh to judge investors' poor inflation forecasts as irrational.

Chapter 9 introduced the simple but powerful idea that the IRP in nominal bonds reflects inflation uncertainty, which varies empirically with inflation level. Inflation uncertainty has many dimensions but they all seem broadly level dependent, even outside hyperinflations (although the sign could flip in deflation). It is not obvious which measures of inflation uncertainty are most relevant, but all alternatives tell broadly the same story (see Figure 17.6). A rolling period measure of inflation volatility may be misleading as an indicator of inflation uncertainty because short-term inflation fluctuations captured by such a measure do not matter much; long-term uncertainty is crucial for bond yields and the IRP. Bond yield volatility may be a better measure but it also includes real rate volatility. Dispersion among forecasters in inflation surveys is a third candidate; it too has varied with inflation level, peaking in the 1970s. However, even such disagreement is not exactly the relevant measure of inflation uncertainty. Only a few surveys ask questions that reveal participants' uncertainty about their own forecasts—a measure of probability distribution—but such data are also consistent with level dependence. Adding the evidence that estimated ex ante bond risk premia track expected inflation, it seems fair to assume that inflation uncertainty and the IRP are both level dependent.

Inflation persistence rose with inflation level until 1980. During the gold standard, prices could go persistently up or down but the best long-term forecasts used to be for no change. That is, the price level followed a random walk and recent inflation had no ability to predict future inflation. Between the 1950s and the 1970s, as inflation's persistence gradually rose, the best time series forecast of future inflation shifted from zero to the most recent inflation rate. That is, instead of the price level following a random walk, the inflation rate did so (the statistical persistence parameter rose from 0 to 1, before reversing after 1980). At least the process did not become more explosive in developed markets, as it does when hyperinflation psychology takes hold. Inflation persistence did fall again through the 1980s, 1990s, and 2000s to levels not seen since the 1960s.

Importantly, we know these structural changes in the inflation process only in hindsight. Real-time investors had to learn about them gradually. Statistical analysis suggests that investors systematically underpredicted inflation in the 1960s and 1970s and then overpredicted it in the 1980s and 1990s. This evidence likely reflects, at least in part, gradual learning about an evolving process rather than irrational expectations or abnormal risk premia. Investors in the 1960s did not expect inflation rates to be as persistent as they turned out to be, based on their experience over preceding decades. Likewise, investors in the 1980s did not instantly see that inflation's persistence was fading, so they did not expect the swift decay in inflation level that actually took place.

# 17.3 INFLATION SENSITIVITY OF MAJOR ASSET CLASSES AND THE INFLATION PREMIUM

Few assets benefit from higher inflation. Spread trades between real (inflation-linked) and nominal bonds are the most reliable bets on higher inflation, but oil futures come close. Inflation-linked bonds, real estate, and some other commodities are good hedges against inflation—they have a near-zero short-term inflation beta and they tend to preserve long-term purchasing power. Stocks tend to have a negative inflation beta in the short term but a positive one in the long term. Nominal government bonds have a consistently negative inflation beta. Thus, bond markets are the best place to study the inflation premium. Caveat: There is some evidence that asset inflation sensitivities (even signs) may differ between unexpected and expected inflation, over short and long horizons, and across stable and volatile conditions. Martin (2010) surveys the literature and presents original analysis of various traditional and alternative assets' inflation-hedging abilities.

Figure 17.7 shows simple evidence on inflation sensitivity for major assets with data histories dating back to 1990, thus excluding TIPS (i.e., U.S. inflation-indexed Treasury bonds). Assets and strategies are sorted here by their correlation with the inflation factor. As expected, commodity futures have benefited most from rising inflation expectations while long-duration Treasury positions have suffered. The inflation factor here is monthly change in the consensus forecast of next year U.S. inflation rate (source Consensus Economics).

Nominal bonds. Higher expected inflation boosts ex ante government bond yields. Higher unexpected inflation tends to boost inflation expectations and also pushes yields higher. Persistent upside surprises in inflation eat up the purchasing power of fixed cash flows and reduce bonds' market values (as investors learned the hard way in the 1970s). The flip side is that disinflation boosts bond prices, and if actual deflation materializes, bonds may be the only winning asset class. Spread trades between real and nominal bonds are even more direct bets on changes in inflation rate.

Short-term rates reflect expected inflation and expected real short rates. Long-term bond yields reflect expected future short rates (which are principally driven by inflation

Figure 17.7. Average monthly returns when the inflation factor is above or below the median, 1990-2009.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/cd6e7fa2d7a0253f0c17df609101b6ea4f707c853f5274e547b90e3d1db1b8d2.jpg)

Sources: Bloomberg, Consensus Economics, LPX, MSCI Barra, FTSE, Bank of America Merrill Lynch, Hedge Fund Research, Barclays Capital, S&P GSCI, Ken French's website, Brevan Howard, own calculations.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/7fc2c6b9c236ca7fd2492fe99938b144c278395efb1c026f154f9346cfc4cd92.jpg) expectations) and required bond risk premia (which are principally driven by inflation risk premia). I stress in Chapter 9 that an inflation-level-dependent IRP can explain most slow-moving variation in BRP—the long uptrend before 1980 to a peak above  $3\%$  and the long downtrend thereafter. Ex ante real 10-year yields in Figure 17.8 follow the same pattern, with an apparent lag in the peak (learning or "bondholders' revenge").

Figure 17.8. Ex ante real yields of nominal Treasury bonds: Lagged Inflation level dependence. Sources: Haver Analytics, Bloomberg, Blue Chip Economic Indicators, Federal Reserve Bank of Philadelphia, Consensus Economics, Sharon Kozicki, own calculations.

As noted earlier, the IRP may reflect standalone inflation risk (potential capital losses on long-duration bonds) or covariance of inflation with marginal utility (bad times). The model of Campbell-Sunderam-Viceira (2010) contains both types of factors. Figure 17.6 shows how various proxies of inflation uncertainty peaked around 1980. This was also the period when bonds were at their worst from a correlation perspective: high inflation coincided with a recession (stagflation) rather than an economic boom, so bond losses occurred in very bad times.

The broad story is that the IRP has the potential to be the main driver of the BRP of nominal bonds—as indeed happened in the early 1980s when inflation uncertainty peaked and high inflation coincided with recessions (stagflation). At such times inflation even makes equity valuations cheap. Conversely, the disinflation of the 1980s and 1990s contributed to the expansion of valuation multiples for most asset classes. For nominal bonds, low and stable inflation—such as has been the case for over a decade now, when long-term inflation expectations have centered tightly around  $2.5\%$ —is associated with a minuscule IRP and BRP.

Linkers. Inflation-linked bonds such as U.S. TIPS are designed to hedge inflation, but, holding inflation rates constant, their performance depends on changes in real yields. Combining a long position in real bonds with a short position in nominal ones is primarily a bet on rising inflation over the life of the bonds (or a short-term bet on widening inflation expectations). Yield spread between nominal and real yields is called break-even inflation (BEI). BEI is often taken as a measure of the market's inflation expectations, but BEI also includes the IRP and any other differences in risk or illiquidity premia between nominal and real bonds. Before U.S. TIPS were introduced in 1997, it was commonly thought that the Treasury would save money by not having to pay the IRP in nominal bonds. This idea was consistent with theory and with U.K. market experience (where "linkers", U.K. inflation-indexed government bonds, had been introduced in the early 1980s): the IRP should make BEI exceed survey-based inflation expectations.

However, in the early years of TIPS, estimated IRP (the difference between BEI and consensus inflation forecasts) turned out to be negative. That is, the Treasury issued TIPS at such high yields (low prices) that it would have saved costs had it stuck with only issuing nominal Treasuries. Real yields were quite high (reaching, at one point,  $4.3\%$ ), surprisingly close to nominal yields at the time (in the range of  $5\%$  to  $6\%$ ), leaving BEI  $(5.5\% - 4.3\% = 1.2\%)$  much lower than survey-based inflation expectations  $(2.7\%)$ . This measured IRP was negative  $(1.2\% - 2.7\% = -1.5\%)$  due to what was eventually understood to be an illiquidity premium or a novelty premium. With hindsight, it is not too surprising that the pure inflation premium was low given the stable and low expectations for inflation, so this premium could be more easily offset and overwhelmed by an illiquidity premium in a novel asset class that initially lacked natural buyers.

After a few years, the liquidity of the TIPS market improved and investors became familiar with the beneficial aspects of TIPS (which really are the riskless asset for long-term investors who care about real returns), so the liquidity premium diminished. For a few years, a tiny positive IRP was apparent (presumably a true IRP near 30-50 bp overwhelmed the illiquidity premium, which was negative but of even smaller absolute magnitude). However, during the de-leverage episode of fall 2008, TIPS cheapened again dramatically; their yields rose while nominal Treasury yields fell, and BEI collapsed to zero. Measured IRP became negative again but this development was more reflective of a spiking illiquidity premium than of actual deflation fears. This

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/53df6d4b501c75ed1cb40077d814842f63ca70822a8a2919af523ac12b3147bb.jpg)

Figure 17.9.
