Sources: Bloomberg, Blue Chip Economic Indicators, Federal Reserve Bank of Philadelphia, Consensus Economics, own calculations.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/1dd2a97e4d3c439954efcff22f6d025f4b09124e26ca8212ece1558fbfd2ce8e.jpg)

Notes: The break-even inflation rate (BEI) is the difference between yields of the nominal 10-year Treasury and 10-year TIPS. BEI is often called the market-based inflation "forecast". I use quotation marks for "forecast" because besides inflation expectations BEI reflects the inflation premium of the nominal Treasury and the illiquidity premium of the TIPS. The net illiquidity premium vs. inflation premium is the difference between the two series in the first graph (a).

liquidity premium quickly disappeared and the TIPS market rallied hugely (see Figure 17.9).

Overall, the technical nature of the TIPS market—in particular, the time-varying illiquidity premium—makes it difficult to use TIPS-derived BEI and IRP as clear measures of the market's inflation expectations and required inflation premium, respectively. BEI has been much more volatile than consensus inflation forecasts, and its fluctuations are probably better explained by changing liquidity conditions than by evolution of the pure inflation premium. Indeed, economists' consensus forecasts of next decade inflation have been extremely stable, well within the  $2\%$  to  $3\%$  range over the whole time that TIPS have existed, while 10-year BEI has averaged  $1.75\%$  while fluctuating within a much broader range (0 to  $3\%$ ). Still, these technical distortions have dissipated over time, except during the fall of 2008, and BEI positions—buying (shorting) TIPS and shorting (buying) duration-matched nominal Treasuries—remain the cleanest way for investors to take views on inflation prospects.

Equities. Equities are poor inflation hedges over short horizons: rising inflation tends to push equity prices lower. Various explanations for the negative inflation beta of equities include a negative inflation growth relation, an inflation-related risk premium, and an irrational money illusion caused by inflation (see Chapter 8). The inflation illusion hypothesis can explain the postwar negative relation between stock returns and inflation but not the positive relation before World War II. Lee's (2009) two-regime hypothesis can explain both periods by noting that the relation between stock returns and inflation depends on the source of inflation. Lee distinguishes between demand-driven inflation (negative aggregate shocks to demand in the 1930s and 2008) and supply or scarcity-driven inflation (the 1970s and 1980s). The former causes a positive relation with stock returns, the latter a negative relation.

Boudoukh-Richardson (1993) show that, while the short-run relation between nominal stock returns and inflation is negative, the long-run relation is positive (using 5-year returns over a 200-year data history in both the U.S. and Britain). The positive relation is even stronger with ex ante variables. Figure 8.9 revealed a surprisingly strong relation between equity market earnings yield and inflation level. The simple story would be that high inflation makes equity markets cheap. However, if we use smoothed P/E ratios, the relation is quite nonlinear. Deflation can be as bad for growth prospects and risky asset valuations as high inflation is; both problems breed instability. Just recall the 1930s and 2008 experiences, or Japan in the 1990s. As Figure 8.10 showed, historical P/E10 ratios have a sombrero shape when scatterplotted in buckets on the inflation level. Market valuations are especially high amidst low, but positive, stable inflation.

Real estate. Data are limited but many studies suggest that direct real estate holdings provide a good hedge against inflation over the long run. Conceptually, this makes sense because rental yields are a key part of real estate return and rents form a roughly constant share of renters' income, which changes with inflation. Moreover, operationally, rents can be adjusted to reflect changing inflation. Other real assets, such as infrastructure, timber, and farmland are thought to have an inflation-hedging ability similar to that of directly owned real estate.

Evidence on inflation and housing booms is complex: one major boom occurred in a high-inflation environment in the 1970s and another in a low-inflation environment in the 2000s. Piazzesi-Schneider (2008) argue that both low and high inflation can produce housing booms, due to disagreement about inflation and real rates between rational and confused or inflation-illusioned investors. Confused households perceived high real rates in the 1970s while rational ones saw low real rates and bought houses by borrowing as much as they could; in the 2000s the reverse occurred. Wallison (2009) argues that the housing boom of the 2000s was government-program-driven (at least in the United States) and would not have occurred if mortgage agencies had not been directed to aggressively lower their creditworthiness standards.

Oil, gold, and other commodities. Many commodities are reasonable inflation hedges but energy even benefits from inflation (in part because rising energy prices are, in many circumstances, the main cause of inflation). It is interesting that among major commodities in recent decades, oil has the best inflation-hedging ability, highest average backwardation, and highest average return. Too much of a good thing. It is probably no accident that all these benefits coincide—it is likely an artifact of two events, the oil embargo and supply crises of the 1970s and demand squeeze driving oil prices skyward in the mid-2000s. Gold earned its highest returns around the 1979-1980 inflation rate peak but also fared well under disinflationary conditions in recent years. The role of gold as a safe haven against financial turmoil, low real yields (little opportunity cost), dollar weakness, and lack of attractive alternative currencies may explain gold's recent success better than its role as an inflation hedge. Unlike other commodities, gold can perform well even under deflationary conditions; empirically, inflation uncertainty or disagreement predicts future gold returns better than the inflation level does.

Currencies. High inflation also undermines currencies in the short term because they lose their international purchasing power. As an exception, if the central bank is sufficiently credible that markets view any rise in inflation as temporary and expect it to trigger policy tightening, higher inflation may result in short-term currency appreciation. Purchasing power parity does not hold in the short term but works reasonably well over long horizons. In the case of hyperinflation, in particular, exchange rate changes tend to offset persistent cross-country inflation differentials.

# Deflation—topical and scary

It is important to distinguish between disinflation (which typically helps risky assets) and deflation (which can be destructive). We do not have much data on deflationary environments but what we have looks scary (the U.S. in the 1930s, Japan more recently). Deflation tends to hurt most asset classes, so it is difficult to hedge against. Nominal government bonds are the most obvious hedges—but even they may be less than fully effective if a deflationary recession raises market concerns about sovereign creditworthiness.

Unfortunately, the current environment resembles classic debt deflation (which is preceded by excessive debt accumulation and characterized by de-leveraging) more than it does the benign deflation experience of the late 19th century, when stock prices, real output, and incomes all rose tremendously. In the latter case, deflation was caused by technological improvement (increased productivity) during a period when money supply growth was severely constrained by use of the gold standard.

Exhibit 26.6 will document several asset classes' average returns in inflationary and disinflationary environments.

# 17.4 TIME-VARYING INFLATION PREMIUM

Clearly, the IRP has not been constant over time. I outlined in Chapter 9 and in this chapter my view that the IRP varies broadly with inflation level and inflation uncertainty. Moreover, the IRP is higher when high-inflation episodes coincide with recessions. Both arguments justified a high IRP in the 1970s and 1980s. If stock-bond correlations are positive at the same time, equities, too, can have a meaningful IRP. In contrast, the past decade has seen conditions under which the IRP is negligible: inflation has been low and stable, and, if anything, bad times have coincided with deflation scares. However, it is easy to see scenarios in the coming decade where inflation expectations become unhinged again and bring back a significant IRP, perhaps accompanied by a sovereign default premium.

# 17.5 NOTES

[1] Survey evidence on long-term inflation expectations is available only since 1978. However, inflation expectations appear adaptive so that statistical estimates proxy them well. Between 1955 and 1978, I use statistical estimates developed by central bank researchers (Kozicki-Tinsley, 2006). Earlier I use an even simpler time series model in which inflation expectations at any date are assumed to be a weighted average of past inflation rates and zero (this worked until 1933 given the way the gold standard used to anchor inflation expectations). For details, see Appendix B.

[2] It is important to stress that the presence (even once) or absence of hyperinflation makes a huge difference to asset performance histories. For example, postwar hyperinflation in Japan destroyed wealth much more effectively than the 1930s' Great Depression in the U.S. or the 1990s' lost decade in Japan. Japanese equities lost nearly all of their value within a year but managed to recover postwar losses by 1960. For nominal assets the losses were more permanent.

[3] Empirical Taylor rules show that inflation-targeting central banks also apply countercyclic demand management, even if they don't have the Fed's dual mandate on inflation and employment. In an expectations-augmented Phillips curve model, wage and price rigidities justify some real effects from monetary policy, albeit temporary. Eventually, inflation expectations adjust and in the long run monetary stimulus only raises inflation. Friedman's quote about inflation being always a monetary phenomenon is true in the sense that sustained inflation needs central bank accommodation (money supply growth sustainably outpacing money demand growth). However, the unstable relationship between monetary growth and inflation has made monetary indicators unreliable policy tools. Close cousins to monetary indicators are making a comeback in financial conditions indices that include money and credit aggregates. Learning from recent boom-bust cycles, some policymakers show renewed interest in indicators beyond consumer price inflation. Notably, they appear more willing to consider "leaning against the wind" policies when asset prices and credit growth seem unsustainably high, in part to promote financial stability and to resist procyclicality.

Source notes. For a historical perspective on the inflation factor, see Ferguson (2007), Greenspan (2007), and Reinhart-Rogoff (2008, 2009). On the economic impact of inflation, see Barro (1995). Chapter 9 already referred to studies that relate the bond risk premium to inflation uncertainty, for example, Wright (2008) and Campbell-Sunderam-Viceira (2010). Focusing on inflation-linked bonds, see Campbell-Shiller-Viceira (2009), D'Amico-Kim-Wei (2010), and Gurkaynak-Sack-Wright (2008).

On the relation between inflation and equity returns, see Fama-Schwert (1979), Modigliani-Cohn (1979), Boudoukh-Richardson (1993), Campbell-Vuolteenaho (2004b), Piazzesi-Schneider (2007), Bekaert-Engstrom (2010), and Lee (2009). On different assets' inflation-hedging abilities at various horizons, see Normand (2006), Amenc-Martellini-Ziemann (2009), Briere-Signori (2009), Bekaert-Wang (2010), and Martin (2010).

# 18

# Liquidity factor and illiquidity premium

- Liquidity is a nebulous, multi-faceted concept which the events of 2007-2008 brought to the attention of all market players.
- Privately traded assets tend to be less liquid than publicly traded assets, alternatives less liquid than traditional assets, small-cap stocks less liquid than large-cap stocks, and non-government bonds less liquid than Treasuries.
- It is clear that liquidity-related premia have a significant impact on expected asset returns. Both the illiquidity characteristic and an asset's tendency to underperform amid liquidity droughts warrant higher required returns.
- These pricing effects are hard to empirically disentangle from each other—or from other asset premia. Yet, long-run average returns appear consistently higher for less liquid assets. The flip side is that liquidity droughts tend to coincide with other aspects of bad times, such as equity meltdowns and recessions.
- Liquidity premia vary over time—a fact that became abundantly clear in recent years. Many ex ante liquidity premia were driven through competition to record-low levels in 2006–2007, and then they widened to record-wide levels in 2008 when the demand for liquidity rose and the supply for it plummeted. The reward for liquidity provision over time is empirically related to measures of illiquidity and to market volatility.
- True long-horizon investors have a natural edge in harvesting illiquidity premia, and they should exploit this edge. Yet, they too should take into account time variation in these premia and act in a contrarian fashion.

Macro-oriented growth and inflation factors do not capture the liquidity differences across investments or changing liquidity conditions over time. This chapter focuses on the liquidity-related factors that drive expected returns (see cube).

# 18.1 INTRODUCTION

# What is liquidity and how does it vary across assets?

The term "liquidity" is used differently in at least four distinct (albeit loosely related) contexts: (1) the overall monetary environment; (2) the condition of balance sheets of corporations and other sectors; (3) funding liquidity (i.e., the ability of traders to finance their levered positions); and (4) financial market liquidity or ease of trading. I focus on the last context and note that it can be assessed at asset, sector, and market levels.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/25711e5ab8bada1b2055d5b91bc37249007fb1d9b6a72077601df5bbfaa3ed4c.jpg)

Market liquidity refers to the ability to trade an asset at low cost and with minimal price impact. Liquidity gives asset holders the flexibility to sell their holdings when needed, akin to a put option. Liquidity is more valuable to an active short-horizon trader than to a passive investor. Even long-horizon investors value liquidity because it gives them the option to rebalance their portfolios, or to modify their portfolios as market prices and prospects change. Large illiquid holdings in bad times, such as 2008, may force investors to sell their more liquid holdings at poor prices and to forego attractive buying opportunities.

Elusiveness is reinforced as a result of the many facets to financial market liquidity:

- tightness (how wide bid-ask spreads are);
- depth (the degree to which one can execute immediately with little market impact);
- resilience (how fast prices recover to "equilibrium" levels after a trading shock)

Alternative empirical liquidity measures reflect these different facets. Measures related to trading costs include bid-ask spreads, commissions, and price impact proxies. (Such proxies include absolute return to volume ratio, return reversal induced by order flow, and "effective cost" or gap between the execution price and the midpoint between bid and ask quotes.) For large institutional investors, the price impact of their trading is often the largest cost. Measures related to trading activity include dollar volume, turnover ratio, proportion of zero daily returns, and short-term return autocorrelation.

Market liquidity can vary by asset type, size of the position, need for immediacy in the transaction, and market environment. Funding liquidity is more trader specific.

Across asset classes, privately held assets are deemed much less liquid than publicly traded securities. Actively traded financial futures, major government bonds, and currencies compete for the honor of being the most liquid assets in the world—even more than blue-chip stocks. Within major asset classes, small-cap stocks are less liquid than large-caps, emerging markets are less liquid than developed markets, and corporate or securitized bonds are less liquid than government bonds. Most alternative assets (private equity, direct real estate, other real assets, and many hedge funds) are much less liquid than traditional asset classes.

Fundamentally, market liquidity reflects the ease of finding counterparties to trade with. Higher search costs for counterparties—whether those naturally needing to take the opposite position, or financial intermediaries or arbitrage capital looking for attractive opportunities—raise the cost of trading. Liquidity differences can often be explained by varying degrees of information asymmetry—a key determinant of illiquidity that can in extreme situations cause market breakdowns. At such times, the credibility of information about market prices—which comes from markets being continuously traded with transparent pricing—can influence asset valuations as much as information about economic fundamentals. General confidence in the financial system and in the health of counterparties supports the liquidity of all assets but especially that of more complex and opaque assets; the loss of such confidence raises concerns about information asymmetry and can result in virtual closing of more fragile markets.

# What is the (il-)liquidity premium?

The illiquidity premium, often called the liquidity premium, is an asset's ex ante excess return over a (more liquid) benchmark asset. I use the terms interchangeably.

If liquidity is valued as a characteristic and assets vary cross-sectionally in how liquid they are, expected returns will vary across assets due to an illiquidity premium. Investors in less liquid assets need first a break-even cushion just to offset expected extra trading costs and to provide equal after-cost (net) expected returns, and then they may well require a premium beyond this. The desire of some investors to avoid marking assets to market may reduce the required premium but is unlikely to fully offset it.

If assets experience liquidity shocks in common, investors may further require a liquidity risk premium for an asset with a higher liquidity beta (sensitivity to a systematic liquidity factor). Academic studies show that the liquidity premium may not just be compensation for the characteristic of being illiquid but also for an asset's sensitivity to fluctuations in aggregate liquidity conditions (i.e., it is a premium for a risk factor). The two features (which come from viewing illiquidity as both a cost and a risk) appear closely related and are hard to disentangle.

Most empirical studies to date compare historical average ex post returns across assets to estimate liquidity premia (effectively assuming that premia are constant over time). If market-wide liquidity conditions or liquidity needs and/or liquidity aversion vary over time, then these liquidity premia also vary over time (globally or in specific sectors or asset classes). Given what appears to be time variation in liquidity conditions and liquidity premia, ex ante measures or regime-switching models would be preferable over historical average returns. Market compensation for illiquidity will be lower when liquidity suppliers are abundant and there is widespread appetite for harvesting presumed liquidity premia or for holding illiquid assets so as to avoid the mark-to-market volatility of liquid assets. Of course, liquid assets only appear to be more volatile, because of the price transparency that allows them to be marked to market. If one could know the prices at which illiquid assets could be sold, they would probably be just as volatile, or more so.

Illiquidity is an undesirable characteristic but exposure to time-varying liquidity conditions is particularly insidious. Assets that only provide "good-times liquidity" can mislead investors until a liquidity crisis materializes and both the asset's performance and liquidity plummet [1].

# Why is liquidity so hard to estimate?

Difficulty of isolating. Liquidity premia and other risk premia are not directly observable; empirically, it is hard to disentangle them. Ex ante valuation measures (such as the spreads of corporate bonds over Treasuries) and ex post excess returns (such as the realized size premium for small-cap stocks) include the impact of liquidity premia as well as other premia. To compound the problem, liquidity is multi-dimensional; any premium may be a reward for illiquidity or for liquidity risk that are not the same; many liquidity proxies may be used; and any premium reflects the amount and price of liquidity.

Time-varying nature. Aggregate liquidity itself is not constant over time, nor is the relative liquidity of different assets or asset classes, nor is the market price of liquidity. Yet, the literature has only recently turned to the topic of time-varying liquidity premia. However, more work on this topic will surely follow, thanks to the experience of 2007-2008.

# 18.2 FACTOR HISTORY: HOW DOES LIQUIDITY ITSELF VARY OVER TIME?

Empirically, many measures of liquidity (trading costs, turnover) vary over time and are related to

- a time trend: Often positive for (successful) asset classes reflecting innovations (e.g., securitization, electronic trading, decimalization) and growing investor acceptance (e.g., cross-over buying of emerging market assets, disappearance of the novelty/illiquidity premium after the early years of TIPS);
- market direction (better liquidity in bull markets and economic expansions);
- supplied liquidity (easy monetary policy and abundant leverage boost liquidity);
- volatility and correlation (stable markets and low correlations coincide with good liquidity);
- confidence in financial system and counterparties.

As usual, the longest data histories are available for U.S. equity markets. Annual turnover in major stock exchanges exceeded  $100\%$  in the late 1920s, fell to about  $10\%$  between 1940 and 1970, and then began to recover with the sharp fall in commissions and other trading costs after May Day (1975) and various technological innovations. Eventually, decimalization and electronic trading helped push turnover to new highs, over  $200\%$  in 2007, even as liquidity migrated outside exchanges as well.

Among the many trading cost studies, I highlight in Figure 18.1 Hasbrouck's (2009) econometric estimates of effective costs based on closing price data between 1926 and 2005. Small-cap stocks have consistently higher trading costs than large-caps; all costs peaked during the Great Depression; and costs have more than halved since the mid-1970s. Other studies analyze within-day data, commissions, etc., and indicate even sharper declines in trading costs—a fourfold to fivefold drop since the mid-1970s.

An even more popular academic measure of market liquidity is the price impact proxy, ILLIQ, developed by Amihud (2002): average absolute daily return over a month divided by dollar volume. My intuition is that a given trading volume causes a larger price move for a less liquid asset. ILLIQ is market wide and adds up each stock's ILLIQ measure. The series shown below is de-trended to offset its trend decline due to sharply rising turnover. Shocks to this level series have frequently been used in empirical studies as the main liquidity proxy for individual assets or for the market. The de-trended ILLIQ level shown in Figure 18.2 is clearly countercyclical. Naes-Skjeltorp-Odegaard (2011) document rising illiquidity in the U.S. equity market before virtually every postwar NBER recession and improvement in liquidity conditions before each recession ends. The slight lead could reflect the impact of the Fed's monetary policy on market liquidity.

Figure 18.1. Effective trading cost estimates (Joel Hasbrouck) for small-cap to large-cap U.S. stocks, 1925-2005.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/810b4e68a215c91a81931342865fe5457cb4fc3c8905774c7dba36d3c0d52316.jpg) number. When the Bank of England began to track this index in early 2007, it flashed a warning sign of exceptionally abundant liquidity. The picture soon changed, and in late 2008 we saw truly unprecedented levels of illiquidity. The Bank of England website only has data until 2009H1 but, based on the Citi Liquidity index—another index that uses a narrower information set, namely market volatilities and debt market spreads—it appears that liquidity conditions had normalized to near-average levels by the end of 2009.

Source: Joel Hasbrouck (2009), "Trading costs and returns for U.S. equities: Estimating effective costs from daily data," Journal of Finance 64(3), 1445-1477, published by John Wiley & Sons Ltd. Reproduced by permission of John Wiley & Sons Ltd.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/1aaac71bb437e282c7a66dfcc3726756e24eda326076bdc9b500f2da73c00c2a.jpg)

Figure 18.2. De-trended market-wide illiquidity, 1947-2008. Source: Randi Naes, Johannes Skjeltorp, and Bernt Arne Odegaard (2011), "Stock market liquidity and the business cycle," forthcoming in the Journal of Finance in February 2011, published by John Wiley & Sons Ltd. Reproduced by permission of John Wiley & Sons Ltd.

Figure 18.3 goes beyond the U.S. equity market. The Bank of England's Financial Stability Review tracks a composite measure of financial market liquidity in equity, debt, and currency markets, based on a variety of bid-ask spreads, return-to-volume ratios (price impact proxies like ILLIQ), and liquidity-related yield spreads. Details are in Appendix B. Each of the nine constituent series is normalized based on 1999-2004 data, and the composite series shows how many volatility units each series deviates from its mean, on average, smoothed over time. I flip the sign to show illiquidity as a positive

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/e0868de1ee6a4247746db322530f7401062b00c0bf329f96702a5b1aea5520ae.jpg)

Figure 18.3. Bank of England and Citi (II-)Liquidity indices, 1992-2009. Sources: Bank of England, Citigroup, Bloomberg, own calculations.

The evaporation of liquidity in many markets in 2007-2008 has justifiably attracted much attention, not only among investors and policymakers but also in academia. Brunnermeier-Pedersen (2009) link the ease with which an asset is traded to the ease with which financial intermediaries and levered traders can obtain funding (borrowing by using the asset as collateral). The two features can reinforce each other, causing liquidity spirals. The vicious circles involving simultaneously falling market liquidity and funding liquidity, lower market prices, de-leveraging, and higher volatility all contributed to the drying up of liquidity in 2007-2008. Such dynamics make liquidity inherently procyclical and raise the risk that liquidations from overcrowded levered positions take place at fire sale prices.

Other lessons from the period include the fragility of liquidity as well as the importance of trust and confidence (at the level of the whole market and specific sectors). In some cases liquidity conditions could be described with an extreme two-regime model—liquidity is either flush or nonexistent. In 2008 and in other liquidity crises, equity markets and other exchange-traded assets exhibited more robust liquidity than various debt instruments traded over the counter. Complex securities whose contents, risks, and prices were opaque saw exceptionally sharp price falls, consistent with the asymmetric information stories noted above.

# 18.3 HISTORICAL EVIDENCE ON AVERAGE LIQUIDITY-RELATED PREMIA

Average liquidity premia across global markets

Before turning again to U.S. equities where most studies have focused, I present some informal analysis of long-run average returns across asset classes. Simply looking at realized average returns for the past 20 years in various asset classes, it appears that liquidity premia have been an important determinant of required returns. Admittedly, the return differences across assets in Figure 18.4 may also reflect risk factors other than liquidity as well as survivorship and other reporting biases among actively managed funds. (Figure 2.2 shows a similar scatterplot but Sharpe ratio on the  $y$ -axis.)

The above scatter plot includes some illiquid asset classes for which only quarterly data exist. To study the impact of monthly variation in liquidity conditions on average asset returns, I need to focus on relatively liquid assets where monthly data are available. The illiquidity factor is calculated as monthly change in the BoE liquidity index, where that index has been sign-flipped (multiplied by  $-1$ ). (Due to data limitations, I extend the series to both 1990-1991 and 2009 using a narrower set of market illiquidity proxies.) The assets and strategies in Figure 18.5 are sorted by their correlation with the illiquidity

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/872e5c8d9049160f84f5c6196f5886523f2007574d786d2d33958c188d1d2988.jpg)

Figure 18.4. Scatterplotting average asset returns 1990-2009 on (subjective) illiquidity estimates. Sources: Bloomberg, MSCI Barra, Ken French's website, Citigroup, Barclays Capital, J.P. factor. Now illiquidity-sensitive assets are on the right, and good diversifiers—those that fare well when markets turn illiquid—are on the left.

# Illiquidity premium across stocks

A pioneering study by Amihud-Mendelson (1986) showed that liquidity is priced as a characteristic: less liquid stocks have higher expected gross returns (price discount). The authors argue that investors price assets based on their expected net (after-cost) returns. Heterogeneous trading horizons and limited capital lead to clientele effects in which short-horizon traders focus on the most liquid assets while long-horizon investors focus on less liquid assets. Long-horizon investors can earn rents because patient capital is in short supply. Empirical evidence on U.S. equities is consistent with the authors' predictions: stocks with wider bid-ask spreads earn higher average returns.

Turnover or volume can be used to proxy for liquidity. Chen-Ibbotson-Hu (2010) show that stocks with low past turnover earn consistently higher average returns than high-turnover stocks. (Yet, small-cap stocks with high turnover perform especially poorly, perhaps because they are the proverbial overpriced lottery tickets.) However, some liquidity crises coincide with high turnover (position unwinds despite high trading costs) whereas a persistent market decline tends to coincide with lower turnover. Thus, quoted or effective bid-ask spreads and estimated price impact measures have become more popular proxies of liquidity in academic studies.

Reasonable counterexamples argue against illiquid assets being systematically cheap. Short-selling constraints may be more binding for less liquid stocks, keeping them overpriced. Meanwhile, more liquid assets may be shorted more easily (e.g., in the context of futures), keeping them structurally cheap or at least keeping them from remaining overpriced.

Figure 18.6. Cumulative profits of a long-short decile portfolio of high-liquidity-beta-vs.-low liquidity-beta stocks based on Pastor-Stambaugh (1968-2008), as well as cumulative small-cap return advantage (F-F SMB, 1968-2009).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/31561f57932ea6afef741f5394a33399cac9f8157842a16f3f4162c49c965013.jpg)

Sources: Lubos Pastor's website and Ken French's website.

# Liquidity risk premium across stocks

Liquidity conditions vary over time and liquidity shocks exhibit commonality across assets, suggesting that liquidity may be a priced risk factor. That is, expected returns may be related to stocks' sensitivity to shifts in aggregate liquidity—and not just to the liquidity of the stock per se.

Pastor-Stambaugh (2003) focus on one measure of market-wide liquidity: temporary price fluctuations (return reversals) induced by order flow. They study stocks' sensitivities to this liquidity factor and document that stocks with high liquidity betas outperform stocks with low liquidity betas by  $7.5\%$  per annum (adjusted for a four-factor model of market, size, value, and momentum). Moreover, liquidity betas appear to partly explain the small-cap effect—and offer a superior reward. Figure 18.6 shows that the profits from buying high-liquidity-beta stocks against low-liquidity-beta stocks were especially consistent in the decade before 2008, but that year saw moderately large losses for the strategy.

# Disentangling illiquidity premia and liquidity risk premia

Acharya-Pedersen (2005) examine separate channels for liquidity-related premia. In a liquidity-adjusted CAPM an asset's expected return depends on the illiquidity characteristic (expected liquidity differences across stocks) and diverse beta exposures (the various ways that asset-specific returns and liquidity comove with market returns and liquidity).

The study uses surprises in the market-wide ILLIQ series to measure liquidity shocks among U.S. equities and estimates that expected liquidity differences contribute to a  $3.5\%$  per year expected return difference between liquid and illiquid stocks. In addition, the various liquidity betas contribute about  $1.1\%$  difference. But since illiquid stocks have high liquidity betas in all dimensions, it is hard to disentangle these effects.

# Further evidence on average liquidity premia in U.S. stock markets

Other studies use numerous liquidity proxies or their composites and typically find positive premia for illiquidity and liquidity risk. Illiquid stocks tend to be small-cap value stocks with low prices and high liquidity betas. Such stocks are especially prone to underreactions and valuation uncertainty and are harder to arbitrage (exhibiting apparent return predictability). Finally, sell-side (brokers') illiquidity appears more important for asset pricing than buy-side (money managers') illiquidity, consistent with the greater demand for immediacy and the relevance of funding constraints.

Yet another strand of literature focuses on short-term reversal strategies that are effectively a way of supplying liquidity to the marketplace. Trading gains from exploiting temporary price concessions, caused by supply-demand imbalances, can be viewed as a fair reward for such liquidity provision. More on this in Section 18.4.

# Evidence from other asset classes

# Government debt (without default risk)

Several studies identify positive ex ante liquidity spreads among securities with identical credit risk but different liquidity: the T-bill vs. maturing T-note spread in money markets; the on-the-run vs. off-the-run Treasury spread; and the government-guaranteed Refcorp bond spread over Treasuries.

# Corporate bonds

Recall the evidence that average corporate credit spreads exceed average default and downgrading losses. Such evidence is interpreted as a sign of long-run credit, liquidity, and/or tax premia. Several studies have tried to disentangle these effects by using some observable proxies for liquidity effects or by using structural credit models and/or rating agencies' default data histories.

One study measures illiquidity in three ways including quoted bid-ask spreads, the frequency of zero daily returns in a year, and a model-based indicator. Controlling for default risk and tax effects, this study shows that illiquidity characteristics have a significant positive effect on yield spreads.

Another study decomposes corporate yield spreads, explaining them with various credit-related factors, and interpreting the residual as compensation for illiquidity. The residual is an especially large part of the yield of highly rated bonds and decays between 2004 and 2006.

Yet another study estimates liquidity betas by regressing U.S. corporate bond portfolio returns on market factors and liquidity factors (where ILLIQ proxies for equity market liquidity and the quoted bid-ask spread on U.S. Treasury proxies for bond market liquidity). Then they study whether liquidity factors are cross-sectionally priced. They estimate that liquidity premia explain a significant part of observed average return differences across bond portfolios, more than is explained by market risk premia. The total liquidity premium averages about 45 bp for long-dated IG bonds and about 100 bp for high-yield bonds.

# Active value and momentum strategies

Asness-Moskowitz-Pedersen (2009) show that systematic value strategies in many asset classes tend to perform poorly amidst liquidity droughts while systematic momentum strategies perform well. This pattern became stronger since the 1998 LTCM liquidity crisis and presumably again in the 2007-2009 crisis as investors were harshly reminded of the importance of liquidity. Specifically, the authors analyze composite value and momentum strategies' sensitivity to individual illiquidity measures (TED spreads, market impact proxies, etc.) and to their composites; they find a clear positive (negative) relation between value (momentum) strategies and liquidity conditions. This finding can partly explain the observed value premium but it makes long-run profits of momentum strategies even more puzzling.

# Alternative assets and privately held assets

Khandani-Lo (2009) show that positive autocorrelation is a good proxy for illiquidity among hedge funds and that higher autocorrelations are associated with higher average returns—presumably reflecting higher required illiquidity premia. Another study shows that hedge funds with lockups earn higher long-run returns.

Part of the long-run return premium of private equity funds as well as real estate and other real assets is likely compensation for the illiquidity characteristic. Many investors in private equity funds only came to understand the cost of such illiquidity in 2008 as their rebalancing needs, spending needs, and private equity capital calls forced them to sell large portions of their public equity and bond holdings (see Siegel, 2008). However, disentangling the liquidity-related component from other premia is even more challenging than for traditional assets, given data limitations. Franzoni-Nowak-Phalippou (2010) find that PE fund annualized returns are  $7\%$  to  $12\%$  higher during periods of improving aggregate liquidity (based on Pastor-Stambaugh or Amihud measures) than during normal times. A liquidity risk premium—compensation for funds' sensitivity to aggregate liquidity fluctuations rather than for the illiquidity of investments—can explain  $3\%$  of an average PE fund's long-term average return.

For assessing the price of illiquidity outside publicly traded markets, studies of restricted stock may provide the most relevant evidence. Restricted stocks in private placements have traded at, on average, a  $20\%$  to  $30\%$  discount, but this figure has edged lower over time. More generally, privately held assets tend to trade at large valuation discounts compared with publicly traded assets. But again, empirical evidence is limited, and some conflicting evidence suggests that private entrepreneurs earn surprisingly low long-run returns for their poorly diversified holdings.

# 18.4 TIME-VARYING ILLIQUIDITY PREMIA

# Time-varying illiquidity premia across assets

To estimate liquidity-related premia, it is sufficient to focus on historical average returns if such premia are constant over time. Yet, the Jekyll-and-Hyde-like behavior of liquidity in various debt markets in 2007H1 vs. 2007H2 makes it clear that illiquidity premia vary over time. Academics have only recently turned to this topic.

Watanabe-Watanabe (2008) analyze the pricing of U.S. equities using the ILLIQ illiquidity proxy and a two-state regime-switching model. They find that liquidity betas vary across two distinct states: a short-lived "abnormal" high-beta state and a more persistent "normal" low-beta state. High-beta states are characterized by rising turnover, high volatility, and cross-sectionally dispersed liquidity betas; they are preceded by rising expected market illiquidity. These states are not only liquidity crises as they include both positive and negative liquidity developments. The estimated liquidity premium is very high (5\% per month) in the high-beta state (which occurs less than 10\% of the time, but disproportionately often in Januaries) and near zero in the low-beta state. Also recall that small-cap stocks and corporate bonds tend to outperform in January, consistent with wide year end liquidity premia. However, many other measures of liquidity conditions or liquidity-related premia do not exhibit a January seasonal.

Perhaps related to the business cycle pattern highlighted in Figure 18.2, liquidity also varies with monetary policy conditions. Jensen-Moorman (2010) show that easy Fed policy tends to improve equity market liquidity conditions—as measured by ILLIQ or effective cost estimates—and boosts the relative pricing of illiquid vs. liquid stocks. When stocks are sorted based on the ILLIQ measure, the least liquid quintile has an average monthly return  $0.9\%$  higher than the most liquid quintile. This average outperformance is  $2.3\%$  when Fed policy is expansive and  $0.3\%$  when it is restrictive. However, I suspect that this relation is weaker when credit conditions tighten despite Fed policy rather than because of it, as happened in 2008.

Acharya-Amihud-Bharath (2010) estimate liquidity betas for U.S. corporate bond portfolio returns regressed on bond market factors and liquidity factors (ILLIQ and the Treasury bid-ask spread). They find only mild pricing of liquidity factors on average—worth about 10 bp for IG bonds and 30 bp for high-yield bonds. However, when they specify a regime-switching model, they find that liquidity betas vary sharply between normal and stress regimes for high-yield bonds—but not for IG bonds. The stress regime probability was often high between 1973 and 1981 as well as in 1989–1990 and 2002–2003 (loosely coinciding with recessions and equity bear markets). Estimated risk premia for liquidity factors are much higher in stress regimes (about 100 bp for high-yield bonds) while premia for bond market factors are stable across regimes. These results are consistent with episodic liquidity premia.

The clearest evidence for time-varying illiquidity premia comes from "off-the-run vs. on-the-run" Treasury spreads, government-guaranteed bond spreads, and interest rate swap spreads over Treasuries ( swaps involve negligible default risk) (see Figure 8.7). Such spreads widen especially sharply during flight-to-liquidity episodes and during Treasury scarcity. Also recall, from Figure 17.9, how inflation-linked Treasuries (TIPS) remained structurally cheap for several years after their inception in 1997, until the initial illiquidity premium wore off.

This may all sound too academic, but if disentangling illiquidity premia is difficult, then quantifying time-varying premia is doubly challenging. The 2007-2009 liquidity crisis will provide a treasure trove of empirical data for analyzing time-varying illiquidity premia, but academics are only gradually getting their hands on such data.

Another strand of literature analyzes gains of short-term liquidity providers over time. Instead of holding illiquid or high-liquidity-beta assets, these liquidity providers supply liquidity to the marketplace by trading a short-term reversal strategy. My intuition is that recent laggard stocks may have underperformed because of selling pressure, while recent winners may have benefited from buying pressure. Thus a classic "stat arb" (statistical arbitrage) strategy of buying recent laggards and selling recent outperformers attenuates the temporary supply-demand imbalance and should be rewarded. Technological progress has changed market structures and liquidity sources. For example, the New York Stock Exchange has lost market share in U.S. equity turnover to various electronic platforms and dark pools. Market-makers used to be the only explicit liquidity providers but they have increasingly been superseded by hedge funds and other stat arb traders. High-frequency traders are important suppliers of short-term liquidity, but they are accused of being "fair-weather liquidity providers" who leave the market when problems arise (witness the Flash Crash in May 2010).

Figure 18.7 plots the histories of an illiquidity proxy and the gross annual profits of a liquidity provision strategy from a study by Rinne-Suominen (2010). (The illiquidity proxy is based on the idea that frequent return reversals are indicative of an asset's illiquidity as buying or selling demand causes a larger temporary price concession for less liquid assets. Estimates of return reversal tendencies for single stocks are then aggregated to get a proxy of market-wide illiquidity.) Both series are positively related to market turnover and negatively related to equity market returns, but they are especially related to stock market volatility. All series peak during the Great Depression but also exhibit similar contours in postwar data. Nagel (2009) documents a higher frequency linkage between reversal strategy profits and the VIX index, while others emphasize the role of financial intermediaries' funding constraints. High volatility makes liquidity providers more conservative while often raising demand for liquidity, and scarce liquidity in turn boosts the reward for liquidity provision. Figure 18.7 shows that liquidity provider profits (before costs!) have declined over time—so that only highly cost-effective stat arb players can still turn a net profit.

Consistent with decaying rewards for liquidity provision, Ben-Rephael-Kadan-Wohl (2009) document decay over time in the reward earned for holding illiquid stocks (as measured by Amihud's ILLIQ measure or volume). In contrast, the reward for bearing liquidity risk may not have declined, at least based on eyeballing the steady gains of the Pastor-Stambaugh strategy between 1997 and 2007 in Figure 18.6.

# Time-varying illiquidity premia in market returns

Some time series evidence that aggregate liquidity measures predict future market returns is consistent with time-varying liquidity premia. The relation is weak when illiquidity measures are the only predictors but more clearly positive when liquidity measures are combined with volatility or value indicators. High illiquidity coincides with bearish market environments (causality can go both ways) and mildly predicts high medium-term future returns. This effect is stronger for small-cap firms.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/2445ceb241cbd33c0b336adbdca53c6d940c0f847e1e84f07c1f73a88e61ca6f.jpg)

Figure 18.7. Rescaled market illiquidity proxy and estimated annual liquidity provider profits from Rinne-Suominen (2010) plotted with annual stock market volatility, 1926-2008. Source: Rinne-Suominen (2010), Ken French's website, own calculations.

Furthermore, forward-looking yield spreads and market valuation multiples may reflect illiquidity premia in addition to other risk premia. Such indicators appear to have some market-timing ability over longer horizons. Even if expected returns vary over time for reasons unrelated to liquidity, only liquid assets enable investors to take advantage of timing opportunities. If, say, the aggregate market or certain asset classes vary between overvaluation and undervalue, investors with market-timing skills can improve long-run returns only if they are not locked into illiquid investments.

# On liquidity crises

Liquidity crises are important both because they are, in part, the reason for the existence of liquidity risk premia and because they may be the most opportune times to harvest liquidity premia. Flight-to-liquidity episodes can push liquidity spreads to wide levels. Systemic liquidity crises coincide with the worst market distress, as in the crash of 1987, the 1998 Russia/LTCM crisis, and the 2007–2008 credit and liquidity crunch. The procyclicality of leverage, confidence, and liquidity are key influences. It was easy to predict some normalization in early 2007 from abnormally low credit and liquidity spreads—but the severity of the liquidity crunch took even most Cassandras by surprise. Once confidence turned, liquidity dried up in many markets and the crisis became self-reinforcing. Traders' deteriorating funding liquidity reinforced lower market liquidity and a vicious circle ensued. Forced selling—one hallmark of a financial crisis—became prevalent, to the extent it was even possible to sell. Previous short-term liquidity suppliers turned into liquidity demanders (they had to de-lever), while long-term liquidity suppliers held off from buying. In another feedback loop, position liquidations contributed to rising asset volatilities and correlations, and further raised required risk premia. In good times, liquidity differences were hardly priced; during this crunch only liquidity seemed to matter.

# Box 18.1. Opportunities and pitfalls in harvesting liquidity premia

Having a long time horizon gives certain investors a natural edge in harvesting liquidity premia; they get rewarded for sacrificing liquidity that they simply do not need. However, in good times investors may underestimate their liquidity needs just as they may overestimate their loss tolerance. Even long-horizon investors can benefit from the flexibility that liquid assets provide when expected returns vary over time.

Yale's David Swensen used to argue that most investors pay too much for liquidity. But the price of liquidity depends on the scarcity or abundance of liquidity provision—and this varies over time. Liquidity suppliers were scarce in the fall of 1998 and in 2008. The opposite was true in 2005–2007 when an ever growing number of endowments, pension funds, and sovereign wealth funds want(ed) to "be like Yale" and they had to compete with "long-horizon wannabes"—private equity funds and hedge funds—in reaping ever slimmer liquidity premia. My one criticism of Swensen's wonderful books is that they understate the time-varying nature of illiquidity premia (and generally rule out the benefits of market timing). Quoting p. 84 of Swensen (2009): "Even in times of normalcy, investors receive unreasonably large incremental returns in illiquid securities." And on pp. 82–83, he makes a strong case for illiquid assets: "Serious managers who attempt to identify inefficiencies frequently gravitate toward relatively illiquid markets, since rewarding investments tend to reside in the dark corners, not in the glare of floodlights … Illiquidity induces appropriate, long-term behavior. Rather than relying on liquid markets to trade out of mistakes, investors in illiquid securities enter into long-term arrangements, purchasing part ownership in a business with which they have to live. As a consequence, increased care, thoroughness, and discipline represent hallmarks of successful investors in less liquid markets." To me, these are good normative statements but hardly descriptive of institutional behavior during the boom-bust cycle of 2003–2009—nor do they constitute easy advice for average or even above-average investors to follow. Rather, they are the self-description of someone who is widely recognized as a truly superior investor, an attribute we should be extremely cautious about finding in ourselves.

After the traumatic events of 2008, asset valuations were low and various ex ante liquidity premia exceptionally wide. Indeed, it seemed likely that ex ante liquidity premia would remain wide for years to come, giving a sustained edge to natural long-horizon investors. However, global policymakers' aggressive stimulus and liquidity support programs have boosted risky asset valuations and sharply narrowed various yield spreads in 2009, and many "long-horizon wannabes" are already on the mend. The easy pickings are already gone.

The time-varying nature of liquidity premia suggests that it is best to be an opportunistic liquidity provider. One way is to enter new or alternative asset classes when they are still relatively illiquid and unpopular. Another way is to buy illiquid traditional assets when liquidity is scarce and markets distressed. My recommendation for true long-horizon investors is contrarian. Save ammunition in good times and gradually take advantage of episodes when liquidity and other premia widen out, while recognizing that nobody can be a perfect market-timer.

# 18.5 NOTE

[1] Illiquid assets' risk-adjusted returns are especially misleading because these assets not only warrant some compensation for their illiquidity (a "risk cost" that is neither deducted from the return in the numerator nor added to the risk measure in the denominator), but their measured risk is understated due to stale prices and smoothed returns.

Source notes. Given the growing interest on liquidity, an excellent survey by Amihud-Mendelson-Pedersen (2005) is gradually becoming outdated. On the reward for illiquidity and liquidity risk among U.S. stocks, see Amihud-Mendelson (1986), Amihud (2002), Pastor-Stambaugh (2003), Acharya-Pedersen (2005), Chen-Ibbotson-Hu (2010), Chollete-Naes-Skjeltorp (2008), Hasbrouck (2009), Goyenko-Holden-Trczinka (2009), and Ben-Rephael-Kadan-Wohl (2009). On the relation of liquidity premia with business and monetary cycles, see Jensen-Moorman (2010) and Naes-Skjeltorp-Odegaard (2011). On time-varying liquidity premia, see Watanabe-Watanabe (2008). On buy-side vs. sell-side liquidity, see Brennan-Chordia-Subrahmanyam (2009). On market timing using liquidity indicators, see Guo et al. (2010). On the profits of liquidity provision strategies, see Nagel (2009) and Rinne-Suominen (2010).

On private equity funds, see Siegel (2008) and Franzoni-Nowak-Phalippou (2010). On valuation of illiquid assets, see Ma-MacNamara (2009) and Swensen (2009).

# 19

# Tail risks (volatility, correlation, skewness)

- Tail risks refer to volatility as well as other higher moments, such as correlation and skewness.
- The empirical relation between volatility and future returns is ambiguous.
- Selling equity index volatility and weighting volatile asset classes appear to offer positive long-run rewards. However, stocks with the highest recent volatility tend to underperform other stocks. Also, among Treasuries and credit-risky bonds, the most volatile securities within the asset class have disappointingly low long-run returns. Over time, and contrary to intuition, higher market volatility does not predict higher near-term market returns.
- One explanation is that correlation risk is more consistently priced than volatility risk. This could explain the fact that for equity index options, implied volatility exceeds realized volatility most of the time, but the same is not true for single-stock options. In the same vein, and again contrary to intuition, high correlation across stocks is a bullish stock-market-timing indicator whereas high average volatility across stocks, or high market volatility, is not.
- There is some debate as to whether investors prefer positive or negative skewness. Market prices seem consistent with liking positive skewness or lottery seeking. Many indicators related to skewness and volatility suggest that the most lottery-ticket-like stocks are overpriced—their average near-term returns lag those of other stocks in a distinct hockey stick shape. Also, in option markets, the relative richness of OTM single-stock calls and OTM index puts seems consistent with skewness seeking.
- My two preferred explanations for empirically observed volatility-pricing and skewness-pricing patterns are that (1) investors prefer asymmetric (lottery-ticket-like) return payoffs and (2) leveraged constraints make the highest bang-for-the-buck securities within each asset class systematically overpriced.
- While selling stock index volatility and levering up low-beta or low-volatility assets are very different investment approaches, both of them offer high long-run returns and Sharpe ratios at the expense of losing money in bad times. The same features are observed for carry trading and harvesting illiquidity premia.
- The rewards for tail risks appear time varying—extra high in the aftermath of an adverse event.

# 19.1 INTRODUCTION

The last underlying factor I focus on actually involves several interrelated sources of risk and return, which I call together "tail risks" (see cube). I already discussed the volatility selling strategy in Chapter 15, focusing on equity index options. Here I extend the analysis beyond option trading and analyze aspects of tail risks, or higher moment risks, other than volatility. Chapter 15 concluded that pure volatility risk may not be well rewarded because, while index option selling has been profitable, single-stock option selling has not. A correlation risk premium seems better than a volatility risk premium at explaining index option richness.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/f592f8befe960733cc40d32764d15fff30afb8f1847cbb9e0890b90807f9e6cc.jpg)

Given the tradeoff between reward and risk, what would be more natural than to find a positive relation between expected volatility and expected returns?

- While a positive risk-return relation tends to work in long-run average returns across asset classes (recall Figure 2.1), cross-sectional relations within asset classes, surprisingly, have the opposite pattern. High-volatility stocks underperform low-volatility stocks, long-duration bonds underperform shorter ones, and credit risk taking at long maturities likewise may earn a negative reward. There are several possible explanations. In general, not all volatility is equal—some volatility may even be liked. Lottery-ticket-like assets are often overpriced, and leverage constraints may push return-seeking investors toward the riskiest assets in each asset class.
- In addition, the time series relation between market volatility and subsequent market returns is ambiguous. If volatility varies over time and investors react gradually to such changes, the instantaneous volatility feedback effect (higher volatility reduces today's market prices so as to raise prospective returns) may be partly delayed, thus deferring the positive relation. Evidence of underreaction between option and stock markets supports this explanation.
- Academics would hasten to remind the reader that no standard theories imply that volatility is a sufficient risk measure.

In this chapter I first turn to cash equity markets for evidence—both time series and cross-sectional—and confirm that the conditional relation between volatility and expected returns is either ambiguous or negative. Correlation risk appears to be more consistently priced than volatility risk. Looking beyond cash equity markets, I also present evidence from option markets and other asset classes.

This chapter's topic could be more accurately (and geekily) called "higher moment risks" than "tail risks". Recall that the first four moments of a distribution are mean (what is the average return?), variance (how variable are returns around the average?), skewness (how asymmetric are returns?), and kurtosis (how fat are the tails?). (Correlation measures the part of an asset's volatility that is shared by a second asset and is thus part of the second moment.) Normal distributions are fully characterized by their first two moments. Tail risks refer to non-normal market moves such as abnormally large price changes or correlations spiking higher during crises.

I focus on evidence for skewness preferences. Investors appear both to like positive skewness in lottery-ticket-type assets and fear negative skewness in volatility selling and related strategies—though the latter point has been challenged. Asymmetry may be less important than the timing of rare large losses. One of this book's unifying themes is that if a strategy resembles selling lottery tickets that pay off to the buyer in bad times, its double-negative characteristics (asymmetric returns and poor timing of losses) justify especially high risk premia.

# Not all volatility is equal

Practitioners often forget (at their peril) a point that is fundamental to academics' understanding of markets: only systematic risks should be rewarded. Diversifiable standalone volatility should not carry any risk premium.

Of course, the CAPM does not imply a positive relation between volatility and average returns; it implies a positive cross-sectional relation between beta (the market-correlated part of volatility) and average returns. However, since the 1960s, high-beta stocks have not outperformed low-beta stocks. Various stories have been proposed to explain this lack of a beta-pricing relation—anti-value, lottery ticket, or "good beta" characteristics may have reduced the returns of high-beta stocks (see Box 8.1). Interestingly, downside betas and other measures of downside risk appear to be positively related to average returns. Beyond the CAPM, finance theory says that different systematic risk factors can carry different market prices of risk, depending on the covariance of losses with bad times.

Since volatility is an insufficient risk measure, reasonable investors may accept a lower portfolio Sharpe ratio if they can trade it off against better diversification characteristics, more attractive asymmetry, higher liquidity, and lesser tail risks. These themes are covered through this book (especially Section 28.23). Here I just make some observations:

- Higher moment tradeoffs include peso problems and the greater likelihood, with asymmetric payoff strategies, that the historical track record is biased—overstating returns and understating risks when the rare bad event has not yet materialized in the sample.
- The combination of (apparent) high returns and low volatility is often indicative of hidden blowup risks. Low volatility may be achieved by questionable or fraudulent return smoothing (Bernie Madoff's Ponzi scheme did not offer fantastically high returns but the returns were suspiciously consistent), by holding illiquid assets which are rarely if ever marked to market, or by selling various forms of tail risk insurance.
- Even at the asset class level, low volatility in good times may conceal growing imbalances, excessive leverage, stretched market valuations, and vulnerability to regime change. Critics noted that many measured risks—including realized volatilities and implied volatilities—were at their lowest in 2007 when the true risk of loss was at its highest.
- Some researchers distinguish measurable volatility from ambiguity, unquantifiable ("Knightian") uncertainty, structural uncertainty, and "unknown unknowns". The latter concepts can be difficult to tell apart in practice, but the concept is simple: if you know the range of outcomes that can happen, but don't know which one will happen, as in a baseball game (one of two teams will win), that is a "known unknown"; if you don't know the range of possible outcomes, as in the stock market (we simply have no idea how far the market can fall in one day), that is an "unknown unknown" and represents a qualitatively greater degree of uncertainty.

# 19.2 FACTOR HISTORY

Chapter 15 presented evidence on implied volatilities. Figure 19.1 gives a long perspective on realized market volatilities. More frequent data sampling makes volatility estimates sharper, so here I use daily return data to estimate rolling stock and bond market volatilities. I will not discuss more elaborate volatility estimation and/or forecasting approaches in this book. The clustering of periods of high and low stock market volatility is evident. Peak stock market volatilities clearly coincide with economic and financial bad times, however defined. The highest volatility levels were reached during the bear markets or crashes of 1929, 1931-1932, 1987, and 2008. Other periods of heightened volatility tended to coincide with recessions (e.g., 1938, 1970, 1974, 2001). Bond markets almost always exhibited less volatility than stocks. The main exception occurred during bonds' peak volatility near the 1980-1982 inflation peak.

As is well known, market volatility and market returns are negatively correlated. Figure 19.2 shows that volatility spikes tend to coincide with the worst market meltdowns.

Figure 19.3 is a stylized graph that makes three points about option skew (implied volatility levels across strike prices):

- Index options have much lower implied volatilities than typical single-stock options, thanks to diversification, but their skew is much more pronounced.
- At least this was the case after the 1987 crash. Before the crash, the implied volatilities of index options exhibited a symmetric smile rather than a one-side smirk as both OTM calls and puts had higher volatilities than ATM options. After the crash, realized fluctuations of the index have been reasonably symmetric but implied volatilities disagree: they implicitly forecast a much higher probability of a large downside move than of a large upside move.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/d2a9a8d601abc6b8592b3910b4bee8284e7fd339773077873051b453b3d97cbb.jpg)

Figure 19.1. Rolling historical (3-month) stock and bond volatility, 1928/1962-2009. Sources: Bloomberg, own calculations.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/5a5f1a3349cc0bdde887fafb80a78d1d226fa2cc27cb099a1e5540fb5a1817e2.jpg)

Figure 19.2. Implied volatility and recent (inverse) market returns, 1986-2009. Source: Bloomberg.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/a21e53dbd2824f027852ef3dd81150ac23188f376ae7ff221576739054f6731a.jpg)
