- All volatilities shot up in 2008 amidst the market meltdown.

The graph does not show the relations between implied and realized volatilities or between put and call volatilities, but any such comparison reveals that OTM index put options are particularly expensive.

# 19.3 HISTORICAL EVIDENCE ON AVERAGE ASSET RETURNS VS. VOLATILITY AND CORRELATION

I first review the empirical relations between average returns and volatility in cash equity markets, covering both cross-sectional and time series patterns. Then I turn to options and other assets and drill deeper into the relation between average returns and correlation or skewness.

Recall from Figure 2.1 that average returns and volatility are mildly positively related across asset classes. Figure 8.4 documents the positive relation between average returns and betas for stocks vs. bonds but not across stocks.

# Cross-sectional relation between recent volatility and future single-stock returns

Counterintuitively, stocks with high recent volatility have lower average returns than stocks with low volatility. Some earlier studies had hinted at this pattern, but academic interest really swelled after Ang et al. (2006) documented high-volatility stocks' underperformance (with broadly similar results whether total volatility or idiosyncratic volatility measured by Fama-French three-factor model residuals is used). When stocks are sorted into quintile portfolios based on these volatility measures, next month average gap between returns of the fifth quintile minus the first quintile is around  $-1\%$ . The average alpha gap is even wider:  $-1.2\%$  to  $1.4\%$  per month, whether CAPM or three-factor model alphas are calculated. A follow-up study by the same authors (2009) shows that the finding is robust to the inclusion of many control factors and that it works in all G7 markets.

Importantly, Ang et al. (2006, 2009) measured volatility using daily returns over the past month (but showed similar results with longer time windows). Some critics questioned the robustness of their findings. However, other studies also found a similar inverse relation when they used a much longer time window (3 years) with weekly or daily returns to calculate volatility. The most consistent opposite result is that several studies measuring past volatility using a 3-year or longer data or time window, and monthly (not daily or weekly) returns, tend to find a positive relation between past volatility and future returns. Cao-Xu (2010) split volatility into short-run and long-run components and find a consistently negative cross-sectional reward for short-run volatility and a consistently positive reward for long-run volatility.

This regularity, like so many others, is stronger for small-cap stocks. Thus, trading costs may eat up much of the apparent gains from exploiting the poor performance of high-volatility stocks, especially in specifications that imply high turnover. New commercial indices—such as minimum variance portfolios or equal risk portfolios—can try to mitigate trading costs while inversely weighting stocks with high recent volatility.

Notwithstanding questions about how exploitable this regularity is, we should ask why it exists even on paper. My preferred explanation is that the volatility effect is related to investors' skewness preferences. Volatile small-cap stocks are the proverbial lottery tickets that may be systematically overpriced. Several studies point in this direction and can largely "explain away" the volatility-return relation:

- Frieder-Jiang (2008) show that when stocks are separately sorted by their upside volatility and downside volatility, they find no relation between downside volatility and future returns. However, stocks with high recent upside volatility have significantly lower future returns.
- Bali-Cakici-Whitelaw (2010) show that when stocks are sorted based on their highest daily return over the past month, the highest ranking portfolios underperform (more below).
- Boyer-Mitton-Vorkink (2009) rank stocks based on their measure of expected skewness and find especially low returns for high-skew portfolios (more below).

All these studies reveal an interesting hockey stick shape between when the ranks of portfolios sorted by a volatility-related variable are graphed against subsequent returns (see Figure 19.4). When stocks are sorted on recent volatility, upside volatility, maximum daily return within the last month, or expected skewness, the top (most volatile) quintile has a much lower return while other quintiles have broadly similar returns. In contrast, many other asset-pricing regularities have a relatively linear return pattern when assets are sorted into quintiles. This evidence seems consistent with lottery ticket preferences (see Section 19.4)

Intriguingly, Doran-Jiang-Peterson (2009) show that while high-volatility stocks and high-skewness stocks underperform in the long run and during the rest of the year, they outperform other stocks in January when risk appetite is seasonally high and skewness seeking especially prevalent. However, others find that high-volatility stocks underperform more when general investor sentiment is bullish; presumably overconfidence makes overpricing worse.

Figure 19.5(a) documents the consistent outperformance of low-volatility stocks over high-volatility stocks in the Ang et al. (2006) study, based on a broad cross-section of U.S. stocks from 1963 to 2000. Figure 19.5(b) shows the returns of high-volatility and low-volatility quintiles separately and adds more recent evidence from Robeco's David Blitz and UBS's Giuliano De Rossi. Blitz's data are on the large-cap universe since 1986;

Figure 19.4. Hockey stick shape in many studies: Poor average returns for highest volume, highest skew stocks.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/f4324221b0f7d9d4bfdc53fbaaaddd2675be2a66b73beab53f3f40339271e205.jpg)

Notes/Sources: Average monthly returns of sorted stock portfolios, from Ang et al. (2006), Frieder-Jiang (2008), Bali-Cakici-Whitelaw (2010), and Boyer-Mitton-Vorkink (2009), using different sample periods between 1962 and 2005.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/1aafb4bf06bfdec0d86fea11560f1656ae4740a90e37d17847575406f3e43be2.jpg)

Figure 19.5. (a) Cumulative outperformance of low-volatility vs. high-volatility stocks, (b) cumulative returns of high-volatility and low-volatility quintiles, 1963-2000 and 2000-2009 (broad universe) and 1986-2009 (large caps).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/575b82898b599a7d42a21b2b1e042041c52cb2fb81da289a369981ce7cdba894.jpg)

Sources: Ang et al. (2006) and Blitz-van Vliet/Robeco (2010 correspondence), De Rossi/UBS (2010 correspondence).

the results are weaker but low-volatility stocks still outperform. De Rossi's data are for a broad universe (Russell 3000 stocks) and cover the period from 2000 through 2009; over this period, high-volatility stocks outperform (especially high-volatility small caps, which rallied during the 2003 and 2009 recoveries). The inferences appear sensitive to research design issues; high-volatility stocks' relative performance in the 2000s would be clearly worse if we exclude small caps, use value-weighted rather than equal-weighted quintile portfolios, or control for other factors including size. As noted, trading costs and other limits to arbitrage may make this regularity hard to exploit in practice.

Diverse findings from other studies. The inverse cross-equity relation between recent volatility and subsequent returns also existed in the 1926-1962 period preceding the Ang et al. (2006) sample period. The inverse relation is stronger in the segment of the market that is dominated by individual investors (arguably lottery-seekers); in the segment where institutions dominate, the relation is positive. The relation can be explained by high-volatility stocks' tendency to have low subsequent earnings; once this relation is accounted for, the volatility-return relation is insignificant. Likewise, the relation appears related to the short-term return reversal effect. Pure microstructure effects, such as the bid-ask bounce, can artificially create a positive contemporaneous relation between volatility and returns as well as a negative lead-lag relation. Finally, stocks whose options have high implied volatilities appear to have higher near-term returns.

In another line of study, Ang-Bali-Cakici (2010) document evidence that information moves slowly between option and stock markets, and in both directions. Monthly changes in implied volatilities predict relative future returns: stocks with rising call volatilities outperform stocks with falling call volatilities by about  $1\%$  over the next month, on average. Conversely, stocks with high returns over the past month tend to see rising implied call option volatilities over the next month. (In the same vein, rising put option volatilities are linked to subsequent and preceding underperformance of stocks.) Given monthly lead-lag relations in both directions, it seems that informed traders may use either stock or option markets for their initial trading and that prices in the other market underreact (information dissemination is surprisingly gradual).

# Volatility factor risk premium

While volatility as a characteristic appears to be negatively rewarded across equities, the reward for volatility sensitivity is empirically positive. That is, stock portfolios that have larger (negative) sensitivity to rising equity market volatility warrant higher long-run returns. This advantage is of smaller magnitude than the return difference between low-volatility and high-volatility stocks. Moise (2007) suggests that small-cap value stocks have especially large negative volatility betas and earn an especially high volatility premium (0.5\% per month). Large-cap and growth stocks perform relatively better when volatility spikes, a fact that partly explains their lower long-run returns.

Moise's analysis covers realized volatility data since 1927. Using my shorter 20-year history, this story is less clear. Figure 19.6 sorts assets by their correlation with the volatility factor (i.e., the monthly change in the VIX index—implied equity market volatility). The patterns resemble those of the illiquidity factor and mirror those of the growth factor. Risky assets are on the right and diversifiers—those that perform well when volatility rises—are on the left.

Almost all stocks—as well as most other risky assets and carry strategies with positive equity market betas—suffer when market volatility spikes. Based on the past decade's experience it would seem that the big difference is between stocks in general and Treasuries. Treasuries were often the only asset class to benefit in flight-to-quality episodes, and this safe haven feature partly justifies Treasuries' low required returns. Interestingly, Figure 19.6 suggests that this feature was not strong for Treasuries over the 20-year window (Treasuries became negatively correlated with VIX changes only around 1998), whereas both momentum and value strategies served such a safe haven role (although value worked as a safe haven mainly in the early 2000s, but not in the late 2000s). Statistical arbitrage strategies (pairs trading or exploiting short-term return reversals) may work even better in high-volatility regimes.

Figure 19.6. Average monthly returns when the volatility factor is above or below its median, 1990-2009.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/0e205eafe203085632be00e244888fcf1ef74a463a036e35bbff82954bbc6f9d.jpg)

Sources: Bloomberg, LPX, MSCI Barra, FTSE, Bank of America Merrill Lynch, Hedge Fund Research, Barclays Capital, S&P GSCI, Ken French's website, Brevan Howard, own calculations.

Market volatility exhibits a strong negative correlation with equity market direction, but it still turns out that the volatility factor can significantly explain asset returns in a two-factor or three-factor model including market and value factors.

# Market timing: Conditional time series relation between volatility and future stock market returns

Empirical evidence regarding the conditional relation between market volatility and expected return is notably weak. As Table 19.1 shows, the contemporaneous relation between realized volatility—both level and change—and realized return is clearly negative, and even stronger with implied volatility data (last row) [1]. (Realized one-month volatility is measured as the standard deviation of daily returns during the month.) However, the predictive relation is not strongly positive; some published studies even find a negative sign between expected volatility and next month returns.

Lack of a positive relation between recent and/or expected volatility and near-term returns could only be temporary. If the contemporaneous volatility feedback effect is partially delayed, due to underreaction (investors perceiving volatility change to be more temporary than it normally is) or gradual learning about time-varying risk (e.g., due to structural uncertainty), this delay can contaminate the positive conditional risk-reward relation. Then volatility feedback not only instantaneously reduces returns but also spills over to give a negative sign to early lead-lag relations. Time series correlations are broadly consistent with this story: in Table 19.1, volatility has a more positive relation with 2-month-ahead returns than with next month returns. Volatility feedback might also contribute to the cross-sectional findings discussed above; recall that the negative relation between volatility and subsequent returns across stocks is consistent over short horizons but tends to flip its sign over longer horizons.

Pollet-Wilson (2008) note that historical market variance can be proxied by average variance of individual stocks and average correlation across stocks. This average correlation turns out to have a significantly positive relation to next quarter equity market

Table 19.1. Correlations between monthly S&P 500 returns and contemporaneous and lagged realized one-month volatility levels (Vol) and changes (dVol), 1928-2009 (and VIX 1986-2009)

Sources: Bloomberg.

returns, while average variance has virtually no relation (and indeed a negative one once the correlation's predictive ability is controlled for). Apparently, the average variance component offsets and conceals the market-timing ability of correlation, leaving stock market variance unable to predict market returns.

Bollerslev-Zhou (2007) present the most positive evidence for a conditional risk-reward relation so far. Their ex ante variance risk premium proxy—the gap between implied and realized variances, using model-free implied variance and realized variance measures based on high-frequency intraday data—gives positive-signed and significant predictions of next quarter equity market returns. Implied and realized variance, each taken alone as predictors, have positive coefficients, but only the former is statistically significant. Looking at the gap clearly improves predictive performance, giving a  $15\%$ $R^2$ . Using Black-Scholes implied variance or realized variance based on daily returns results in clearly worse performance—but the coefficients are still positive. The authors provide theoretical arguments on how this variance risk premium can be linked to time-varying market risk aversion.

Also, looking beyond equities, it is common to find that near-term expected returns do not rise (at least instantly) when volatility rises. Even if expected returns were unchanged, such a rise would imply lower ex ante Sharpe ratios and thus discourage investment. In many cases (e.g., duration timing and many carry and trend-following strategies), higher volatility predicts mildly lower returns in the near future.

# Option strategies

As a reminder, the most interesting results in Chapter 15 about profitable option-trading strategies are that

- Equity index options tend to be expensive (implied volatilities higher than realized volatilities), especially OTM puts (with a more pronounced skew than for single-stock options):
- index volatility selling can be profitable, with high, but volatile returns and terrible timing of losses;
- index skew trading (or volatility selling with OTM puts) may be even more profitable;
- correlation/dispersion trading (selling volatility on an index while buying volatility on its constituent assets) can give more consistent profits as some volatility risk is hedged.
- For single-stock options, implied volatilities do not consistently exceed realized volatilities, but OTM calls have especially large negative average returns (perhaps because skewness-seeking investors prefer them as speculative vehicles), and volatility selling is more profitable when done with stocks that have higher volatility or a higher gap between implied and realized volatilities.

# Correlation premium in equities and a lesson in the CDO market

Arguably the best place to study a correlation risk premium is in the relative pricing of index and single-stock options. Index volatilities are lower than single-stock volatilities, thanks to diversification, but index options are more expensive investments. Several studies show that the average gap between implied and realized equity index volatilities is between  $2\%$  and  $4\%$ , while for single stocks the average gap is nearer zero, statistically insignificant, and sometimes even negative. Table 19.2 shows representative evidence from one recent study.

This regularity can be converted into a profitable trading strategy, namely correlation or dispersion trading, where volatility risk is hedged by selling index volatility and buying single-stock volatility. The ex ante attractiveness and ex post performance of the dispersion strategy can be proxied by the gap between implied and realized correlations (i.e., the correlation premium). The economic argument for this premium is that higher correlations hurt diversification opportunities and thus worsen the investment opportunity set—a systematic risk factor that should be priced. Heightened correlations tend to coincide with bear markets and elevated volatility. Thus, correlation sellers lost money in times like the fall of 2008. Compensation appears abundant since implied correlations average almost  $50\%$ , while realized correlations across stocks average about  $30\%$ . Figure 19.7 shows correlation estimates by Buraschi-Kosowski-Trojani (2010) using synthetic correlation swaps or market quotes. The correlation risk factor in this study is the return of a strategy whose payoff depends on the gap between implied and realized correlations. Before costs, the reward for selling correlation risk is  $14\%$  per month, with an annualized SR of 3.3, over the sample period 1996 to 2008. The opportunity is attractive but actual trading profits are not as consistent as the graph suggests; trading costs and other market frictions could eat up much of the gains. Moreover, the plotted correlations are smoothed averages; shorter dated realized correlations spiked above  $75\%$  in October 2008, well above implied correlations, thus resulting in sharp losses.

Table 19.2. Mean implied and realized volatilities of the S&P 100 index and the mean of its constituents, 1996-2007

<table><tr><td>S&amp;P 100 index implied volatility</td><td>S&amp;P 100 index realized volatility</td><td>S&amp;P 100 index IV-RV gap</td><td>Average single-stock implied volatility</td><td>Average single-stock realized volatility</td><td>Average single-stock IV-RV gap</td></tr><tr><td>19.2\%</td><td>16.7\%</td><td>2.5\%</td><td>32.7\%</td><td>31.8\%</td><td>0.9\%</td></tr></table>

Source: Buraschi-Trojani-Vedolin (2009).

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/db4a51d204487d61ee38cdf808f7b919aac8c8615e2d922c6be4fa1811dbe2a7.jpg)

Figure 19.7. Implied and realized correlations in the S&P 500, 1996-2008.

Source: Andrea Buraschi, Robert Kosowski, and Fabio Trojani (2010), "When there is no place to hide—Correlation risk and the cross-section of hedge fund returns," working paper. Reproduced by permission of Professor Andrea Buraschi.

Other studies show that correlation risk is priced in the cross-section of equity returns (stocks with higher sensitivity to rising correlation need to offer higher long-run returns) and in time series (the aggregate market has higher returns following higher average correlations).

There is a large literature that goes beyond equities and focuses on implied default correlations based on collateralized debt obligation (CDO) tranche prices in liquid credit default swap (CDS) indices. The manufacturing of CDOs involves two steps: first, many securities are pooled into a diversified portfolio (special purpose vehicle or SPV), then the resulting cash flows are redistributed to tranches of varying seniority within the CDO. Tranches are typically assigned credit ratings from AAA to BBB, except for the unrated, most junior (equity) tranche, which takes the first default losses; higher yield spreads compensate lower seniorities. Although the equity tranche is sometimes called "toxic waste" its losses are of a relatively benign nature—diversifiable and not concentrated in the most distressed economic scenarios. In contrast, the most senior tranches only lose money in particularly severe economic crises when default correlations are very high. Correlations across defaults are central to CDO pricing. Increase in the perceived default correlations of constituents of a CDS index lowers the price of senior tranches and raises, at least in relative terms, the price of the junior (equity) tranche. Thus, for a given default probability a rise in correlation redistributes value from senior to junior tranches:

- In the 2000s it became common market practice to quote a single estimate of implied correlation where the estimate was based on relative tranche prices and an assumed Gaussian copula model. This implied correlation was low during good times, except at the time of the Ford and General Motors debt downgrade in 2005, but became ever wider during 2007–2008. Estimating a single correlation number was convenient but dangerous, because it ignored the diversity of possible correlation shocks and other problems ("model risk").
- Standard models underestimated the risk of correlated defaults, thereby compounding problems caused by investors and rating agencies using overoptimistic inputs regarding the probability of default by single issuers (they were essentially extrapolating the continuation of good times).
- Some studies decompose CDS index spreads into parts driven by idiosyncratic, sectorwide, and systemic default correlation risks. These distinct drivers will not have a proportionate impact across different tranches. Indeed, simple hedge ratios led to disasters when positions across tranches were hedged using naive single-correlation measures. The first spike in implied correlations in the spring of 2005 was mainly idiosyncratic and sector wide, related to downgrades of automobile company debt into speculative grade, while 2007–2008 correlation rises were increasingly systemic. The systemic nature of the crisis partly explains the terrible performance of the top tranches of CDOs in 2007–2008, although de-levering and the liquidity drought also contributed to their meltdown.

# Box 19.1. An investor lesson (mispriced CDO tranches)

Better understanding of risk premium drivers could have saved investors a lot of money: the timing of losses matters. In general, defaults of senior claims tend to be associated with progressively worse economic outcomes. The typical economic state where an AA-rated bond defaults is much worse than one where a BBB-rated bond defaults. For top-rated bonds, most of the yield spread over Treasuries may be compensation for systemic risks rather than for expected default losses. This observation partly explains the wide yield spreads observed for top-rated bonds given their low default probability. CDOs exacerbate this phenomenon: due to tranching, the risk of default gets further concentrated into adverse economic states, substantially increasing the fair risk premium of senior tranches relative to rating-matched bonds or to junior tranches. Bearing systemic risk in the senior tranche warrants an extremely high market price of risk (because losses materialize disproportionately under terrible economic conditions), while idiosyncratic risk in the equity tranche deserves hardly any risk premium beyond expected default losses (because such losses are more diversifiable). CDO ratings completely ignored this feature because ratings only deal with standalone default prospects and not with the (economic) "states of the world" in which defaults occur.

With hindsight, default correlation risk was massively underpriced before mid-2007, especially in AAA tranches. Coval-Jurek-Stafford (2009) highlighted these issues in Harvard working papers before the Credit Crunch, but many investors learned the lesson too late. Of course, so many errors were made by rating agencies and by investors relying on ratings that it is hard to judge where this error ranks in importance. (This story is not really about correlation risk, but I tell it because it highlights the occasional usefulness of academic advice to investors and because it speaks so well to my theme that selling financial catastrophe insurance warrants a large risk premium.)

# Hedge funds

Several studies highlight hedge funds' exposures to equity market risk, to illiquidity, to alternative betas (such as the carry factor) and to higher moments (such as variance, skewness, kurtosis, and correlation). These systematic exposures can explain much of hedge funds' return variation and high average returns. Thus, as noted in Section 11.3, most of the apparent hedge fund alpha may be beta in disguise.

Bondarenko (2004) shows that many hedge fund styles are negatively exposed to variance risk. Agarwal-Bakshi-Huij (2007) sort hedge funds based on their exposure to higher moments (changing variance, skewness, and kurtosis) and estimate significant risk prices for these exposures. Given typical fund exposures, hedge funds can earn  $4\%$ ,  $1\%$ , and  $2\%$  per annum, respectively, from these premia. The data sample ends in 2003, however. Buraschi-Kosowski-Trojani (2010) show that high correlation risk explains a significant portion of hedge funds' returns, especially in certain style groups. Hedge funds' strategy of combining and levering up many long-short positions makes them more vulnerable to unexpected correlation spikes than most other investors. Including the correlation factor influences hedge fund performance evaluation, in that some funds and/or styles appear to earn an insurance premium for selling correlation risk. Implicit correlation sellers often include market-neutral and convergence-oriented long-short funds that rely on stable correlations. Such funds benefit in normal times but underperform when correlation spikes.

# 19.4 THEORY AND EVIDENCE ON THE SKEWNESS PREMIUM

# Skewness preferences: Which sign?

Skewness preferences appear to influence the relative pricing (expected returns) of various assets. At first blush, it may seem obvious that investors prefer positive skewness and dislike large negative jumps—they especially like lottery tickets and fear crashes. However, a plausible counterargument exists. Taleb (2004) argues for negative skewness preferences, saying that delegated asset managers may prefer frequent small gains and rare blowup losses; they prefer "blowups" over "bleeding". To underperform peers consistently in normal times is bad for business, while outperforming, even by small amounts, helps greatly to gather or maintain assets under management [2].

What do the data say? I show below that empirical evidence seems more consistent with positive skewness preferences. I discuss the possible reasons below and dwell on new academic research on this hot topic. The focus here is on positive skewness driven by the desire for exceptional upside rather than downside protection. Investors appear willing to overpay for assets that have an asymmetrically large upside, echoing the way actual lottery buyers overpay more when the jackpot is outsized (compared with other rewards) and racetrack punters overpay for long shots. Downside protection is perhaps better reflected in volatility, correlation, or jump premia [3].

By expanding many standard utility functions to higher moments (beyond mean and variance), one can see that investors whose utility conforms to those functions should like positive skewness (the third moment) and be averse to kurtosis (the fourth moment). Three-moment versions of the CAPM, in which investors prefer positive skewness, imply a premium for coskewness (also called systematic skewness, and defined as the sensitivity of stock returns to market variance) but not for standalone or idiosyncratic skewness (analogous to the statement that only non-diversifiable risk is priced). Harvey-Siddique (2000) show empirically that low-coskewness portfolios outperform high-coskewness portfolios by  $3.6\%$  per annum.

The best ideas for explaining a premium for standalone skewness appear related to lottery-seeking preferences:

1. Mitton-Vorkink (2007) develop a model in which a subset of investors, whom they call "lotto investors", display a direct preference for positive skewness. These lotto investors are willing to sacrifice Sharpe ratio (mean variance efficiency) to get higher skewness. They are deliberately underdiversified because diversification reduces the skewness they seek. The authors document that a meaningful segment of retail investors is underdiversified and exhibits "lotto" preferences (favoring highly skewed stocks and holding portfolios with positive skewness). Such investors do not tend to outperform but they are more likely to be among jackpot winners.

- Another study identifies the socioeconomic characteristics of state lottery players (poor, young, urban, male, less educated) and finds that the same groups are more likely to prefer lottery-type stocks—and underperform as a result. Individual investors are generally more inclined to have lottery-type preferences than institutional investors and their demand for lottery-type stocks increases in economic bad times. My one qualm is that it is hard to see how this poor clientele could include the marginal investor who sets the market price in any given situation.

2. In a paper titled "Stocks as lotteries," Barberis-Huang (2008) develop a model in which investors overvalue ("overweight") low-probability events (an idea originating from Kahneman and Tversky's prospect theory) and then overpay for assets with positively skewed, or lottery-type, return distributions. As a result of such overpaying, positively skewed securities may have negative expected returns.

- Neuroscience research confirms that large payoff size attracts more attention than the (naturally) low probability of the payoff. The "reflexive brain" is much more responsive to the amount of reward at stake than to the likelihood of receiving the reward.

3. Cornell (2009) argues that investors who are overconfident in their stock-picking skills want the most bang for the buck in their investments and often prefer high-volatility, high-skewness stocks, making them overpriced. (I will argue that leverage aversion has similar implications, causing high-volatility stocks to be overpriced and have below-par expected returns.)

# Empirical evidence on the skewness premium

The stories above are consistent with the hockey stick shape across sorted portfolio returns in Figure 19.4. I now give some further detail on the stock market, followed by evidence from the option market.

Bali-Cakici-Whitelaw (2010) analyze the cross-section of U.S. stock returns between 1962 and 2005. They back up their assumption that investors have lottery-like preferences with a variety of empirical evidence, including long-shot bias in betting, popularity of lotteries and other gambling despite negative expected returns, and, increasingly, financial market data (poor long-run performance of small growth stocks and IPOs). Their proxy for a lottery-like characteristic in a stock is exceptionally high recent daily return. When they sort the cross-section of equities each month based on previous month highest daily return, the decile with the highest previous month daily return (which they call MAX return) underperforms the decile with the lowest MAX return by about  $1\%$  per month (statistically significant and robust to a battery of tests). The authors stress the nonlinear (hockey stick) pattern across portfolio returns. Securities with the highest MAX returns tend to be illiquid small-cap stocks with low prices and high idiosyncratic volatility. The MAX effect subsumes idiosyncratic volatility and skewness effects (although MAX could be just a proxy for expected skewness that is hard to measure). Controlling for beta, size, value, momentum, reversal, and illiquidity factors even boosts the average slope coefficient and its statistical significance. The intuition behind this last, surprising finding is that being an illiquid small-cap stock should boost the lottery-like stock's required return, so adding these controls makes its low return even more exceptional.

Boyer-Mitton-Vorkink (2009) present empirical evidence on expected idiosyncratic skewness. Due to the rarity of large events, past historical skew is not a very good predictor of future skew. Thus, several firm-specific variables are used to predict idiosyncratic skewness. Lagged skewness has some persistence but past idiosyncratic volatility is a better predictor; small-cap and negative momentum also predict positive skewness; and industry dummies can be helpful. Skew is predicted over the next 60 months (but the results are similar if a 6-month horizon is used); this data requirement conservatively rules out young firms in hot industries, often deemed to be overpriced lottery tickets. As shown above, stock portfolios with high expected skewness tend to underperform peers (a  $0.67\%$  gap between monthly average raw returns of quintile 1 and quintile 5, and a  $1\%$  gap when three-factor alphas are used in place of raw returns). Expected skewness can explain most of the underperformance of high-volatility stocks, and intuitively it is clear that investors are more likely to seek positive skewness than volatility.

Other studies suggest that many lottery-like stocks are distressed and that their debt has high default risk. Again, skewness preference could explain the puzzling pattern that such apparently risky stocks tend to have low future returns.

Some empirical evidence from option markets is consistent with the hockey stick shape. Sophie Ni's (2006) analysis of single-stock call option portfolios shows that ITM and ATM calls have on average mildly positive returns  $(+2\%)$  per month for the three lowest quintiles of option "moneyness"), while portfolios of high-strike and high-skew OTM call options—the proverbial lottery tickets—have large negative returns  $(-28\%)$  per month for the top quintile). Doran-Jiang-Peterson (2009) show that the richness of OTM call options relative to ATM options is especially high in January when lottery seeking is seasonally prevalent.

Studies that use option skew data to predict cross-sectional return patterns in underlying equities come up with mixed evidence. Cremers-Weinbaum (2010) show that deviations from put-call parity are able to predict near-term stock returns in a way that is inconsistent with a negative skewness premium. Stocks for which associated ATM calls are relatively expensive outperform stocks for which associated ATM puts are relatively expensive by 50 bp per week. That is, stocks where markets apparently expect especially high upside volatility (reflected in relatively expensive call options) tend to subsequently outperform. Conversely, stocks where markets expect especially high downside volatility (reflected in relatively expensive put options) tend to subsequently lag. The authors link this evidence to gradual information dissemination. As a result of slow-moving information, option prices thus contain information that is not yet fully incorporated into stock prices, which makes near-term relative stock price moves predictable if the needed option-pricing data are available. Such predictability is empirically stronger for firms that face an especially asymmetric information environment. This apparent underreaction in stock prices to option market information could partly reflect insider trading; users of superior information may prefer to trade options than underlying assets so as to maximize their profits.

Doran-Krieger (2010) dissect the ability of several measures of option skew to predict future cross-sectional equity returns. They confirm the Cremers and Weinbaum result that future returns are higher for stocks whose ATM call options have higher volatilities than ATM put options. However, when comparing option volatilities across strike prices, negative skew predicts higher future equity returns. In particular, stocks with higher volatility for OTM put options than ATM put options have higher subsequent returns, consistent with a negative skewness premium. Some studies mix the two features (call vs. put and moneyness) and therefore get conflicting results.

# 19.5 VERDICT ON WHY HIGH-VOLATILITY ASSETS FARE SO POORLY

I have already noted that high-volatility stocks and options perform poorly in the long run. Figure 19.8 reveals similar patterns in fixed income markets and Falkenstein (2009) presents further examples of this phenomenon in several other contexts. In government bond markets, extending duration to the longest bonds gives a much less consistent

Figure 19.8. Inferior long-run reward for volatility for the most volatile segment within each asset class.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/0b00b137832671258562f3594eedd725ebdab47965ee63b621a473676c05f37d.jpg)

Sources: Ang et al. (2006), Bloomberg, Bank of America Merrill Lynch, Barclays Capital.

return advantage than duration extension at short maturities. In credits, the reward for bearing credit risk is much less consistent at long than short maturities. Also, in the rating quality dimension, long-run reward for taking credit risk is worst for lowest rated CCC bonds.

My main explanations for the low returns of the most volatile assets within each asset class are lottery preferences and leverage aversion or constraints. Lottery preferences are reviewed in the previous section. What is the leverage story?

Leverage. Figure 19.8 indicates that levering up low-risk opportunities would have achieved much higher returns than buying high-volatility securities. Leverage aversion may have made return-seeking investors overpay for the riskiest segment in each asset class.

Theory can help us here: according to the classic two-fund theorem and capital market line, utility-maximizing investors select the most attractive portfolio of risky assets (with the maximum Sharpe ratio) and then lever this portfolio up or down by borrowing or lending, if needed, to reach their volatility target (or the optimal portfolio that is tangent to their indifference curve). However, this theory requires unlimited ability to borrow at the riskless rate.

In reality, if exceptionally risk-tolerant investors want high long-term expected returns but cannot lever, they need to hold a greater weight in inherently volatile assets than the weight at which they are held in the market portfolio. Thus, leverage constraints or leverage aversion can make investors reach for volatility to get the most bang for the buck that they can for their unlevered outlays. Overconfident active investors may also prefer to buy the most volatile assets for similar reasons. Such tendencies can make the most volatile assets in each asset class structurally overpriced, and the same is true for assets or funds that embed high leverage. Moreover, any leverage constraints are likely to restrict short selling, which could otherwise mitigate overpricing effects.

The story is not new: Fischer Black (1972) pointed out that the empirical security market line is too flat—high-beta (low-beta) stocks have surprisingly low (high) average returns compared with their betas—and suggested borrowing restrictions as the explanation: aggressive investors use high-beta stocks as a substitute for a levered market portfolio.

While I emphasize the value or potential alpha in levering up low-volatility opportunities, it is important to remember that leverage does raise the risk of all investments, especially procyclical ones, and may increase any existing need to de-lever with the crowd. The 2007-2008 experience highlighted the time-varying cost and availability of leverage. Any impact of leverage constraints on market prices may become more pronounced in the coming years, because various regulatory reforms seem likely to limit leverage.

Baker-Bradley-Wurgler (2010) combine the stories of lottery preferences and leverage constraints in typical delegated asset management contracts (where the manager tries to beat the benchmark by stock selection and cannot lever up low-volatility opportunities). Besides updating evidence for the long-run underperformance of high-volatility stocks, they note that these stocks have higher transaction costs and greater risk than low-volatility stocks (higher volatility, higher betas, and lagging performance especially in bad times such as 1973-1974, 1987, 2000-2002, and 2008). A high price to pay for the upside, indeed.

# BAB Sharpe ratios by asset class

This figure shows annualized Sharpe ratios of BAB factors across asset classes. To construct the BAB factor, all instruments are assigned to one of two portfolios: low beta and high beta. Instruments are weighted by ranked betas and portfolios are rebalanced every calendar month. Both portfolios are rescaled to have a beta of one at portfolio formation. The BAB factor is a zero-cost portfolio that is long the low-beta portfolio and short the high-beta portfolio (Sharpe ratios are annualized).

Figure 19.9. Betting against beta (BAB) pays off in virtually all asset classes.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-11-22/aa485235-5155-4ade-b88b-be1df0035d75/6e5fa65f2bbdcd5b8cb3e04eb766d2f3a13e4d1755377b8925d7d12c3d25c26c.jpg)

Source: Andrea Frazzini and Lasse H. Pedersen (2010), "Betting against beta," working paper. Reproduced by permission of Professor Lasse Heje Pedersen.

Stop the press! Just as I am about to submit my final manuscript, I find a new study by Andrea Frazzini and Lasse Pedersen (2010): "Betting against beta". The study presents a model with two types of investors—those who cannot lever (and overpay for high-beta assets) and those who can lever but face margin constraints (and must de-lever when these constraints become binding). The model predicts high Sharpe ratios for portfolios that are long low-beta and short high-beta assets, scaled to similar beta levels. Moreover, long-run profits of such levered "betting-against-beta" portfolios are higher the more prevalent the leverage constraints. However, these profits are not riskless because the levered portfolio tends to underperform when margin constraints tighten—which usually coincides with "bad times". (Tighter funding conditions imply short-term losses for levered portfolios, but higher required returns also imply better ex ante opportunities.) The empirical results—highlighted in Figure 19.9—show that betting-against-beta portfolios have indeed been profitable in several asset classes. The evidence is similar to mine above but more comprehensive. The prevalence of this regularity appears as strong as those of value, carry, and momentum discussed in Chapters 12 to 14. Portfolio turnover is comparable with that of equity value strategies and much lower than, say, when trading is based on past month volatility signals. Moreover, the authors show that such betting-against-beta portfolios tend to lose money when the TED spread widens, presumably because tightening funding conditions lead to de-levering these levered portfolios.

It is interesting that both index volatility selling and the betting-against-beta strategy (levering up low-volatility assets) provide positive long-run returns, but at the cost of ill-timed losses. Thus, both strategies resemble buying carry and harvesting illiquidity premia in that at least part of the profitability reflects the tendency to lose money in bad times.

Status seeking. Eric Falkenstein's book, Finding Alpha (2009), highlights empirical evidence similar to what I have presented and also emphasizes the story of lottery preferences. He argues that average returns are unrelated to volatility except in two extreme cases: people overpay for certainty (least volatile assets) and for hopes and dreams (most volatile assets). Falkenstein adds another story with his claim that volatility does not affect required returns because investors maximize status rather than wealth (people are more envious than greedy). For status-seekers who care about their relative wealth and compare themselves with a peer benchmark, underperformance is equally painful whether caused by long or short positioning relative to a benchmark. Such symmetry makes relative risks unpriced in the same way diversifiable risk is unpriced in standard models. This unorthodox but intriguing idea is related to the "keeping up with the Joneses" utility function (see Note 9 in Chapter 5) and could explain the "Easterlin paradox" that average happiness as measured by surveys has not increased over decades-long periods when material wealth has grown many-fold.

# 19.6 TIME-VARYING PREMIA FOR TAIL RISK EXPOSURES

Since the literature on tail risk or higher moment premia is young and limited data histories are available, it is hard to say much about the time-varying nature of these premia. Tail risks are diverse, but heightened volatility and correlation disproportionately occur in bad times (recessions, financial crises), so these factors clearly warrant high-risk premia. In principle, these premia could be constant over time but empirical evidence suggests that they are not.

On one hand, investor tendency to overvalue low-probability events points to high premia on tail risks. On the other hand, overconfidence can lead to disaster myopia—the tendency to underestimate the probability of rare disasters—and underpricing of black-swan-like risks. How can these Kahneman and Taleb effects be reconciled? The balance may be decided by salience and recency. The result is outsized ex ante premia for asymmetric risk strategies just after losses have materialized, gradually receding premia (apparently as investor memories of the disaster decay), and exceptionally low premia after a long period in a benign disaster-free environment. Implied volatilities, correlations, and skews may have some ability to predict future realizations, but they certainly have a tendency to lag or "shadow" these realizations. Following a spike in realized volatilities or correlations, implied volatilities and correlations can remain elevated for months or even years. The implied skews for equity index options only became distinctly asymmetric—emphasizing greater downside risk—after the crash materialized in 1987 (and the same happened to FX carry strategies in 2007–2008).

These historical developments partly reflect market learning ("we did not deem it possible for such a risk to exist") but I may generalize and claim that tail risks are particularly well rewarded after realization of pertinent tail risk events. One reason is the scarcity of speculative capital following such adverse events, because capital tends to be de-allocated from loss-making, insurance-providing, or liquidity-providing strategies. I do not claim such timing is easy. Many speculators who began to buy implied volatility in 2005, already then at historically low levels, bled money for two years and may have gone out of business before volatility finally rose in 2007 and then soared in 2008. Conversely, some large, well-capitalized institutions that recognized the exceptional reward from volatility selling after the late-2008 losses decided to bypass this opportunity, as the ride had simply been too wild.

# 19.7 NOTES

[1] The main explanations for the negative contemporaneous relation between equity return and volatility are the leverage effect (lower price raises the debt/equity ratio and riskiness of the equity part of the firm) and the volatility feedback effect (higher volatility raises required returns; higher expected returns can be achieved by lower prices today).

[2] Note that we should not confuse skewness preferences with the obvious investor preference for higher mean returns. Skewness preferences refer to preferring a more positively asymmetric return distribution for a given mean. In jargon, this is a mean-preserving tradeoff between frequent small gains and rare blowup losses.

[3] The richness of OTM put options for equity indices, perhaps a sign of "crash-o-phobia", was highlighted in Chapter 15. Jump risk has also attracted much academic attention for explaining implied volatility skew, time-varying volatility, and fat-tailed return distributions. Some studies estimate jump risk premia, but I leave the topic aside.

Source notes. Tail risk is a diverse topic that overlaps volatility selling, so see the "Source notes" of Chapter 15 as well.

On the relative returns of more volatile and less volatile stocks, see Ang et al. (2006, 2009), Blitz-van Vliet (2007), De Rossi-Jessop (2008), Doran-Diavatopoulos-Peterson (2008), Frieder-Jiang (2008), Fu (2008), Falkenstein (2009), Cao-Xu (2010), Clarke-de Silva-Thorley (2010), and Huang et al. (2010); as well as closely related studies by Ang-Bali-Cakici (2010) and Moise (2007). On the time series relation between volatility and future market returns, see French-Schwert-Stambaugh (1987), Glosten-Jagannathan-Runkle (1993), Whitelaw (1994), Ghysels-Santa-Clara-Valkanov (2005), and Bollerslev-Zhou (2006).

On skew-related patterns, see Harvey-Siddique (2000), Ni (2006), Pan-Poteshman (2006), Cremers-Weinbaum (2010), Xing-Zhang-Zhao (2010), Boyer-Mitton-Vorkink (2009), Bali-Cakici-Whitelaw (2010), and Doran-Krieger (2010). Stories related to skewness preferences include Mitton-Vorkink (2007), Barberis-Huang (2008), Doran-Jiang-Peterson (2009), and Kumar (2009).

On leverage constraints and the relative performance of low-beta and high-beta assets, see Black (1972), Baker-Bradley-Wurgler (2010), and Frazzini-Pedersen (2010).

On other influences on skewness pricing, see Taleb (2004) on delegated asset managers; Doran-Jiang-Peterson (2009) on gambling seasonals; Cornell (2009) on overconfident stock-pickers; and Falkenstein (2009) on status seeking.

On correlation-related patterns, see Deng (2007), Driessen–Maenhout–Vilkov (2009), Krishnan–Petkova–Ritchken (2008), Pollet–Wilson (2008) for equity markets and Bhansali–Gingrich–Longstaff (2008), Longstaff–Rajan (2008), Coval–Jurek–Stafford (2009) for debt markets. On hedge fund sensitivities to higher moments, see Bondarenko (2004), Agarwal–Bakshi–Huij (2007), Lo (2008), and Buraschi–Kosowski–Trojani (2010). On time-varying rewards due to learning, see Benzoni–Collin-Dufresne–Goldstein (2010).
